When it happens to someone that a tremor occurs from cold in the nerve, or from quartan fever, or another: then it is necessary for you to cauterize him with four cauteries, or five upon the dorsal vertebrae, and between every vertebra a cautery, and on his chest a cautery, and upon his stomach a cautery.
For the tremor subsides with an olive-oil cautery, and hastens the healing of the cold illness.
Concerning the method of cauterizing according to Albathir.
Chapter Fifty-Seven.
Sometimes foul albathir are expelled in the body, that is, pustules, which are made from corrupted thick materials.
Therefore, it is necessary first that when they appear, they be cauterized over the entire head of the pustules with a delicate cautery made of myrtle wood, whose end has been ignited with fire. Or with a long aristolochia root. Or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the very beginning of their expulsion in this way: and they do not grow larger: and the excess is broken down making them, and they are healed from them.
However it is necessary that this be done after evacuation with phlebotomy.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
Very often it happens that there is a flow of blood from an artery which has been cut at a wound, which occurs externally, or at the incision of an abscess, or the cautery of a limb, and similar things to these; and it becomes difficult to stop it.
Therefore when this happens to someone then hurry with your hand to the orifice of the artery and place your index finger upon it; and press it very much until the blood is confined beneath your finger and nothing comes out from it.
Then place a small olive-wood cautery into the fire, and many more of a large size; and blow upon them until they become very ignited.
Then take one from them, either a small or large one according to the wound and the place where the artery has been severed, and apply the cautery upon it; after which you quickly remove your finger and hold the cautery until the blood is cut off.
If it is expelled when you raise your finger from the orifice of the artery and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from those that are prepared in the fire.
Therefore do not cease to do this with one after the other until the blood is cut off.
And beware that you do not burn the nerve which is there, and other harm befall the sick person.
And know that when blood flows from an artery it is not possible to cut it off; especially when the artery is large, except with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it has not been extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the blood is cut off. Or it is tied with a strong ligature thread. Or medicines of a nature to cut off blood are placed upon it, and it is bound with a decent constricting bandage.
But whoever performs its cutting off with a ligature, and constriction with cloth, or application of burning things, and similar methods, does not heal it completely except rarely.
But if it should happen to someone and there is no doctor or medicine present, then let him begin by placing the index finger over the opening of the vein itself, as we have described, and constricting it tightly until the blood is blocked from above the wound and over the artery. And the finger should not be removed, but water of a very cold temperature should constantly be poured over it until the blood freezes and thickens, and is cut off.
Meanwhile he should consider what he needs from a cautery or medicine.
On Incision, Perforation, and Phlebotomy in Wounds and Similar Things.
We have already said in the First Chapter that every illness for which it is appropriate to use fire with a cautery and burning medicine, its occasions, causes, instruments, and forms of cauteries.
And I placed chapters there from the head to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter the same way so that it may be relieved for the one seeking his question.
And before I begin that, it is necessary that you learn, oh sons, that in this chapter there is from the terror that which is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And on account of that it is necessary that there be more caution in this one, because in the operation which is in this chapter, very often evacuation occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision over an abscess, or opening of an exit wound, or healing of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision over a stone, and those similar: from all of which trembling and fear are associated, and death falls upon many of them.
And I warn you, lest that which is hesitation fall upon you, for sometimes there fall upon you in this art ways of men with ways of illnesses. For from them is he who becomes angry with his illness, and death is easy for him on account of the vehemence which he finds from his illness and the length of its malice. And with illness there is fear which signifies death.
And from them is he who gives his lyre player and enriches you with it, hoping for healing, and his illness is harmful.
Therefore it is not necessary that you consent to him who comes to you from them, whose way is such. And let your caution be more vehement than your greed and your avarice. And do not presume upon anything from that, except after certain knowledge, which verifies at you that to which praiseworthy succession arrives.
And use in the healing of all your sick a prognosis and an indication concerning it to which conveniently belongs health.
For in that there is help for you in acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire in you his intellect, and not take away from you direction and inspiration: for that is in his hand.
And I indeed ordered this chapter by distinguishing it into chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on Cautery, from the neck down to the feet: so that you may be relieved of what you seek and need from it.
Concerning the treatment of water which gathers in the heads of children.
Chapter I.
Know that this infirmity often happens to boys at birth: or when the midwife tightens the head of the boy without gentleness.
And whenever it also happens from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I indeed have not seen this illness in anyone but boys; and to all whom I have seen from them, death has hastened: therefore it seemed to me to abandon the operation in it.
And I indeed saw from them a boy whose head was filled with water, and his head swelled every day until he could not sit upon himself because of the size of his head: and the humidity increased until he died.
And this humidity indeed gathers either between the skin and the bone; or it gathers under the bone above the suture.
And the operation in that case, if it is humidity between the skin and the bone, and it is a small abscess, then it is necessary for you to make a fissure in the middle of the head one fingerbreadth wide: and let the length of the fissure be as much as two knuckles until the humidity runs out.
And this is the form of a spatula.
If therefore the humidity is increased more and the abscess is larger, then make those two fissures according to this form.
And if the humidity is under the bone and the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head open from every side: and the water sinks when you press it with your hand to its inside, and it is not hidden from you; then it is necessary for you to make in the middle of the head three fissures according to this manner.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the fissures with cloths and lint. Then embrocate it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and care for the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be compressed evenly. And feed the weak with all dry food of little humidity, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another manner of incision is, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess appears, and the collection of water.
For sometimes it is more at the back of the head, or at the top of the head, or on the right, or on the left.
Therefore ascend with the incision where you see the abscess and the filling of water: and cleave it according as is possible for you.
And observe, lest you strike an artery, and there be a flow of blood, and the weak person dies from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When it happens to someone that catarrh occurs at the eyes, or at the chest, and it becomes old, and treatment with medicines does not benefit it: then a more final remedy is in this case, incision of these two arteries.
Therefore it is necessary when you wish to incise them, that the sick person shall shave his head. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries may appear. Then let the sick person compress his neck, with any of his cloths. Then consider where the vein pulses. And the places of both are places which are depressed behind the ears: and it rarely conceals itself, unless in some people.
Then mark with a cautery over each one; and incise each one with a scalpel on both sides, the incision reaching the mouth; and let it be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert a speculum beneath the artery and cut it above with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be like two joined fingers.
For when a vein is cut, blood flows out in a pulsing stream, which rises to the insides with frequent leaps.
But if the artery does not appear to the sense, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear a distance of three fingerbreadths. Then mark with sealing wax and cut down to the bone.
And that which ought to be emitted from the blood is six ounces, and perhaps you may emit from it more or less. All of this according to the quantity which appears to you from the weakness of the sick person and the fullness of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything remains at the bone from the incision, then cut it so that a hot abscess may not occur: then bind the wound with strips of linen cloth.
Then care for it with ointment until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When it happens to a man that he has an old headache or sharp catarrh because of acrimonious humors and heat in the temples, or an old soda, vehement, and similar things: and it is treated with the methods of medical treatment and it does not help, then we have found in these illnesses the extraction of arteries from the temples, or cauterization of them, as we have narrated.
And the method of operation in extracting them is that the sick person shaves the hairs which are in the temples and stretches towards the apparent artery in the temple. For it indeed shows itself to you from its pulse and rarely is it concealed except in a few people or vehement cold.
If it is concealed from you, then let the weak person bind their head with any cloth they have: then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or vaporize the place with warm water until it appears to you manifestly. Then take the spatula, which I had previously made. Then flay the skin with it easily until you reach the artery. Then throw a hook into it and draw it upwards until you draw it out of the skin and make it come out from all the tunics that are under it.
But if the artery is slender: then enfold it with the extremity of the hook. Then cut a part from it according to the amount by which its extremities are lengthened and contracted so that bleeding does not occur. For if it is not incised and if it is not cut off, the blood does not ascend thoroughly.
Then evacuate from the blood six ounces up to three.
But if the artery is large: it is necessary that you tie it in two places with a strong double silk thread: or with al-hodad cords so that putrefaction does not hasten to it before the consolidation of the wound and bleeding occurs.
Then cut off the excess which is between the two ligatures. Do that in that hour or another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone with a knife cautery until its extremities fall and it stands in place of the same work.
And it is a better operation unless the weak person has a fever or a hot complexion. Because a cautery is one of those that help to finish humidities. Therefore it is a better remedy.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries, that the place be filled with well-cooked lint: and let poultices be placed on top of it loosely made.
And after the loosening, it should be treated with dry medicines, which cause flesh to grow, and with dressings, until it is healed.
And if meanwhile while you are working there should occur a flow of blood from an artery, then hasten to cut it off with a cautery, or to fill the place with wool; and bind your hand until the blood is severed. And if none of these things are present to you, then place your finger upon the place until the blood coagulates. And embrocate the place with water of violent cold, until the sharpness subsides, and it is bound as is necessary.
And because that which is lighter and easier for the extraction of an artery is, to cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives; let the sharpness of the two knives be like a razor. However they are of much less sharpness than two knives. For if they are sharp like two knives, cold quickly follows to both; and they do not strike the flesh with speed. Therefore since there is a little thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut quickly; with this therefore the work is better than all other work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space which is between the two knives be the thickness of a finger, after you have marked two places with sealing wax; and lay both ignited strongly, until they reach the mouth, and they strike the vein in two places: so that what is between its ends is lengthened. For they are not fully consolidated with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
On the treatment of sharp, persistent courses of tears from the eyes.
When tears are persistent and the course of them is from the veins which are upon the exterior skull; and no medicine treatment confers anything upon them with medicines, and you see the face of the sick person already reddening, and he feels in his forehead a walking motion, as there is a walking motion of ants; and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that his hairs are already corroded by corrosion; and his eyelids are heated from the sharpness of the tears; then the ancients used this operation in their treatment, and they called it treatment with a Sword.
And it is to be commanded that the patient shave the hairs on his forehead. Then make three incisions in the forehead at equal distances according to the length of the forehead; and let the length of each incision be about two fingers; and let one incision be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second a little distance beyond the length according to the movement of the tear ducts, and the third on the other side; and beware of cutting the arteries which are on both sides; and extend your hand from the continuation of the two mandibles, and let the space of the whole incision be the size of three joined fingers; and have a piece of sponge or many dry cloths ready with which to dry the blood.
Then introduce a speculum with two pointed ends, of which the following form will be. From the incision which is from the temple side to the second incision; and with it flay all the skin that is between the two incisions, along with the flesh which is above the mouth. Then do the same from the middle incision to the second incision. Then withdraw this speculum and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a sharp curette, blunt on one side and not sharp on the other. And this is its form.
And place the sharp side of it toward the flesh joined with the skin, and the blunt side towards the mouth; and press it until it reaches the middle fissure; and cut off with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes, except that the incision be continued up to what appears from the skin.
Then do the same in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And when an equal quantity runs out of blood, squeeze out the places from the clots of congealed blood.
Then place a linen wick steeped in old wine upon all the fissures; and put poultices soaked in wine, oil or vinegar over them. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the ligature, and administer much infusion with warm water. Then treat it with royal ointment, and other things with which wounds are healed: until it is healed.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of sharp tears and cataracts to the eyes from within the head.
When acute, sharp, and persistent catarrs occur to someone: and you see his eyes weakened, small, and their aspect already debilitated: and the eyelids ulcerated, and hair falls out from them, and there is a sharp, hurting pain in the depth of the head, and consequent sneezing: then take from these accidents that those matters and catarrhs do not come except from many veins.
And the better cure in them is this. And it is, that you order the sick person to shave his forehead. Then make one fissure in the middle of the forehead, or another a little further by width, and begin with the fissure from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision be up to the mouth: and extend your hand from the muscles of the temples, which are moved at mastication: until when the mouth is uncovered: and you will dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the fissure with old thread, or with linen strips. Then constrict from above with lint: and soak the lint with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you loosen that, and you see that the hot abscess already diminishes: then it is necessary for you to cauterize the mouth until flesh begins to be born in it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that causes flesh to be born: such as if you take two parts from wheat flour, and four parts from colophony: and prepare an adhesive plaster from them, and administer it until the birth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of that which falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a mineral stone, or something like a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a fluid substance, such as water, vinegar, and things similar to them; or some animal.
Therefore, when a stone or a type of stone falls into the ear from those which are not added to and do not grow in the ear, expose the ear to the sun: if then you see the stone, then distill something from violet oil or sesame oil into the ear. Then perform its extraction and make him sneeze with something that has been mashed up, and plug his nostrils at the time of the sneeze, after you place into the circumference of the ear wads from cloths or wool, and extend the ear upward.
Very often it comes out with this care.
But if it does not come out, then administer its extraction with forceps, that is, with subtle pincers: whose form is this.
If therefore it comes out with the forceps, it is good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a thin blind hook of few turns.
If therefore it does not come out through that, then make a cannula from bronze, and insert the end of the cannula into the ear canal far, and plug what is in the circumference of the cannula with softened wax with oil so that there is no way for air except through the cannula: then extract it with your strong suction.
Very often it comes out with what we have narrated. But if it does not come out this way: then take a small amount from hellebore, or from birdlime, that is, prepared gluten with which birds are caught; and place it on the end of a probe after you have wrapped a well-placed wad upon it: then insert it into the ear canal with ease after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not go out with all that we have narrated, then hasten to incise, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the manner of incision is, that the phlebotomist should first make a small incision on the cephalic vein; and draw from him blood according to his strength. Then make the sick person sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near his fat in a depressed place; and let the incision be crescent-shaped, until you reach the stone; then remove it with what is possible for you from the instruments.
Then sew up the incision quickly with care, until it heals.
But if the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains that grow and swell; then perform its extraction.
But if it does not yield to your efforts for removal, then take a fine, slender spatula according to this form.
And apply it to cut off that type of grain which falls into the ear, and do not do this when you think that the grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear until you have put many splinters into it; then extract them with hooks or a fine probe or suction as we have said, for its extraction is alleviated.
But as for the water which enters into the ear, it is necessary that the sick person should use sneezing when he first sits down; and he has already filled his ear with some old cooked oil; and he lies on that ear in which there is water. If therefore it comes out through it, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones according to the length of a finger, thin and smooth, and heat them slightly with fire, and let the sick person insert one of them into the opening of his ear; and leap on one of his feet from that side, and strike it with another stone upon the one which is in the ear. Therefore, he should not cease to do that with stone and stone until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out so that you may take one from a crucible, or from a rush, and insert one of its ends into the ear; and you ignite the other end with fire until most of it is burned up. Then repeat with another rush, and do that many times until all the water comes out; and extract it with a tube according to what was said in the stone.
However, in the extraction of an animal entering into it, if it is of small body, such as a flea, and the like, take care of it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which appears to the senses; then administer its extraction with gestures and hooks; and its matter is easier than all those that are held in the ear.
However, in the extraction of a generated worm in the ear, when you care for it with what we said in the division and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not work; then it is necessary that you look at the ear in the sun. If therefore something from worms appears there, then extract it with gestures or with subtle hooks.
But if nothing appears there, then take a tube, which has this shape, narrow below, wide above; and insert the subtle end into the ear according to the amount that the weak one can tolerate it; then suck with strong suction. Do that many times until all the worms come out.
But if they do not obey you for the exit, then cauterize the circumference of the tube with wax, as I said in the stone.
But if they do not come out with what we said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already experienced in the infection of worms; and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your effusion with which you pour oil and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it yourself from silver, or bronze, narrow below, in which there be a small hole, wide above.
Let it therefore be so that driving what is in the concavity of the cannula, be made decently from bronze. And if you wish, take a ray, and involve cotton in its extremity with decent involution; then pour oil, or juice, or whatever you wish from these medicines into the cannula; and let the cannula itself be in the ear; then introduce the ray with cotton above, and press it with your hand with temperate compression until the oil is driven into the concavity of the hearing, and the sick person feels it within; and let that which is poured into the ear already be warmed a little with fire; and beware lest anything that is distilled into it be very cold: for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
On the cure of an accidental Auricular Exudation.
Sometimes certain infants issue from the wombs of their mothers not perforated.
And it also happens to some people that an exudation occurs in the auditory passage from a wound, or flesh born therein.
And this exudation sometimes occurs in the depth of the ear hole, which does not follow sight; and sometimes it happens to the outer part of the hole a little, and follows it by sight.
That which occurs in the depth of the ear, and which does not comprehend the view, is in many dispositions difficult healing.
And in that which follows sight, it is necessary for you to place the ear of the sick person in the sun, and look into it; then if you see an apparent exudation, open it with a subtle speculum, whose form is as follows: let its extremity be where there is a small breadth, of which a part be acute; and let the rest of the speculum be of two smooth sides, so that it does not injure the ear.
If therefore the growth is from the flesh born in the ear canal: then hold it with a fine hook, and cut into it with strong ease, until all the flesh is removed.
But if the growth is deep in the ear: then take a fine, gentle ray, and heat it slightly with fire: then put it into the ear canal.
If therefore you perceive the growth with that which the weak find with gravity of hearing, endeavor its opening with ease: and beware, lest you wound the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a linen compress according to the quantity of the amplitude of the hole which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do this for several days until you are sure of the healing of the wound.
Or take linen and infuse it in rose water, and sprinkle upon it powdered zedoary, and use it.
But if you see the ear already to have become abscessed: then it is necessary that you remove the linen and allow it with another linen which you wrap in cerate made with rose oil, until the hot abscess subsides: then care for it until it heals.
But if a flow of blood should occur: then immerse a sponge or cloths in cold water and place them upon the ear: and use the remaining care which separates the flow of blood as mentioned in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the Care of Althel's Eyelids.
Chapter Eight.
These althel which occur on the eyelids of the eye are sometimes moist, and sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold them with tweezers: or with a hook, and cut into them with a scalpel from their roots. Then place upon the spots powdered zedoary.
But if blood should flow from them, then cauterize them with a fine lentil-shaped cautery.
And to cauterize them, is better after incision. For very often they return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and they do not return.
On the Treatment of Hailstones on the Eyelid.
Chapter Nine.
Very often there occurs in the eyelids something which is likened to a Hailstone in its firmness and hardness, and on account of that it is called a Hailstone: and it is an aggregation of thick humidity in the upper eyelid, and the lower eyelid.
Therefore the operation on them is, that you consider then whether it is a Hailstone proceeding from it, which appears from the eyelid and moves to every part, and its matter is easy.
Therefore make above it a fissure through its breadth. Then scrape it from every part with ease until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook and cut it off.
But if the cutting of it is not prepared for you except after you penetrate the eyelid with an incision a little, then that harms the weak one somewhat.
But if the fissure is large: then join it together with suture: and take care of it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and makes it flesh.
And if the hailstone is sloping toward the inner parts of the ear toward its inner surface, then turn back the eyelid and suspend the hailstone with a hook; besides which you will need an incision and press it from every part.
But if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, that does not harm anyone somewhat.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the hailstone with salt water and take care of the place with that which makes flesh until the weak one heals.
On the Treatment of Eyelash Ailments.
Chapter Ten.
Axirnath is a fat which is generated in the tunic of the upper eyelid; and it most often happens to boys; and it weighs down their eyes, and they have catarrhs in the mornings, and they cannot look at the light of the sun, because tears rush to them, and on account of that you see them lying face down upon themselves always, and sleeping on them, and their eyelids are moist beneath their eyebrows, which already has been covered by swelling; and indeed the eminence of Axirnath is visible to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel Axirnath between them.
And the method of operating on it is for the patient to put his head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare on it tow, and make from the tow a circle according to the quantity which contains the Axirnath on all sides. Then place him upon it and press with your fingers from all sides so that the Axirnath gathers in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that moisture with an entering scalpel fissures across its width, and let there not be a fissure larger than that which is made in phlebotomy in depth.
Or it is necessary that you cut all the skin until you reach the Axirnath; for in many dispositions the Axirnath proceeds from the fissure according to the shape of a white tallow candle.
Therefore extract it with the cloth which you inserted between your fingers to the outside; and you turn your hand to the right and left until it is dispersed; and beware lest you add anything in the fissure so that you do not reach with the scalpel to the eye and injure it.
But if the Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the fissure, you add a little into the fissure with ease until the Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then immerse cloths in vinegar and water, and place them over the area, and bind it with bandages.
And there are those among men who grind salt, and put it into the hollow of the fissure so that it liquefies what remains from that moisture.
Then care for it until it heals.
But if a hot abscess should occur there, then treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
On the methods of treating a relaxed eye, that is, when the eyelid softens, causing it to bulge as it should not.
Chapter XI.
When hairs in addition to the natural course of hairs are born on the eyelids, and grow crooked: then they harm the eye, and cause illnesses to occur, such as continuous tears, and softening of the eyelids, and leukoma, until it becomes a cause of the destruction of the eye.
And indeed, relaxed eye is treated according to four methods. Or by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, as previously stated in the chapter on cautery. Or relaxed eye is treated by incision and suture. Or by means of canes, as I will explain.
You must place the head of the sick person on your lap. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
Therefore if it is turned, it is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, in which there is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle with thread penetrate from above, so that it is near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs are born: then draw the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with a rod.
Then find on the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with the spatula incident from the larger lacrimal to the smaller lacrimal.
Then extract the thread, and place under the eyelid a small ball of lint from cotton or cloth; then mark upon the eyelid with cerate in the form of a myrtle leaf.
However it is necessary that the figure be made according to the quantity and elevation of the eyelid; because this is now diversified in men.
For among them there are some who need a large quantity to be cut from the eyelid according to the quantity that is softened by the eyelid.
Then with the spatula find above the two lines that you have marked and begin from the larger lacrimal to the smaller lacrimal, and let there be one fissure near the natural hairs as is the thickness of a ray.
Then insert the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then scarify the entire skin itself. Then join the two lips with suture using a sharp needle and fine wool thread; and wash away the blood, and make what overflows from the threads adhere to the two eyebrows with certain occult things if you wish to do this; and if not, do not care.
Then leave the suture and threads for three or four days, then afterward take care of it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without suture and care for it with that which dries and constricts it. For the eyelid rises at the sealing of the wound and its joining; but it will be better in the future.
This method of healing was taught by the ancients according to Ataxmir.
However there is anxiety about the sick person, and it is from a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And there is another method in Ataxmir also; and it is that you mark upon the eyelid a figure which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate or joined hooks, according to this form, and then cut off what overflows with small forceps, according to this form, with equal incision.
But if it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it does not fit: then take a needle with thread in it, and insert it into the middle of the figure; and insert another thread near the larger lacrimal, and a second thread near the smaller lacrimal; and join the threads between your fingers equally.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation; and raise the skin above which is the marked cot, as we have described.
Then join the two lips of the wound with suture; and care for it until it heals.
But if an abscess should occur at your incision or fissure: dry it up with cerate and the like, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens that the eyelashes of the eyelid also turn around.
Therefore, you must use what we said in the incision; but the suture for both is more artful. Concerning the care of Ataxmir with dogs.
AND it is necessary to turn the eyelid and split the fissure that is within, according as I have described. Then make two canes, or two slender pieces of wood; whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and whose width is less than the width of a span; and you have already cut into the ends of each from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then join each with subtlety over what overflows from the eyelid of the eye, and tighten the two canes from both sides with a strong binding, and leave it for several days. For the skin that is tightened will die and blacken and putrefy until it falls off by itself.
If then it is delayed, cut it with forceps. Then care for it until it heals.
When therefore it becomes firm, elevate the eyelid: and the hairs do not sting.
And this is the form of two tubes, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
On raising stinging hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation on it is, that you take a fine needle, and insert into it a fine, soft thread from silk. Then you gather its extremities, and tie both with a fine knot very tightly: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place another fine thread under it briefly in an Alinxotati, and tie its extremities if you wish.
Then let the patient put his head in your lap, and be near the sun so that the operation is declared: because it is a subtle, or minute operation.
Then insert the needle with the Alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate into the eyelid quickly, until you draw it out from above the natural hair.
Then draw the hair up to above the Alinxotati: and insert into the Alinxotati that hair: if it is one, or two, or three, no more.
Then draw your hand with it until the hair emerges with the Alinxotati into the eyelid with the natural hair.
But if the Alinxotati is drawn, and the hair does not emerge with it: draw the Alinxotati downwards, with the thread which you placed in it, until the Alinxotati emerges from the upper opening, and you see it with the eye's vision: then indeed draw the Alinxotati and the thread: and pull out both, and close the eye: and let it remain closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either join to it a long hair from natural hairs, and raise it with it; or leave it alone until it lengthens after a while, and then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
When this alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental therefore arises from the consolidation of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and similar things to these.
And the method of operation in this is that you open that consolidation and separate its lips, and put into it a ligature of linen thread, and bind it until it heals.
And it is not necessary that you use things in its treatment which dry and constrict: for if you do that, the alxatrati returns worse than it was; rather use in it things which soften, such as fenugreek; and embrocate with water in which marshmallow root and flaxseed have been boiled; and ointment of dyaquilon which has been liquefied with some oil; and wrap it with a linen cloth, and care for it with that.
And the treasure of your treatment is, that you try with all your ingenuity, so that it does not consolidate according to the form which it was at first.
And indeed this operation which we have said is not except a certain correction of the alxatrati, so that it does not return to the form of the place according to what it entirely was.
Concerning the care of alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkle or contraction upward to the right and left.
Chapter Fourteen.
This alxatrati which occurs below is the one that truly is called alxatrati. It is natural and it is accidental.
And the accidental kind is made from a wound, or fissure, or cautery, and things similar to those.
And the way of operation on it is that you take a needle with a double thread and stain it with flesh color, and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a broad scalpel. Therefore if the shape of the eyelid returns as it should be, it is good.
And if not, take a ray and place it in the fissure and turn it with the eyelid; and make two fissures on the inner side of the eyelid, let the ends of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision until they meet and form an angle: until when they come together the shape of them becomes this shape, and it is the letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount which is its sharp lower side, from that which follows the eyelid. Then join the separate parts; sew them with wool thread.
Then care for them with what we said, about softening medicines and cleansing ones, until they are healed.
And if a luxation should happen from the fissure and suture or cautery: then it is necessary that you make a flat fissure under the eyelashes of the eyelid, also according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with cleanser, according to what we said.
And the sum of the speech about the care of luxation, when it is above or below, is that the operation runs in it according to what is prepared from the form of the luxation.
For it itself is of many different forms; and a skillful craftsman prepares his ingenuity with whichever way is possible for him, until he may return the shape to its natural form or near it, and himself, according to what suits him from the operation and instruments in many positions.
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation occurs in many people, so that the upper eyelid consolidates with the white of the eye and prevents it from its natural operations.
Therefore, you must insert the extremity of a radius under the eyelid and raise it upward; or extend it with a hook; then incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which is not sharp like the incision of other spatulas, but rather slightly blunt: so that the weak person is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed and does not injure the eye with its sharpness; and let your incision be as if you were scraping away that adhesion easily, until the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, with blue water itself; or distill into it from a sieve made, from ziniar dissolved with water.
Then separate between the eyelid and the eye with flaxseed linen; and place wool infused with egg white on the eye; and after the third day use a sieve, and with compresses, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the nail and eminence of the lacrimal flesh.
Chapter Sixteen.
Know that the nail is according to two ways. Either it is nervous; and it is similar to a hard, thin reed. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white humidity; which when it touches iron, or you try to grasp it with a hook, it breaks off, and the hook does not stick in it.
And both kinds do not begin except from the larger lacrimal, until it gradually spreads, until it covers vision and prevents light and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operating in its incision is: that the weak person should place their head in your lap. Then open his eyes and raise his eyelid with your hand. Then take a nail with a small hook and extend it upward. Then take a needle and insert into it a hair from the hairs of a horse, ox, or cow; and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the nail; and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upward: and flay with the hair the side of the nail which follows the pupil, as if you were to grasp it with the hair up to its end.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the lacrimal with a fine speculum, or with small forceps; and let go the natural flesh of the lacrimal, so that a constant course of tears does not occur from its incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with the hook alone; or with thread, as we have said. Then flay it with a gentle speculum, which has this shape.
And be careful that the speculum does not touch the corneal tunic, and a rupture occurs in it, and the pupil is perforated.
Then after the incision, throw something crushed salt, or made from a sieve out of ziniar into the eye and leave it until the next day. Then take care of it with what is necessary until it is healed.
But if the nail is not nervous and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor does a hook attach to it, then this kind of nail is often healed with medicine, and is healed as I have related in the division.
But if you wish to cut it, then open the eye of the sick person and take a subtle, gentle, dull-pointed speculum, according to this form. And shave the nail from above with reason and subtlety. If you see that it becomes thin and breaks up, and a razor presses into it, then distill from your hour into the eye a sieve made of zinc, or a red sieve, or ground salt; and bathe the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation on it until all of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in your eye; for then let it alone and treat the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat the operation on it with a razor until it heals.
But if the prominence of the tear duct causes great harm to the eye, suspend it with a hook and cut a part from it; and do not close the incision so that the course of tears does not occur.
Then instill into the tear duct a red sieve or one made from zinc until it heals.
On the incision of Alguardenic and flesh born in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes red flesh is born in the eyes of some men, superimposed or composed, until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or tightens upon the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward; and it resembles a rose balustrade.
And there is also an operation on it, so that the sick person places his head on your lap. Then open his eyes and collect all that flesh with suitable hooks; and hold it with one forceps or a hand.
Then cut the flesh first until all of it is finished with a surgical knife. And be careful of the eye so that you do not injure it during the operation; and let your incision with which you cut it, be either with a scalpel, which we have described in the incision of a nail, or with small forceps, with which pus is collected, according to what its form will come after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is complete, and the whole of the algardenici is removed, then fill the eye with pounded salt: or distill into it red powder, and those similar to it from corrosive medicines: and put on the eye outside cotton with linen so that it may be guarded against a hot abscess.
But if anything remains of the algardenici, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess: then leave the eye alone, and treat it with that which subdues an abscess.
Then repeat the procedure upon it until it is healed.
And do similarly with added flesh which occurs in the eye from this kind.
However it is necessary that strangers should perform the treatment of every weak eye from natural or accidental causes with iron; and do not put your hand into any of these operations until you see that that member can tolerate it.
On the Collection of Sebum from the Eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Sebum are red veins which spread over the eye and prevent vision from its operation: and they weaken the eye with the length of days.
It is necessary therefore for you first to consider if the eye in which there is sebum is strong and there is no sickness in it except the sebum: then collect its sebum.
And it is necessary that you instruct the weak person to put their head in your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your prudence: and let the hook be subtle of double shape, according to this form, or let there be two hooks graded into one body, according to this form.
Then collect those veins with subtle forceps with subtlety, and wash away the blood hour by hour until you see those veins being removed from the eye and resolved; and be careful not to injure the eye with the ends of the speculum.
Let your operation be in the middle of the day before the sun, and be very steadfast in your operation so that you do not fall except upon those veins.
And when you have finished, then distill into the eye a green or red sieve so that its sharpness may corrode what remains from the sebum.
But if it is not possible for you to collect all of it in that hour, then plaster the eye with something which quiets the pain of the eye and let it be for several days until its pain subsides and guard it from a hot poultice; then repeat upon it in the same manner until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps.
Concerning the reduction of a deviated septum to the nose.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call a deviated septum a fistula, which you should treat with a cautery or with a sharply burning medicine, according to what was previously stated concerning it.
And if it does not heal, then there is no skill in it except to cut above the abscess at its origin and extract all the moisture that is in it and the pus until the opening is exposed.
Therefore, when the opening is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then excise it with an instrument whose form is this.
And it is called an Asperi's head.
Let it be made of tempered iron, and let its head be round like an azir, which should be punctured with the puncture of a file or with a delicate puncture of a sharp chisel and place it upon the corrupted place of the bone.
Then revolve it between your fingers and press your hand upon it a little while until you know that the corruption has already been scraped away; and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying astringent medicines.
Therefore, if the place consolidates and flesh grows in it and the flow of blood is cut off; and it remains for forty days and does not reoccur and does not become abscessed and no accident happens to it: then you know that it has already been healed.
And if not, then there is no skill in it except to reduce the fistula to the nostril orifice, according to this manner.
And it is, so that you expose the second bone with an iron, or with a sharp medicine; and when the bone has been exposed, then take an auger according to this manner, let its iron end be triangular: and let its wood be a pyramid tapered down to the extremity, as you see.
Then place that upon the bone itself; and let it be near the tear duct; and extend your hand a little from the eye. Then revolve your hand with the auger until it penetrates the bone, and it feels weak; through this it finds wind coming out of it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with styptic drying medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh is born, and the place hardens; then the matter which was flowing to the outside, returns to the nose; it is therefore lighter upon the weak spot.
On the Reduction of an Eye Prominence.
Chapter XX.
When an eye protrudes with its entirety, and there is no vision impairment, nor diminution; then it is necessary that you loosen the weak spot: then phlebotomize him: afterwards apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place upon the eye a liniment which is made from acacia, and aloe, and frankincense, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye from above upon a plaster with many powders.
And the manner of powders is: that you take soft cloths, many, and double every cloth from them over four; make many from them; then place them one upon another, according to the amount which the eye needs: then bind upon them with a hard cloth, a strong binding.
And repeat aspirations with a syringe without scarification.
Then remove the aspirated fluid, and let the eye be kept closed day and night.
But if the ligament again becomes softened, then it must be tightened much; then remove the ligature after a day and night, as we have said.
Therefore, if you see the eye has already returned to its former tunic, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster, and the poultices, and the ligature, and the aspirations until it is healed.
On the Incision of the Vitreous Tunic.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture occurs in the vitreous tunic, then it protrudes and proceeds outside the eyelids like a seed of the vitreous, and spoils the appearance of man because of it, and there is a desire to excise it: then the operation on it is as follows, according to what I relate: and it is, that you introduce a needle into the root of the vitreous from the lower to the upper part.
Then introduce another needle in which there is a double thread from the lacrimal part, and make it penetrate; and let the first needle go according to its own position.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the vitreous around the needle; and tighten it very much.
Then withdraw the needle, and place wool steeped in albumen over the eye; and let him be, until the thread falls with the vitreous.
Then treat the eye with what strengthens it, until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the vitreous is constricted from every side with a circle made of cloth, until the eye adds great prominence.
Then perforate it with a fine trocar that reaches the depth of the eye, and let the albuminous moisture flow, and let the eye descend, and enter immediately; then tighten it until it is healed.
On the treatment of al-qonmāti.
Chapter XXII.
This ailment which is called al-qonmāti is nothing but a humor that collects in the eye, resembling descending water, and is not itself.
And the method of operating on it is to have the patient sit upon a prepared seat, or straight.
Then take his head with your hands on both sides and move it until you see the humor come down towards your eyes. Then fix it so that it does not descend, and clear the vision; and let the patient see things as he used to see; and if it does not descend below, you know that it is water.
But if the descent of the humor is not prepared as we said, then make the patient sit between your hands. Then take a fine spatula and with it make an incision above the corneal membrane a little at the continuation of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the crown until the humor emerges.
When it therefore emerges, then distill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey; or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey; then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
On the treatment of water that descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already spoken of the kinds of water in the division, and what is suitable for piercing speech explaining and setting forth; then take that same thing according to health.
Then it is necessary for you to make the patient sit between your hands squarely, opposite the light near the sun; and bind his sound eye, and bind it tightly; then hold down the eyelid of his eye with your right hand if the eye in which there is water is the left eye; or with your left hand if the eye is the right.
Then take with your right hand an al-ma'ghazal if the eye is the left; or with your left hand if the eye is the right.
Then place the end of the almaga near the cornea for the width of a radius in the white of the eye apparent below the lesser lachrymal; then press the almaga with force and turn your hand with it until it enters into the albuginea of the eye; and you will feel that the almaga has now reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the measure of the journey of the almaga to its depth be the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness; and this is the cornea of the eye. For you will see it in the pupil itself by the vision of the eye, on account of the clarity of the tunic of the cornea.
Then place the almaga up to the place where there is water. Then press it down below with juice one after another.
And if therefore the water descends immediately, then the sick man sees that upon which he opens his sight immediately, and the almaga in his eye.
Then rest a little while. And if therefore the water descends, then place it second beyond that by which the almaga comes out.
When therefore it is fixed and does not ascend; then draw out the almaga with ease; and you turn your hand little by little.
Then dissolve something of clear salt of gems in water; and wash the eye within it. Then place outside the whole eye a plug, or wool steeped in rose oil, and albuginea; and bind it with the sound eye.
We however, and those who are of our time, place upon it pounded cuminum with albuginea.
But if the almaga does not obey you to enter into the eye on account of its hardness because it is from men whose eyes are very hard; then it is necessary for you to take a speculum, which is called an Alberid, whose form is this:
Therefore perforate it only with this conjunctiva and not your whole hand into the hole; and there is nothing else to do except make a way to the almond-shaped place underneath.
Then introduce the almond as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, prepare for the sick man a well-made bedchamber in which he may sleep upon his back in a darkened room; and prohibit him from all motions and coughing; and give him food that soothes his nature; and let him not move his head to the right or left at all.
And let the ligature be according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then loosen it in that darkened room and test his sight; and have him see things. Then return the ligature until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that he do this at the hour of healing or immediately after the incision; but rather, that others do it; because water ascends quickly through a forceful opening.
But if a hot swelling occurs; then it is fitting that you loosen the eye before the seventh day and correct it with that which stills the swelling until it is stilled; then release his sight, and place upon his face a covering by which his vision may be regulated under those days, with him being in that darkened room.
Then let him go out from the house gradually and frequent his own affairs.
And know that in surgery the student is not excused from sight often; then let him dare upon the operation.
And now indeed there has come to us a certain one of those who are from Al-Arayrakh, who said that a device was made in Al-Arayrakh which is perforated so that it sucks up water.
But truly I have not seen anyone in your land who has made this; nor have I read of it in any ancient book; and it is possible that it is new.
These are the forms of species from the ulcer, so that it may stand over him who does not know them.
Let them be made of bronze properly: and let their extremities be with this same subtlety triangular, and the extremity acute.
Chapter XXIV.
Concerning the treatment of flesh born on the nose.
Sometimes diverse added fleshes are born in the nose, from which there is something that resembles a scorpion of many feet; and from it is cancerous flesh, stony, of a dark color; and from it is that which is soft flesh, not of a dark color.
Therefore, in that which is soft flesh from these fleshes, not fraudulent nor cancerous, it is necessary for you to make the weak one sit between your hands opposite the sun, and open his nostrils, and throw a hook into those fleshes; then draw them outwards, and cut what you have grasped from them with a thin sharp scalpel on one side, until you know that all the flesh has now been removed.
And if anything remained from that which its excision is not possible: then shave it with one of the subtle instruments with ease, until nothing remains from it. But if blood dominates you, or a hot abscess occurs: then oppose it by what is necessary: or if it is from malignant abscesses, then hurry to cauterize it until the blood is cut off and all flesh is removed.
Then vinegar and water, or wine, should be thrown into the nose after the incision. But if the nose opens, and moisture proceeds from it up to the palate, then you know that it is now healthy.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that there is flesh born within it on the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary for you to insert a linen thread which has some thickness, and make many knots in it; and place the distance of a finger, or less, between each knot: and have the weak one guide one end of the thread into his nose with a rod, or with whatever is possible for him after he has made it like a plowshare; and pull on his own odor until it reaches the back, and comes out over his throat.
And very often they do something like this dissection of a boy in schools; and it is an easy thing for whoever wants to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end that goes over the nose, and the other that remains in the nose. Then apply a carneal lock with the knots that are in the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is already joined with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread and put in the nose after washing away the blood a linen cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment; do this for three days, or more, until the ointment eats away all that remains from the flesh.
Then finally put a lead tube into the nose for several days until it heals.
But if it needs treatment that will dry it up, use that.
To make a nasal inhaler, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it from silver or bronze: it should be like a small lamp, open like a shell; and its passageway similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed tube, like a reed.
And the shell of the instrument for making a nasal inhaler should be full and open; it should have a handle at its end with which you hold it when heating oil or whatever you wish from juices and flowing things in it.
Chapter 25.
Very often a swelling arises at the end of the nose and it magnifies and increases with days until its appearance becomes ugly.
And because of that it is necessary to cut into it at the beginning of its appearance and eradicate all of it. Then place on top of the spot either a cautery or a burning medicine that takes its place.
Therefore, if the incision of this is delayed until it grows larger, then consider, if it is stony, hard, dark in color, and insensitive: then do not touch it with iron; for it is a cancerous ulcer.
For I have often seen those who suffered a felon and great misfortune came upon him who had it.
And if the felon is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that incision is possible throughout its entirety, then evacuate the weak spot, and cut it without fear, without hesitation: and care for the place with that which dries and constricts, until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On the suture of the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when a disruption of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, little benefit results from surgery except in certain individuals.
Therefore, when it happens to someone that they suffer one of these things, you must consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood; and then join the two lips of the wound together with suture: then care for it until it heals.
And if the lips of the disruption have already been separated and each part has become healthy; then it is necessary that you scrape away all the skin from its surface until it bleeds. Then join the two lips together with suture and tighten them, and sprinkle dragon's blood and ground frankincense over it; and place a piece of diachylon plaster and some other consolidating plaster on top of the powders, and leave it bound for two or three days. Then unbind it and change the medicine; and leave it until the threads separate on their own. Then care for it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suturing is to join the disruption together either with needles as we have described in suturing the abdomen or to join the disruption with thread as I have taught you.
Chapter XXVII.
On the Extraction of Nodes from the Lips.
Many people indeed have hard swellings within their lips, similar to grains of herb, some smaller and some larger.
Therefore, you must turn back the lip and cut over each node, suspending it with a hook and cutting into it from all sides.
Then fill the place after the incision with powdered tragacanth until the bleeding stops.
Then wash it with vinegar and treat the area with it because of its astringent properties, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the Incision of Flesh Born Over the Gum.
Very often flesh grows over the gum, which the ancients call an abuluss.
Therefore, you must either suspend it with a hook or hold it with forceps and cut it at its root, allowing the pus to flow or the blood.
Then place powdered tragacanth or other astringent, drying powders over the area.
But if the flesh returns after treatment, which often happens, then cut it a second time and cauterize it; for it will not return after cautery.
Chapter XXIX.
On the Scraping of Teeth with Iron.
Sometimes rough and foul crusts accumulate on the surfaces of teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums, often becoming blackened, yellowish, and even greenish, until corruption from them spreads to the gums, making the teeth foul.
Therefore you must make the weak person sit between your hands and place their head in your lap, then with a file and rasp remove any crusts from the teeth where they appear to you like sand until nothing remains. Do the same for blackness, greenness, yellowness, and other discolorations until they are cleansed.
Therefore, if that which is in them from the beginning of reasoning is taken away, it is good; and if not, then iterate reason over them on another day, the second, and the third, until finally study reaches what you wish.
And know that a tooth needs razors of various shapes, many figures; according to how your operation is prepared; for which the razor with which the tooth is shaved from the lower part is different from the razor with which it is shaved between the teeth, according to other forms.
And let these forms of razors be prepared by you, which you see.
Chapter XXX.
On the Extraction of Painful Teeth.
You ought to cure the tooth from its pain with all your ingenuity, and delay in its eradication. For there is nothing which can supply its place when it is eradicated, because it is a lofty substance: until when there is no excuse for its eradication, then it ought to be eradicated when the infirm one is forced, so that it may be strengthened and certified by you as a painful tooth.
Very often pain deceives the infirm, and they think that it is in the tooth which is sound and eradicate it; then the pain does not recede until the infirm tooth is eradicated.
We have already seen this from the operation of phlebotomists.
Therefore, when the painful tooth itself is certified by you, it ought to be scarified around with a spatulum, in which there is some virtue, until the gum is loosened from every part: then move it yourself with your fingers or with subtle forceps at first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps there with a good placement and the head of the infirm one between your knees which you have already held well so that it does not move: then draw the tooth according to straightness so as not to break it.
But if it does not come out, then take some of those instruments and insert it under it from every part with ease, and try its movement as you did at first.
If, however, a tooth is perforated or corroded, then it is necessary that you fill the hole with cloth and tie it with the end of a fine wire so that it does not break during the time you constrict it with forceps.
And you must act very carefully with the scarring all around the gum on every side.
And guard your strength so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and it returns upon the weak one from that evil which is greater than his first pain.
And beware lest you do what ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste and boldness in its eradication, beyond what we have described to be administered.
Very often they cause great infirmities to befall men, which are more difficult to break, and all its roots or some of them remain, or that they extract it with some bones of the mandible: as we have testified many times.
Then wash after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If it should happen that blood flows from the places (for this often happens), then apply something from zegi and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if zegi does not offer itself to you.
The shapes of the subtle forceps with which a tooth is moved first should have long shafts, a short handle, and be thick so that they do not bend when pressed upon a tooth: they should be like you see with large handles, until when you squeeze them they do not give way or bend; short shafts, and made of good iron or steel properly forged, straight-edged shafts: and on their extremities there should be teeth, some of which enter into others, and tighten with decent, strong constriction.
And sometimes the extremities are made like the shape of the mouth of a bird which is called a stork and they are strong constrictions.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the removal of teeth, and the extraction of broken jaw bones.
When there remains something broken during the removal of a tooth, it is necessary to place a compress with butter on the spot for one or two days until the area softens; then introduce an elevator or forceps into it, whose ends are similar to the beak of a bird called a stork. This is the shape of these forceps.
Let their extremities be made like a file inside: or like an awl.
But if it does not obey you in order to exit with these forceps, then it is necessary that you carve above the root and detach all the flesh, and insert an instrument called a small lever, whose shape is this.
Let its extremity be short and slightly thick, and let it not be soaked so that it does not break.
Therefore, if the root comes out through that, it is good; and if not, you must pry with these other instruments whose shapes are these.
The shape of the first has a triangular extremity, in which there is a certain thickness.
The shape of the second has a slender triangular extremity.
And with the other instruments and tools that preceded for the reason of teeth.
You can also pry with this instrument which resembles a large hook of this shape, with a twisted triangular extremity, in which there is a certain thickness so that it does not break; and let it not be soaked.
And know that dental instruments are many; and similarly, the remaining instruments are not counted, and a skillful craftsman, subtle in his art, invents an instrument for himself according to what the operation and illnesses themselves indicate to him.
Because there are illnesses from which the ancients did not say an instrument, due to the diversity of their kinds.
If, however, the jawbone or other bones of the mouth are broken, or become putrid: then carefully examine it in its place with the instruments and forceps which I have mentioned for the extraction of roots, and aid it with a speculum, the form of which is as follows.
Let there be something of a moderate size in it, so that the mouth may be drawn tight or held with it, and does not escape and wander, until the bone comes out; and restore the place with suitable medicines for that purpose.
But if there is a little putrefaction in the bone, then scrape it clean of its putrefaction and care for it until it heals.
Chapter XXXII.
Of the Locking of Teeth Which Project Beyond Others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, it makes the form ugly; and especially when this happens in women and girls, namely those who are marriageable.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth and does not fit, or its locking is not possible, nor its filing: then extract it; and if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, the form of which is as follows.
It is like a small beak, and is made of hardened iron, with a very sharp end.
And let your incision be made upon it over many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and that another tooth may not be disturbed.
But if it projects exposed to filing: then file it with a file of hardened iron, the form of which is as follows.
Let it be entirely of iron, and its handle have very small points, just like the file with which needles are made; with which the tooth may be filed gradually over many days easily so that the tooth is not disturbed and falls out; then soften it at the end and extract it with certain rasps.
If it is dense, from which a part breaks off and injures the tongue, then it must be filed even until the roughness of that fracture is removed and it is made equal; and smoothed, so that it does not injure the tongue nor corrupt speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the Retention of Teeth with Gold Wires.
When the front teeth suffer a concussion and contusion from a blow or fall, and the infirm person cannot hold anything they eat on it lest it fall out: and you have treated them with astringent medications, but the treatment has not been successful; then there is an ingenious way to bind them with gold wire: for silver turns green and decays after a few days, but gold never changes.
And let the wire be medium in fineness and thickness according to the amount that fits between the teeth.
And the method of retention is as follows: you take the wire and insert a double length of it between two sound teeth; then you weave the two ends of the wire between the loose teeth, whether there is one or more, until you reach with the weaving up to a sound tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving back to the side where you started; and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom until it is not moved at all; and let your constriction where you bind the wire be at the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose.
Then cut off the excess ends of the wire with forceps; and join both ends together and twist them with a gesture; and conceal them between a sound tooth and a loose tooth so that they do not injure the tongue; then let it remain tightened in this way.
But if it loosens or breaks, bind them again with another wire and it will remain thus for the whole time.
And this is the shape of the teeth and the figure of the retention of two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their places after a fall and are retained as we have described and remain; and this is not done except by a skillful, prudent, and experienced craftsman.
And sometimes one of the vaccine bones is adapted and made into the shape of a tooth, and it is placed in its place, from which the tooth is fortified and tightened as we said, and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV
Sometimes this ligament which occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a man is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already healed.
And the operation on it is to open the mouth of the sick person in your lap and lift up his tongue; then cut that nervous ligament across its width until the tongue is freed from its restraint.
If there is something of hardness and nodulation in it, and if it is from the healing of a wound, then project a hook into it and pierce it across its width until you strike the ligament and loosen the nodulation.
And be careful that there is no fissure deep in the flesh, and that you do not cut into an artery there, lest blood flow occur.
Then wash the area of the incision with rose water or vinegar, or cold water.
Then place a linen ligature under the tongue, which the sick person should hold in his mouth every night so that it does not heal back together again.
But if bleeding occurs, then put powdered alum on the area. But if blood overcomes you, then cauterize the area with a lenticular cautery which is suitable for it; then care for it as with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue resembling a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and perhaps it grows so large that it fills the mouth.
The operation on it is to open the sick person's mouth in front of the sun and consider the abscess.
Therefore, if you see it to be of a dark or black color, hard, and one to which the sick person does not feel sensation: then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
But if it inclines toward whiteness, and there is moisture in it, then thrust a hook into it, and cut it with a fine scalpel, and free it from every part. And if blood overcomes you during your operation, then place ground zedoary upon it until the bleeding stops; then return to your operation until you extract it with all its contents.
Then gargle it with vinegar and salt; and care for it with the remaining treatment suitable for it until it is healed.
Concerning the Treatment of an Abscess of Two Tonsils.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
Sometimes there occur between the throat glands, which are called almonds,  that protrude outward; and they are called two tonsils: when you treat these as we have said in division, and they do not heal, then consider.
Therefore if it is an abscess that is hard, dark in color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is reddish in color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood, but rather let it mature. And then either pierce it or it will burst on its own.
And if it is white in color, round, and has a slender root: then you must cut into it.
And the operation on it is that before its operation you should consider if the abscess has become quiet, and its heat completely subsided, or whether it diminishes somewhat; then make the sick person sit in front of the sun, and let their head be in your lap; open their mouth, and let a servant take it between his hands and press the tongue down with an instrument, which is shaped as follows.
Let it be made of silver, or of bronze, which is as fine as a knife.
Therefore when you press with it the tongue, and an abscess is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the amygdala, and draw it forth externally as much as you are able, besides which you draw with it something from the tunics.
Then incise it with an instrument, of which this is the following form. It is assimilated to forceps: nevertheless the extremities of it are curved, so that the opening of each one of them is directed into the other acutely: whence care should be taken that it is made of tempered iron, or silver imbued with brass.
But if this instrument is not present, then incise it with a scalpel, of which this is the form. Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one amygdala, incise the other according to this same kind of incision.
Then after the incision let the patient gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
But if there should happen to him a flow of blood, let him gargle with water in which have been boiled the husks of pomegranates: or myrtle leaves, and such like from astringents, until the flow of blood is cut off: then care for it, until it heals.
And sometimes there arise in the throat other abscesses, besides the two amygdalae. Therefore incise them according to what I said in the incision of the two amygdalae equally.
And I myself indeed have already treated a woman from an abscess, which had arisen between her throat, which was drawing it to a burning, few senses, which already almost closed the throat: and the woman was breathing from a narrow passage, and already prohibited her eating, and drinking water: and already she had arrived at death, if it had remained one day, or two days.
And the swelling had risen so high that it had sent forth two branches, until they extended beyond the two nostrils.
Therefore I hastened and fixed a hook into one of them. Then I drew it out, and a large portion was extracted from it.
Then I cut it off where it was grasped from the nostril.
Then I did the same to that which protruded from the other nostril.
Then I opened her mouth and pressed down her tongue; and afterward I fixed a hook into the swelling itself; then I cut off a portion from it, and only a little blood flowed from it; therefore the woman's throat was healed, and she immediately began to drink water, and then took food.
