Therefore I did not cease to cut from that swelling many times over a long period; and the swelling would succeed in the place where I had cut, until it was prolonged excessively and came out with it.
I then devised and cauterized the swelling between her throat.
Therefore it stopped from growing; then I departed, and did not know what God did with her after me.
On the Incision of a Swelling of the Palate.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
When phlegm descends to the palate and swells up and is long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a vault.
When it is treated with what we have said in the division and the treatment does not succeed; and you see that the swelling has become quiet and thin from it, then it is necessary to cut into it.
And that which is round and gathered from it, and does not have length, and is bloody or tawny colored or black, and has no sensation; then it is necessary to make an incision into it. For there is fear concerning the weak one.
Therefore it is necessary that when you see it according to the manner I have said from its whiteness and length, you should make the weak person sit before the sun, and press their tongue with the instrument of which I have previously spoken; then fix a hook in the uvula and draw it downwards; and cut it with one of the two instruments I mentioned for cutting the two tonsils.
And it is necessary that you not cut from it except that which is added beyond the natural term without addition.
For if you cut from it more, you will injure the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use that which we have narrated in the incision of the two tonsils; and care for it until it heals.
But if the weak person is frightened by the incision, then it is necessary that you use skill in cauterizing it without fear and dread.
And the method of cauterization in it is not except with sharp water; and it is, that you place the head of the weak person in your lap. Then press their tongue with the instrument which we have mentioned. Then take from the water which I have told you in the chapter on cauterization and mix it with a coal not extinguished, and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill from it the concavity of this instrument, of which this is the shape.
Let the extremity in which medicine is placed have a concavity, like the concavity of a radius cochlea.
And place the instrument with medicine on the uvula itself; and let the weak person lie on their side so that saliva runs from their mouth out of the inner medicine, so that nothing descends from it to their throat and harms them.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you from the first place it on the uvula for half an hour, until you see it already blackened and the bite of the medicine subdued.
And if you wish to take a piece of cotton and wrap it over the end of the radius, and infuse the cotton with medicine, and introduce the radius with the cotton into the cannula from above, until the cotton adheres onto the tip.
Do that many times, until you achieve what you desire concerning the cauterization of the vein. Then let it alone; for it will dry up and fall off after three days or four.
And if you wish to iterate the medicine, repeat it; and after the cauterization wash around the vein with cotton soaked in butter, and cleanse it of what is around it from the medicine; and bathe it with cold water.
And let it be treated externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
Therefore this kind of treatment is healthier than incision and further from danger.
And sometimes a vein is also treated with something more subtle than cautery and incision; for it is treated with fumigation, in this manner. Take calamus, and hyssop, and origanum, and rue, and sage, and chamomile, and abrotanum, and things like them from herbs; and gather all these together, or some of them in a pot, and submerge them with vinegar, and let them boil; and the pot should be plastered well with clay; and there should be a hole in the middle of the pot, above which an instrument concave in shape is placed.
Let it be made of silver or bronze; and insert the extremity, in which there is a pomegranate, into the mouth of the sick person, until the vapor rises to the vein above the cannula, until the vein becomes very dark.
Then repeat it over it many times, until it dries up.
And be careful not to do this treatment at the beginning of the arrival of an abscess. For it usually adds to the abscess; and it is not right for you to do this unless at the decline of its heat.
If you do not have this instrument present, then take a tube and put bark on the end of it so that the mouth of the sick person is not burned; for the bark prevents vapor from burning the mouth; and this is good healing with safety.
On the Extraction of Thorns and Bones from the Throat.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
Very often a bone or a fishbone, or something else adheres in the throat.
Therefore you must extract what is apparent from them upon which vision falls after you press the tongue with an instrument toward the sun, and it is declared to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, you must make the sick person vomit before the food is digested in the stomach. For perhaps the attached thing will go out through vomiting.
Or let the sick person swallow a piece of turnip or a lettuce root, or swallow a mouthful of dry bread; or take a piece of dried gentle sea sponge and tie it with thread, then swallow it. When it reaches the place where the spines or bone are, and they are extracted, then they come out.
But if it does not come out with what we have said, then use an instrument of lead according to this form.
Let it be somewhat thicker than a spoke and let there be a curve at the end of it, so that it may enter his throat easily; and let him have his head elevated upward.
And be careful of touching his epiglottis so that coughing does not happen to him and he drives out the bone or spine with it, or let the doctor introduce it with his own hand.
But for the sick person to introduce it is better: because of his knowledge of the location of the attached thing; and he drives it down or pulls his hand upward with the instrument, all of it according to the quantity that is prepared for him until it comes out.
On the Extraction of a Leech.
Chapter XXXIX.
When a leech is treated with what I have said in the division on healing with medicines and it does not detach, then consider in the throat of the sick person in front of the sun, or by the sun after you press its tongue with an instrument which I have described to you.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook, or with delicate forceps discreetly.
And if this is not possible with them: then take a concave cannula and insert it into the throat of the sick person up to near the throat; then insert iron heated with fire into the concavity of the cannula. Do this many times, and let the sick person abstain from all water for a whole day.
Then let him take a goblet full of cold water and open his mouth into it; and moisten his mouth with it and not swallow anything from it; and stir the water with his hand one hour after another. For the leech immediately, when it feels the water, falls off.
But if it does not depart with what we have described, then fumigate the sick person with bedbugs, or with asafoetida with an instrument which I have described in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this many times, for it will fall off.
And the manner of operation in fumigation is to take a pot in which there are live prunes with fire; and let the pot be covered with a lid, in the middle of which there is a hole; and let the extremity of the instrument be placed into that hole.
Then project the fumigation and let the sick person place his mouth at the extremity of the cannula and close his mouth so that the fumigation does not escape until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. For the leech will immediately fall off.
If it does not fall out, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the sick person endure thirst, and eat salt things, and garlic, and let him not drink water.
Therefore it is necessary that he goes out with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is extracted from the throat when it falls upon it by sight.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature which is inserted into the throat. And the extremity of it is similar to the beak of a bird which is called a stork, in which there is roughness like a file: when it grasps something, it does not release it at all.
Concerning the opening of abscesses, and their incision, and care in general.
Chapter 40.
There are many kinds of abscesses, of many ways, according to what comes to the recollection of them, namely one, and one in this book: and they are different in their perforation, and incision of themselves in two ways.
One of which is from the kind of abscess itself, and that because it comprises from humidities.
And the second way is on account of the places, in which it happens in the body.
Because an abscess happening in the head is different from an abscess happening in the anus: and an abscess happening in a fleshy place is different from an abscess happening in joints: and to each of them there is a judgment of its own from the operation.
And from abscesses there are which ought not to be perforated except after the maturation of blood in them, and its completion; and from them there are which ought to be perforated while they are raw, and do not mature according to completion.
Such as abscesses which are near joints.
Because an abscess when it happens near a joint, and is prolonged to be its own, until it putrefies what is in its circumference perhaps the ligaments, or nerves of that joint are corrupted: therefore there is a cause for the closer contraction of that member.
Either there is an abscess at a major limb; for if you delay its incision until it matures, it will harm either that limb or the main limb through it.
Or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it while immature; and it is not necessary to perforate an abscess crude, of incomplete digestion, except that which is near the anus, lest it putrefy deeply, and extend to the interior of the anus, and a fistula be formed, or a termination thereof which does not heal.
You ought to know the hour of incision of an abscess, which is matured according to completeness; and it is at sedation of pain, recession of fever, and diminution of redness, and throbbing, and sharpness of the head of the abscess; and the remaining signs.
And it is necessary that you make the same incision in the lower part of the abscess, and of more vehement eminency; and let the incision be wading in length according to the body, if the abscesses are in parts of the hands and feet, and places of muscles, and chords, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflection.
But in those which are reflected, wade with the incision according to that limb.
But when an abscess is in fleshy places; then it is better that you dismiss its incision until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to completeness.
For if you perforate it before that, the course of the virus is prolonged from it, and there is much filth and foulness, and perhaps its lips will become hardened, and its depth.
And certain abscesses are perforated according to the breadth of the body according to necessity; or according to that in which the limb needs.
And it is necessary that you administer a small incision in small abscesses, and a large incision in large abscesses; or many sections through similarity to the magnitude of the abscess.
And they are from abscesses, in which the skin must be concave; and it should be cut when it has become like cloth, and has been made at the end, which is already dead: just as happens in many, such as boils and buboes.
And of these are those which are cut with a section having three angles. And of these is one, from which it is cut like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Such as an abscess of the groin.
And of these are those in which a round section and a lunar section are administered; and similar to them from sections.
But that which has no head, such as mild superficial abscesses, must be perforated with a superficial perforation or only flat.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected much pus and you perforate it, do not hurry and draw out all the pus in that hour; rather, draw out a part of it. Then close the abscess until another day. Then again draw out something of the pus.
Do this many times according to gradation, until it comes out; especially if the patient is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old man who has grown weak; for indeed the animal spirit is often resolved from the animal with the outflow of pus; and perhaps the sick person dies, and you do not perceive it.
Therefore beware of this chapter with great caution.
And after you have perforated these abscesses, it is necessary that you wash the wound; and consider. Therefore if the wound of the abscess is small, or it is a flat section, then apply a bandage from linen or old cotton. And if the abscess is large and there are many fissures of the perforation, it is necessary then to insert a bandage into every fissure until they reach each other.
And if in an abscess you have cut off a part of the skin, or have scooped it out around its circumference: then it is necessary that you fill it with old lint, or as when cloth is woven, or shaved with flax down without moisture; and bind it up until the third day.
Then loosen it, and care for it with what is necessary from ointment, until it heals.
But if bleeding should happen during your operation, then administer cold water and vinegar, after a cloth has been soaked in them; and place it upon the place of the bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding should persist, then it is necessary that you administer a regimen and care, and powders which I have described in many places of this book of ours, and by division.
But if it is in the time of winter, and the place of the abscess is of many nerves: then it is necessary that you pour warm broth with wine and oil; and place them upon the places.
And if it should be in summer, and the places are fleshy: then it is necessary that you place broth soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and all of it cold. Until when it will be the third day, as we said, then it is necessary that you loosen the abscess, and wash it out, and administer in its care what suits it, until it heals.
This, therefore, is what is necessary from knowledge of treatment of abscesses universally.
According to the way of distinction, I have now spoken of every abscess, how its way to cure is taken up individually.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which occur in the skin of the head.
Small abscesses occur in the skin of the head, and they are of a species of nodules, which contain sheaths, which are sacs to them: as if they themselves were like pouches of a hen.
And there are many kinds of them.
Among them are some that are fatty; and among them are some that contain moisture like mud; and among them are some that contain moisture like curdled milk and pulps; and those similar to them; and among them are some that are hard and stony.
And in the section of all of them, and their extraction, there is no fear, as long as the artery does not make resistance at their section.
And the operation in their section is that you try them with an instrument, whose form comes after this, which is called an Intromissum, until you know what it contains.
For if it is that which contains moisture, then dry it by a section according to its length with a superficial section, according to this form.
And begin with a section from line b to line g.  Therefore when the moisture is evacuated, then the skin kistin which contains it, and do not let anything remain within it.
For very often it returns if anything remains within it.
Then immerse the stump in Egyptian ointment if it is present; and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For it will corrode what remained from the kist.
Then repeat upon the stump with a second, and third Egyptian ointment, and if it does not yield to that, until you are confident that nothing remained from the kist, then care for the wound with ointment until it heals.
But if the abscess contains a fatty node, then dry it according to this form, with a section having the form of a cross; and project hooks between the two lips of the section, and skin it from every side; and exert your strength in extracting the tunic which contains it.
But if the artery makes resistance to you, then do what I will tell you afterward.
And do likewise for an abscess, if it is stony, from incision and treatment the same as we have said.
And incision over a stony abscess is easier because there is little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are certain of these abscesses which are in the head in some people with which there is no moisture at all.
Which is because I cut into an abscess on the head of a young woman and found the abscess like a solid and hard stone, rough and white, which could not be broken; and if one should strike it with a projectil it would wound the person.
But in other abscesses occurring in the head, besides these abscesses which occur in the heads of infants and at the roots of ears, the incision of all of them should be a superficial incision.
Or make a puncture of them always from their lower part so that the course of blood is relieved downwards.
Then treat it with what suits them from its treatment.
Concerning the incision of strangles in the neck.
Chapter 42.
Very often these abscesses occur in the neck and under the armpits and groins; and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And those that occur from them in the neck are one and many and they are generated from each other; and every strangles from them is within its own tunic as it is in nodes and abscesses of the head as we have described.
And there are many kinds of these strangles. For some of them are stony and some contain moisture and some are malignant, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore in that one which you see from them of good disposition to the touch, of which it is clear that it is close to the color of the skin and moves to every part, and is not joined with the nerves of the neck, nor the gland, nor the artery and is not deep: it is necessary that you cut it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body from line b. to line g. and flay it from all sides; and extend two lips of skin with a hook or hooks if needed as we said in abscesses of the head, and extract it gradually.
And it is a precaution, that a vein or nerve not be cut.
And let the knife be not very sharp, so that it does not add to your hand with the incision, nor agitate the sick person, and you may cut what is not necessary.
But if you cut a vein or artery, or it prevents you from the operation, then put into the wound a boiled herb, or certain powders that staunch blood, and constrict the wound, and let it be until the sharpness of the inflammation subsides, and the wound softens: or it begins to become putrid.
For blood is staunched by a tunic. Return therefore to your operation until you complete it.
Then inquire with your index finger if anything remains from the little styptics or others: therefore cut them and clean them.
But if there is in the root of a stypsis a large vein: then it is necessary that you not cut that stypsis from its root: but rather that you tie it with a strong doubled thread, and let it be until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old lint, which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment. Then take care of it.
But if the whole stypsis is cut, then it is necessary that you bring together the two lips of the wound: and sew it immediately after you see that there is no surplus remaining at all.
But if you see that there is a surplus remaining because of the size of the stypsis, then it is necessary that you intend to cut its root and extract it: and apply the suture as we have said.
And that which contains moisture from the styptics, pierce it with a superficial perforation so that the place of its maturity appears to you: and place the perforation from what follows below the body as we have said.
Then administer after the perforation linen with ointment and things similar to them so that it corrodes what remains from the corruption until when you see that the wound is already cleansed: then take care of it with a generative ointment of flesh until it heals.
Chapter XLIII.
On the incision of the epiglottis affected by an abscess within the throat.
The ancients spoke of this incision in the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has done it; and this is the writing of their words.
But in those who have fluxion, the epiglottis must be incised when there is no benefit from that; because all the veins and lungs are weak.
But in those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no pain in the canal, the epiglottis must be incised to avoid the destruction which results from premonition.
Therefore, when we wish to do this, we must cut the epiglottis below three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision in width, in that which is between the two circles by an amount that makes the incision in the flesh, not in the cartilage; and indeed this place is suitable for the incision because it is private to flesh, and blood vessels are far from it.
But if the physician is timid, then he must extend the skin of the throat with a hook. Then let him pierce the skin until he reaches the canal; divert the blood vessels if he sees anything from them.
Then let him cut the flesh as we have described; and it is indicated above the incision of the canal by phlegm which issues from it, with what is broken up, and cessation of voice.
And leave the wound open for a time.
Therefore when the hour is removed in which premonition is feared, join the two lips of the skin wound and sew it alone without cartilage.
Then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow until it heals.
Says Albucasis. The sum of this discourse which we have narrated is not exposition except when they see that some of these apostemata already has suppurated the throat of the sick person and he is raised up on the point of death and desires to intercept his breaths: they go to cutting the epiglottis so that the sick person may breathe upon the place of the wound with a certain respiration and be saved from death; and for this reason they ordered to leave the wound open until the malice of the sickness is consumed and it is the malice of this three days and as if itself: then therefore they ordered to sew the wound and to heal it until it heals.
And that which I myself saw was that a certain handmaid took a knife and sent it into her throat and it fell upon a part of the pulmonary canal.
And I was called to her healing and I found her moaning like is the moan of a dead person.
I therefore examined from the wound and I found little blood which had issued from the wound. I was therefore perplexed because she had not cut a vein nor her gullet and wind was coming out from the wound.
I therefore hastened and sewed up her wound and healed it until it was well and nothing happened to the handmaid except hoarseness in her voice and she returned after days to a better disposition of hers.
From this therefore I say that there is no fear in the cutting of the epiglottis.
Concerning the cutting of a hernia of the throat.
Chapter Forty-Four.
This swelling called a hernia of the throat is a large swelling according to the color of the body and it is much in women.
And it is according to two ways either it is natural or accidental.
In the natural there is certainly no craft.
The accidental however is according to two ways of which one is similar to fatty nodes. Another method is similar to an abscess which occurs from tying off an artery. And there is fear within it, and it is not fitting that presumption be made against it with iron except in that which is small; and you test it, and investigate it with an Intromisso. If therefore you find it similar to a fat knot, and it is not suspended with any veins: then cut it off, as knots are cut; and extract it with that which it contains from the cyst, if it is in a cyst: and if not, then scrutinize its entirety. Then care for the place with what is fitting from healing, until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On the Section of Different Kinds of Knots.
There are many kinds of knots, and I have already said all their kinds in the division; and it is fitting that I tell here the difference between a Knot and an Excrescence: since there is a doubt-causing one.
I say therefore that when there is an Excrescence there is heat, and fever, and violent pains until the boiling of superfluity is calmed, and putrefaction is completed; then indeed fever and heat subside.
But with a Knot there is no heat, nor fever, nor pain; and they contain them in cysts sifachin, which are their own sac, and it is according to the color of the body; and its beginning is like a pea, and it becomes like a date-stone, larger and smaller.
And they are according to two kinds: either fatty or containing moisture. And the colors of moisture are many, according to what I said in the division.
Therefore it is fitting that when you come to the healing of a Knot, that you experiment on it, and inquire into it first with an instrument called an Intromisso, according to how its form will come in the chapter which follows this.
And the method of inquiry for abscesses, and all Knots, is to take this instrument, and insert it into the moister place which you find in the abscess; and you revolve your fingers with it gradually, until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then place your last finger according to the measurement of the size of the abscess.
Then extract the intrument and consider that which comes out in its wake.
Therefore if flowing moisture emerges, whatever its color may be: then dry it with a superficial incision, as I have said in other abscesses.
And if moisture does not emerge in the wake of the instrument, then know that it is thick: therefore dry it above with an incision in the shape of a cross, according to this form as I have taught you: and suspend it with hooks, and flay the skin from every part with ease: and keep the plaster if you are able upon it so that you extract it whole with its knot.
But if the cyst ruptures during the operation and it is not possible to extract it whole, for this often happens, then extract it in pieces until nothing remains of it.
For if a little or a lot remains of it, the knot will return for the most part.
And if it overcomes you and a little remains from it: then fill the wound upon completion with certain corrosive and sharp powders: and constrict the wound and place above it what sits upon the abscess hot, and care for it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
Therefore if the knot is large, then sew its two lips together, and care for it as it heals.
But if there is a vein contrary to you, pulsatile or not pulsatile: then hurry and fill the place with finely ground triturated matter, and let it remain constricted for two or three days until the wound putrefies and the boiling of the blood subsides: then return to incise what remains from the knot.
Chapter Forty-Six.
Concerning external instruments for incision and perforation.
Among them are the forms of instruments, and they are of three kinds.
For from them is a great one, and from them is a medium one, and from them is a small one.
Let them be made of well-tempered iron with square ends, so that they hasten entry into the abscess.
And these are the forms of Instruments, and namely, piercing; and one of them is called a bistoury, that is, a piercer; and they are of three kinds.
For from them is a great one, a medium one, and a small one.
They are suited to investigate abscesses through them, and outlets, and fistulas, and buboes from what is between bones.
And others are made round, smooth, gentle, like large needles with which sutures are taken, namely from brass, or bronze, or iron, or silver.
And the better ones of them are those made from brass.
And Instruments are also made of black lead, which are suitable so that fistulas may be investigated through them, with which there are reflections due to their concavity, so that they may be reflected gently with those reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. For from them are long ones, from them medium ones, and from them short ones, according to the quantity of the width of the fistula and its tightness.
The form of Hooks, and they are of many kinds.
For from them are simple ones, namely those which have a single curve; and they are of three kinds as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And from them are blind Hooks; and they are of three kinds.
And from them are Hooks having two curves, and they are of three kinds; and from them are Hooks having three curves, and they are of three kinds; and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of Knives, with which nodes and abscesses are cut and pared; and they are of three kinds: for from them is a great one, a medium one, and a small one.
Their extremities by which the section is made are sharp, other extremities are not sharp; and they are not put thus, except so that with them refreshment may be made in the excision of nodes at fear of incision of a vein or nerve; and that he who is ill may be healed by them, and find a little tranquility from the burning which he finds in the excision of an abscess.
They are made of metal, and are similar to a Radiator, with which alcohol is made; and at the wide end there is a hidden Spatula point: and it runs to the inside, and outside when you wish, as you see.
The shape of the spatulas which are hidden between the fingers at the excision of abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
Shapes of Suction Cups with which the flow of blood is cut off; and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
Let them be made of metal or brass, round, a little in length as you see; and let them be delicate.
And it is necessary that these kinds of Suction Cups be large and small with you so that you may cut off blood quickly when needed and then when medicine is not present for you.
However, do not use them in the cutting off of blood in every place of the body, and do not use them except in fleshy places: such as the calves of the leg, and the hip, and the calves of the arms, and the breasts, and the belly, and the groin, and similar ones, from fleshy calves, moist.
And sometimes other small instruments are made from them similar to pistachio shells according to their shape; with which blood is cut off when it flows from the place of phlebotomy or at the incision of a vein or artery: and sometimes they are made round according to this shape.
Chapter XLVII.
On the treatment of the mammary glands of men which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the nipples of certain men swell up during sleep, until they resemble women's breasts, and they remain foul abscesses.
He who wishes to get rid of this should make a crescent-shaped incision above the nipples, from line b to line g. Then he should remove all the skin and fat. Afterwards fill the wound with flesh-producing medicine. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with sutures and care for it until it heals.
But if the nipple hangs down and softens because of its size, as happens to women: then it is necessary to make two incisions on its upper sides, similar in shape to a crescent moon, each contained with the other at the ends of both, until there is a larger line containing the smaller one, according to this form from line b. to line g. Afterwards remove the skin which is between the two lips and take away the fat, and apply what we said about sutures and powders, which are necessary for healing, until it heals.
But if you cannot complete the incision as needed because of the patient's restlessness or the flow of blood: then you must fill the wound with cotton soaked in a corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then care for it until it heals.
On the perforation of an abscess which occurs under the nipple.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses which occur under the nipple are of a hard kind like a Strophulus, which contain cysts and those which contain moisture.
Those therefore which are of the kind that contain moisture, should be cut with a crescent-shaped incision, according to this form, from line b to line g. When all that is in the abscess has been evacuated as pus, then fill it with old cotton and leave it until the next day.
Then let the shirt be removed, and let it be treated with a suitable ointment until it is healed.
But if the abscess is of the type of knotty Strophulus, then cut it above it, as we said in the section on Strophulus equally.
But if the wound extends so as to discharge pus, then apply a cautery to it, according to what we have said before.
Chapter XLIX.
On the section of an abscess which occurs due to Artery and Vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the skin above it becomes consolidated, then an abscess often occurs from this; and similarly it happens with a vein, so that an abscess and swelling occur in it.
And the signs by which it is known that an abscess and swelling are due to an artery and vein are: because when the abscess is due to an artery, it is long, gathered deep in the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a rasping sound.
And that which is due to a vein is round, manifest on the body.
And the section of this abscess is an error and especially that which is under the armpits and groins and neck and in many places on the body; and it is very large. Therefore, its treatment must be divided with iron, and what is also from them in the extremities, or in the head, must be divided.
Therefore, on that which is from them by inflation of the orifice of the artery, make a section in the skin lengthwise. Then open the section with hooks, then the artery; and free it from its tunics until it is exposed.
Then insert a needle under it and make it penetrate up to the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread in two places, according to what I have taught you in the extraction of the two arteries that are in the temples.
Then puncture the place which is between the two ligaments with a trocar, until all the blood contained within it comes out, and the abscess is resolved.
Then apply a treatment that produces pus, until the ligaments fall away; afterwards treat it with suitable ointments, until it heals.
But if the abscess is due to a vein being cut, then you must hold with your hand as much of the abscess with the skin as possible. Then insert a needle under the place which you held with your hand and make it penetrate; and let there be a double thread in it until you draw it out from the other side. Then tie with it the abscess with a good ligament; according to what I told you in the vein ligament, from two parts with two ends of thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread under the whole abscess at the puncture; press and tighten your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle, until what is inside it comes out; then cut away the excess skin and release what was tied from it.
Then place upon it a poultice which you have soaked in wine and oil. Then apply treatment, made with licorice and ointments until it heals.
Chapter I.
Concerning an abscess which occurs from the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in arteries and veins, similarly an abscess occurs in nerves; which happens to them through a blow or from excessive labor and similar causes; and it most often occurs in those places where the limbs join, such as the heel, and in every place where the joints are moved, and the abscess is severe.
Its color resembles the rest of the body, and it is in many positions without pain; and when it is squeezed with force, a weak sensation similar to numbness is felt within it.
And it is not a gathered abscess deep within the body, but rather under the skin, and itself moves to every part, and does not go forward, nor before, nor backward to the rear.
Therefore, that which is from them in the joints should not be invaded with iron. For perhaps a contraction may occur.
But that which is from them in the head or forehead, cut the skin with a scalpel. And if therefore it is a small abscess, then hold it with forceps and cut it from the root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook and flay it: then remove it and gather the wound with sutures: and tend to it until it heals.
Chapter LI.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the abdomen.
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in the other parts of their bodies, boils which are called fungal, by similarity to fungi.
The root of which is subtle, and the head is thick, and their lips are inverted. And there are some small ones from them, and there are some that greatly enlarge.
And I myself have indeed already seen a man, and in his belly were two boils which were more like fungi than anything else: there was no difference between them, white, of a small root, and their lips were inverted, and they split open, and moisture always flowed from them.
Therefore I cut into them and found in the weight of one almost ten and eight ounces and in the other almost six ounces. And the operation in their incision is that you consider then if he is weak and moist and the color of the boil is white, moist, of a delicate root: then cut it with a broad scalpel, and have a cautery present with you in the fire.
For very often much blood is expelled at its incision; therefore hasten and if blood overcomes you, then cauterize it.
But if you see a weak and timid person from the incision with a knife, then take a thread made of lead properly, and bind it with that thread: whose method is what we said, and let him go for two days.
Then add lead to the ligature. Therefore do not cease binding the lead whenever it presses into the wound, until it is cut off and falls by itself without labor.
But if the wart has a thick root, then there is some fear and dread in its incision; especially because it is on the abdomen.
However it is possible to cut into half of it. Then cauterize it so that it does not return.
Then care for it until the wound heals.
And beware that you do not presume to cut into a wart which is of a dark color, with little sensation, and an ugly appearance; for it is indeed a cancerous abscess; and I will come with the remembrance of Cancer after this.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On the care of the prominence of the navel.
The prominence of the navel arises from many causes. Or from fissures of the omentum, which is over the abdomen; and there issues forth fat, or intestine, according to what happens in other ruptures. Or from blood flowing from a vein, or an artery, according to what has been said previously. Or from wind compressed within it.
But if it is due to a fissure of the omentum and the issuing forth of fat; then its color resembles the color of the body; and it is soft, without pain; and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of intestine, then its location is, along with what we have described, of a more violent diversity. When therefore you press it with your fingers, it is concealed; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it magnifies greatly at the entrance of the bath, and a vehement labor.
But if it is on account of humidity, it is soft, then it is not concealed when you press it with your hand, nor does it become constricted, nor is anything added.
But if it is on account of blood, then with these signs an abscess appears tending to blackness from the clefts.
But if it is on account of flesh that has formed, then the abscess is heavy, thick, hard, and it is fixed according to a single quantity.
But if it is on account of windiness, then its touch is soft.
And the procedure in that case is, that you then consider whether the prominence of the navel is on account of blood from an Artery or Vein, or windiness; then it is necessary that you forbid its treatment. For there is fear and dread, as I taught you in the chapter in which I said abscesses are on account of artery and vein.
But if the prominence of the navel is on account of intestine or membrane; then it is necessary that you direct the sick person to hold his breath and stand upright extended. Then mark with sealing wax all around the circumference of the navel. Then direct him to lie down between your hands on his back. Then encircle with a broad spatula around the navel upon the place which you marked with sealing wax. Then extend the middle of the abscess upward with a large hook. Then tie it around firmly with thread or a cord made of silk with a strong binding; and let the knot of the ligament be away from the constriction.
Then open the extended middle of the abscess above the ligature. If then you find it already caught by a ligament: then extract the alenxotati, and insert the index finger into it, and push the intestine towards the interior of the abdomen; and if you find a zirbum, then take it up with a hook, and cut away its excess.
But if it is an artery or vein to the contrary, then bind it very much. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert into them two strong threads; and insert the two needles into the circumference which you made around the apostema in the manner of a cross, which you make penetrate.
Then constrict the apostema in four places over the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place until the extended flesh rots; and falls off by itself: or cut it when it softens and decays.
Then treat it with what is necessary from ointments, until it heals.
But if the eminence of the umbilicus is from fleshous growth in it, or from moisture: then it is necessary that you make concave apostemas, as we have said. And draw out the blood or moisture which you find in it.
Then treat it with what strengthens the place.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
Concerning the Care of CANCER.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of Cancer, and how is the way to its cure with medicines; and we warned against it when curing it with iron, so as not to ulcerate it: and we spoke about Cancer generated in the womb, and cautioned against its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes Cancer is in a place where it is possible to eradicate it; such as Cancer which is in the breasts, or in the thigh, and those similar to them, from the limbs located for extracting all of it; especially when it is beginning and small.
When it is old and large, you should not approach it closely.
For I myself was unable to heal anyone from it, nor did I see anyone before me who had succeeded in doing so.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is to precede and loosen the weak part from the black bile several times. Then phlebotomy itself if you find it with manifest fullness.
Then prepare the weak part with preparation in which it will be placed during the operation.
Then introduce into the cancer hooks that are suitable for it. Then scoop it out from every part with the skin, according to careful examination, until nothing remains of its roots; and let the blood flow, and do not cut it off quickly, rather press the locations and wipe away all thick blood with your hand, or with whatever instrument is possible there.
But if the flow of thick blood from the incision of an artery or vein opposes you, then cauterize the vein until the blood is cut off. Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Hydropsy.
Chapter LIV. We have already explained in the division the kinds of hydropsy, and how the gathering of water occurs; and the signs of each kind, and its treatment with medicine.
And that which is treated with iron is only the kind of ascites alone; and it does not approach the kind of tympanitis with iron, nor the kind of hydrosarca deeply; for that is fatal.
When you have already treated this kind of hydropsy, tympanitis with medicine, and your medication has not been successful, then consider. For if the weak person has already experienced weakness, or is with it another infirmity besides hydropsy; such as if there is a cough with it, or loosening of the bowels, and things similar to those: then beware of treating him with iron, for there is fear of that.
If you see a weak person with excessive humors and there is no illness with him except dropsy alone: and he is neither a child nor an old man: then the method of operation on him is that you raise the weak person standing between your hands, and the assistant behind him presses his belly with his hand, driving the water to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a spiky speculum in this form. Let it be of two sharp points, long, with an acute extremity like a speculum. However, there should be some slight shortness in it so that you do not pierce through to the intestines during the operation and injure them.
Then consider. For if the dropsy generation is from the part of the intestines, then it is necessary that you lengthen the incision from the navel by the length of three fingers, downwards directly above the breastbone.
But if the dropsy generation is due to liver disease: then let your incision be on the left, from the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is due to the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side; and it is also not necessary for the incision to be on the side on which the weak person wishes to lie, so that the superfluities do not flow to that weak spot.
Then pierce all the skin with an instrument. Then insert into that incision and lift your hand with the speculum between the skin and the flesh as if you are flaying it; and let the amount that is flayed be about the size of a nail or thereabouts.
Then pierce the flesh until the speculum reaches the hollow place; and that is the place of the water. Then extract it, and insert into the hole an instrument of this form. And it is like a cannula: made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth, polished; with a small hole at its lower part and three holes on its sides, two on one side and one on the other: and the tip of it is cut in the manner of a reed's incision, as you see; there is a ring at its upper end.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it descends immediately upon the instrument, therefore it evacuates from the water in an hour a medium quantity.
For if you evacuate from it more than is necessary, in an hour perhaps the sick man dies through the resolution of his animal spirit; or syncope happens to him which approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the quantity of his strength, and according to what the dispositions of the sick man signify to you from the virtue of his pulse, and from his good nature.
Then extract the instrument, and retain the water.
Which is because it is retained immediately on account of the skin which retains the hole which is above the sifac: of which I have spoken, so that you perforate it according to that manner.
Then repeat the instrument on the next day, if you see the sick man tolerate it; and evacuate also from the water a small quantity.
Do that for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error: until there does not remain from the water except a little.
But if you fear for the sick man, and dismiss from the water a great deal; then care for him with burial in warm sand, and much sweat in a bath and sun, and wait for thirst upon him; and care for him with drying medicines until he is healed; and cauterize above the stomach, and liver, and spleen, after the extraction of the water: according to what I have spoken in that which precedes.
Concerning the care of infants who are born with their urine places not perforated.
Chapter Fifty-Five
When any of the infants exits from the belly of its mother, and the head of its penis is not perforated, or there is a tight opening, or it is in a not proper place.
It is necessary therefore to assist with its perforation immediately when it is born with a fine Spatula according to this form.
Then place a fine lead plumb bob in the hole and tie it in place; hold it for three or four days.
Therefore, when he wishes to pass urine, remove it and let him urinate. Then replace it.
And if you do not place a lead weight, it is possible that the urine which flows over the area will prevent it from closing.
But he whose opening is narrow should be treated with a lead weight, as we have said, for many days until it is enlarged.
Those in whom the opening is in an unnatural place are injured. This is because the opening in those who are born with it is at the end of the penis: therefore he cannot urinate forward until he lifts his penis upward with his hand. Nor can he procreate because the semen cannot reach the womb in a straight line; and it is a very foul disease.
The method of operation for this condition is that the patient should lie on his back. Then extend the head of his penis with your left hand and make an incision on the top of the penis from its original position, with a sharp knife or scalpel, as though cutting a reed or radish so that the middle becomes like the head of the penis; and so that the opening falls in the middle as it should.
And beware of bleeding during the procedure. It often happens. Therefore apply a styptic to him with it and care for the wound until it heals.
Concerning ailments that occur in the foreskin and head of the penis, and putrefaction, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
These ailments often occur on the penis and are a foul prominence of flesh.
And some of them are malignant, and some are not malignant.
Therefore the non-malignant one must be suspended with a fine hook and cut off until it is entirely cleansed. Then place upon it cotton submerged in Egyptian ointment. Then care for it afterward with dyafinicon ointment until it heals.
But if it is malignant, of foul color, then it is necessary that you apply a cautery to it after its incision and the removal of its root.
But if the albather is in the foreskin of a captive, uncircumcised man, and part of it is between the foreskin and part of it is outside, then you must first remove the albather which is inside; until it consolidates, then care for the one outside. For when you care for them both at the same time, the foreskin will not be secured lest it be pierced.
And they also sometimes occur in the testicles, and blackness and corruption of the foreskin. Therefore it is necessary that you cut out everything which has already become blackened and which is wanting to be corrupted or has already been corrupted.
Then smear it afterward with honey which has already been mixed with flour of pomegranate rinds and crushed herb crib.
Then care for it with the remaining treatment until it heals.
But if there occurs a flow of blood, then apply a cautery with a lunar cautery according to this form.
For the cautery is helpful for two conditions at the same time, namely for a flow of blood and for a wound when it becomes corrupted.
But if the head of the penis is corroded and entirely destroyed within an hour: then it is necessary that you insert a lead cannula into the urethral opening so that the weak one may urinate upon it.
In the case of adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the penis, and this adhesion does not occur except in him whose foreskin is healthy and circumcision is not required upon it: and sometimes adhesion occurs because of a wound or an abscess: therefore it is necessary that you scrape it with a blunt spatula until the ligament is loosened and the head of the penis is freed from every part.
If it is difficult to distinguish these exquisitely, then it is necessary to excise something from the head of the penis, and from the foreskin. This is because when the foreskin is delicate it may perhaps be perforated quickly on account of its delicacy.
Then separate between the foreskin and the head of the penis with a fine linen cloth which has already been moistened in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
Then let it be treated with astringent wine until it firms up.
Chapter LVII.
On the cleansing of infants, and the treatment of injuries accidental to them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds: nevertheless since it is from our operation, by our will, and its use is proper in boys, it is necessary that we mark in it a better operation and an easier way leading to health.
I say therefore that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books: because they do not use it in their laws and it is only something we have acquired through experience.
This is because I have found the greater part of craftsmen and phlebotomists using cleansing by circumcising with a razor and forceps; and they use cautery and ligation with thread, and incision with a nail.
And I myself have now tested all these methods, and I have not found a better one than cleansing with forceps and ligation with thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, the skin often escapes. Because the skin of the foreskin has two coats: therefore perhaps the outer skin is cut and the inner coat remains: it is then necessary to make an incision to cut the other one and pain will occur. And with cleansing which is done by cautery, that is, a tool used by shoemakers, the incision of the extremity of the male member is not safeguarded: because perhaps it enters into its opening.
In cleansing however, if the nail should happen to be caught by the iron, it spoils your operation; and the skin of a child is naturally thin, and many times they are born thus, so that they do not need cleansing; and I have already seen this.
The cleansing however with forceps and the tying with thread has proved a good experiment to me; because the forceps are proportionate to the incision: therefore, because the sharpness which is above, is like the sharpness which is below. Therefore when you put forth your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportionate and they fall according to one operation in one time; and a compression is made of a similar figure containing the skin of the rod from every side with which no error occurs at all.
And the method of the proper operation is that you should estimate the boy especially, if he is one of those who understand a little, that you do not do anything except tying thread on his rod only; and let him go until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be merry in every way as possible for you from that because he receives his own understanding. Then make him stand between your hands in a prepared posture and let him not be sitting; and conceal the forceps in your sleeve or under your foot so that the boy does not see them at all nor anything of the instruments.
Then introduce your hand to his rod and blow upon the skin and draw it up until the head of the rod comes out. Then cleanse it from that which collects on it filthiness.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards tie below it a little further down a second ligature. Then hold with your index finger and thumb the place of the lower ligature with a good grip and cut between the two ligatures. Then lift the skin up quickly and extract the head of the rod. Then let the blood flow gradually (for it is better) and put powder on top of it; and upon the powder a cloth in which has been spread egg yolk boiled in rose water, stirred with good fresh rose oil; and let him place it on top of it until another day; and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
The form of the forceps for cleaning is this.
Let the two ends be blunt, and let them be incident: so that a branch of the clavicle does not come out in them; and let the length of the two points be equal to the length of two handles equally.
But an error occurring in cleaning is this: because perhaps the whole inner skin escapes, or a part of it at the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you extend it from your hour with your fingernail before applying the dressing to the place; and cut it according to equality.
And if you are not able to hold it with your fingernail, then extract it with a hook, and cut it.
But if three days pass it, and what is under the rod remains swollen, apply a dressing; then let that be until the abscess sits hot, and flay it easily, and cut it according to what is prepared for you; and beware of the head of the rod. For it will not harm him.
Therefore treat it with what strengthens the wound from powders, which we have related in the treatise on powders; and if it is cut above measure from the skin, and reaches upwards, it will not harm him either.
Therefore treat it with what we have said until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Eight.
Concerning the extraction of retained urine in the bladder.
The retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction made from stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or flesh grown, and similar things from those which we have said from the methods of treatment in division.
And if urine is not discharged, and you see that its retention is due to a stone which is already in the neck of the bladder; then it is necessary that he be weak, bent over on his knees. Then let his feet be placed upon his back, and let him extend his belly from the upper part of his back, and let the weak one restrain his breath as much as possible for him. Therefore, with the neck of the bladder directed towards the expulsion of the stone to the rear: urine is discharged.
But if it is not discharged with that which we have said, and the matter is vehement upon the weak one: then it is necessary that you administer his extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be of silver, and let it be slender, smooth, concave like the quill of a bird in the subtlety of a ray, long in the likeness of one and a half palms; to which there be a slender cannula at its head.
And the method of the exit of urine through it is, that you take a doubled thread, and bind wool or tow at its end with a good ligature; and introduce the end of the thread into the lower part of the syringe: and cut off with forceps anything superfluous from the wool, but introduce it into the cannula with vehemence, like an elevator.
Then smear the syringe with oil, or butter, and egg white as well; and make the weak one sit upon a seat, and embrocate the bladder, and its member with moist oils, olive oil, and warm water.
Then introduce the syringe into the member with ease until it reaches the root of the member.
Then double it upwards from the part above the umbilicus.
Then press the syringe within it until it reaches the bladder, and let the weak one feel that he has already reached the empty thing; and this is not done according to this order unless because the passage in which it goes is one in which there is tortuosity.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For the vein will follow the wool.
Then draw it out, and make the vein run through the passage of urine; and repeat the syringe, and do not cease to do this until the bladder is emptied and the sick person finds relief.
Concerning the quality of washing out the bladder with a Clepshydra.
Chapter LIX.
When an ulcer occurs in the bladder, or blood freezes in it, or pus constricts in it, and there is a desire to distill waters and medicines into it: then let it be done with an instrument called a Clepshydra, and this is its form.
For it is deaf, that is, closed. And there are three holes in it, two on one side and one on the other side, as you see.
And the concave place, in which is that which drives it, let it be according to the amount which it will fill itself without addition, until when it is drawn with it something is attracted from the moistures; and when it is driven, with it it is expelled afar: according to what the clepshydra does, with which naphtha is projected, that is, Greek fire in battles of Mars.
When therefore you wish to project moistures into the bladder, insert the extremity of the clepshydra into the moisture, and draw with it that which drives it upwards, for the moisture is attracted into the concavity of the clepshydra.
Then insert its extremity into the urethra, according to what we have related in the syringe. Then drive the moisture with that which drives it; for the moisture arrives at the bladder immediately, so that the sick person feels it.
The form of a delicate enema by which the bladder is washed out.
Let it be made of silver or electrum: whose head is higher, similar to a small cucumber, and beneath it a groove into which a ligature may fall.
Then take the bladder of a ram and put into it the moisture with which you wish to irrigate the bladder. Then tie it between two folds with a strong ligature using a double thread, and heat that moisture a little over the fire.
Then insert the end of the syringe into the rod, then squeeze your hand over the bladder with the moisture with a decent constriction until the weakling feels that the moisture has already reached the bladder.
But if a bladder is not present for you, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle out of it, and pierce it through all its roundness. Then insert a strong thread into those holes as they revolve. Then gather the circle like a bundle of reeds is gathered: after you put into it what you wish from moistures, oils, and waters.
Then tie it into the instrument and do with it as you did with the bladder from expression with your hand until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On the Extraction of a Stone.
We have already said in the division the species of Stone, and its treatment with medicine, and I said the difference between a Stone generated in the kidneys and the bladder, and the signs of it all: and I said the Stone in which the operation falls with the hand and incision: and it is a Stone generated properly in the bladder: and that which is attached to the rod, and I am narrating that with manifest exposition and abbreviation.
Therefore, I say that a Stone generated in the bladder most often happens to boys.
And from its signs is that the urine coming forth from the bladder is similar to water in its subtlety, and there appears in it white sand, and the weakling rubs his rod, and labors with it, and often hangs down. Then he is erected, and comes forth with it the anus in many of them.
And healing is easy for children up to the age of fourteen. And it is difficult in the elderly. But in young people it is intermediate, in that which is between those things: and of the one whose stone is larger, healing is easier; and if small, it is the opposite of its care.
Therefore, when we come to healing, it is necessary first of all that we cleanse the sick person with an enema, which extracts all the dung that is in their intestines; for it sometimes hinders the discovery of the stone during examination.
Then let the sick person be taken with their feet and shaken, and moved downwards so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or jumps from a high place several times.
Then make him sit between your hands prepared, and his hands should be under his own hips so that the whole bladder becomes sloping downwards.
Then examine him and touch him externally.
If therefore you feel the stone in space, then hasten immediately with an incision over it.
But if it does not fall under your touch at all, then you must smear the index finger with oil of the left hand, if the patient is a boy, or the middle finger if he is a fully grown young man; and insert it into his anus, and inquire about the stone until it stands under your finger, and turn it gradually towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press on it with your finger and push it outwards towards the place where you desire the incision; and instruct an assistant to press the bladder with their hand, and instruct another assistant to extend the testicles with their right hand upwards; and with their other hand, to remove the skin which is under the testicles in the part where the incision is.
Then insert an incizing speculum, whose form is this. And find in it what is between the anus and the testicles, and not in the middle, to the side of the left natal: and let the incision be upon the stone itself, and your finger be in the anus: and let the incision be transverse, so that the incision externally be wide, and internally tight, according to the quantity so that the exit of the stone from it is not greater.
For perhaps the finger, which is in the anus, compresses the Stone at the incision, and it exits without difficulty.
And know that from a Stone which has angles and margins, therefore its exit becomes difficult because of that.
And from it is smooth, similar to a gland, and round: and its exit becomes easy.
Therefore to him who has angles and margins, add a fissure.
But if it does not exit thus, then it is necessary that you ingeniously work upon it: or you bind it with a bandage gently, whose end is like the moon, which binds upon the stone, and does not depart from it: or that you insert beneath it a subtle instrument, of curved extremity.
But if you are not able to do so upon it, then enlarge the opening a little. If something overcomes you from blood, cut it off with a cautery.
But if there are more than one stone, then first press the largest towards the mouth of the bladder. Then make an incision upon it. Afterwards press a small one after that, and similarly do if there are more than two.
But if it is very large, then ignorance is such that you make a larger incision upon it. For it happens to the weak one of two things, either that he dies, or there occurs to him a continuous distillation of urine: because the place does not consolidate at all.
But manage its expulsion until it exits: or ingeniously work upon fracturing it with forceps, until you extract it in pieces.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense, and aloe, and dragon's blood; and bind it up, and put upon it a cloth infused with oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: so that a hot poultice may be applied.
Then place the patient on their back upon their neck, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day. When therefore you loosen it, embrocate the place with water and much oil.
Then care for it with dyafinicon ointment, and basilicon ointment until it heals.
But if it should happen that a hot abscess is added to the wound, and corrosions, and things similar to them: such as if blood should congeal in the bladder, and urine be prevented from flowing from it; and this is known from the discharge of blood with the urine. Therefore introduce your finger into the wound, and draw out that blood. For if it remains, it provokes corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt; and oppose to it every kind of thing suitable for its healing until it heals.
And it is necessary throughout the whole course of treatment, that you bind together the two thighs and the groin, on account of the medicines which are placed upon the spot.
But if the stone is small and is in the urethra, and it is fixed in it, and the passage of urine is prevented: then care for it with that which I have recounted before you come to incision. Very often indeed you are excused from incision by this treatment, for I have already proved it.
And it is, that you should take a perforator made of alferic iron, which has this shape: a triangular point, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take thread and tie it with the penis below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the penetrating iron into the urethra with ease, until the perforator reaches the stone itself; and revolve the perforator with your hand in the stone gradually; and try to perforate it, until you make it penetrate from the other side.
For urine is then immediately discharged.
Then press your hand upon that which remains of the stone from the outer part of the urethra. For it itself is broken, and exits with the urine, and the sick person is healed.
But if this cure is not prepared for you, because of something that prevents it from him: then tie a thread below the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone in the urethra itself between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Afterwards untie the ligature, and clean the congealed blood which is made in the wound.
And the ligature below the stone is not necessary unless so that it does not return to the bladder; and the other ligature above unless so that when the thread is untied after the exit of the stone, the skin returns to its place, and the wound heals together; and because of this it is necessary for you when you tie the upper thread, that you elevate the skin upwards so that it returns at your completion, and the wound heals together as we said.
Chapter LXI.
Concerning the Extraction of a Stone in Women.
Few stones are generated in women.
But if it should happen to any of them a stone, then the cure is difficult, and is forbidden because of many ways.
One of these is: that the woman perhaps is a virgin.
And the second is, because you do not find a woman who will reveal herself to a physician when she is chaste, or among those having husbands.
Thirdly, because you do not find a woman who knows this art well, especially the operation with her hand.
And fourthly is the distance from the place of the stone; therefore it needs deep incision, and there is fear in that.
But if necessity provokes to that, then it is necessary that you take a female physician, well-knowing; and few are found.
But if you deprive her of these things, then seek a chaste physician, subtle, and present a female midwife well-taught in the matter of women, or a woman who inclines towards this part of the art; therefore make her present, and especially to her, that she do all that you command her from the inquiry about the stone.
First of all it is, that she look whether the woman is a virgin; then it is necessary that she insert a finger into her anus, and seek the stone.
If therefore she finds it, she shall constrict it under her finger, and then command the midwife to insert her finger into the vulva of the weak woman, and inquire over the stone, after she places her left hand upon the bladder, and compresses it with good compression.
If therefore she finds the stone, then it is necessary that gradually she move it from the orifice of the bladder to the lower parts with all her strength, until it arrives with it at the root of the thigh.
Then she shall cut above it at the opposition of the middle of the vulva at the root of the thigh, from whatever part it is prepared for her, and feels the stone in that part; and her finger is not removed from the stone, constricting under it: and let the incision be small at first. Then she shall insert a ray above that incision.
Then she shall insert a ray above that incision. If therefore she feels the stone, then let her add to the incision according to the proportion of what the stone goes out from it.
And know that there are many kinds of stone. For among these there is a small one and a large one, a smooth one and a rough one, a long one, and a round one having branches.
Therefore know its kind, so that it may be made known to you by this concerning that which you see.
If therefore blood overcomes you, then add ground zegi to the place, and hold it for one hour, until the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation until the stone comes out; and make sure you prepare for yourself from the instruments which I mentioned in the extraction of a stone from men so that you may be aided by them in your operation.
But if the flow of blood overcomes you and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery which has been cut: then place powder over the place and bind it with powders in a proper binding; and do not change this; and let the stone go, and do not extract it. For perhaps an infirm woman may die. Then care for the wound.
When therefore the sharpness of the blood subsides after days and the place festers, then return to your operation until the stone comes out.
Chapter LX II.
On the incision for watery hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but a collection of moisture in the sifac alba, which is under the skin containing the testicle; and it is called sifac.
And sometimes it is in its own groin, which nature prepared for it in the part where it swells until it appears; which is another testicle, and it is the skin of the testicle between the sifac alba, as we said; and this does not happen except rarely.
And the generation indeed of this hernia is from weakness that happens to the testicles; therefore this material flows down to them. And sometimes it happens from a blow upon the testicles.
And this moisture has many colors. For its color is either sloping towards yellowness, or it is bloody red, or fecal black, or it is watery white, and that is what is more.
And the signs by which it is known where there is a gathering of water are these. For if it is in the white sifac as we said, then the abscess is round in length a little like the shape of the letter O, and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture contains it from every side.
And if the moisture is in its own groin, then the abscess is round with a part of the letter O, and therefore a man judges that it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under the sense.
Therefore when you wish to know the color of the moisture, then experience the color of the moisture in the abscess with a square instrument, whose form preceded. According to that therefore which comes out in the trace of the instrument, judge concerning that which is between it.
When therefore we come to the treatment with iron, it is necessary that you advise the sick man, so that he be bled if it is possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then let him be laid on his back on a high thing a little, and put many cloths under him.
Then sit you on his left and order the attendant to sit at his right, who extends his rod to one of the two sides of the skin of the testicles, and to a part of the belly cavity.
Then take a broad scalpel and cut the skin of the testicles from the middle through the length, up to near the comb; and let the incision be according to the straightness of an equidistant line which divides the skin of the testicles, until it reaches the white sifac comprehending it; and flay it out, and observe, or beware from this, lest you cut it: and let your flaying be from the part which hangs with the egg more; and carefully scrutinize with the flaying according to the amount that is possible for you.
Then pierce the full bladder with a wide perforation and draw out all the water.
Then separate between the two lips of the incision with hooks and extend the bladder upward, and do not touch the skin enveloping the testicles; and incise the bladder in whatever way it is possible for you to incise it, either with its entirety or in parts, especially its thin side. For if you do not meticulously examine it, there is no safety in its incision lest the water return.
But if the external testicle proceeds from its skin during your operation: then when you have completed the incision of the bladder, return it. Then join the two lips of the skin of the testicles with suture.
Then care for it like other wounds until it heals.
But if you find the testicle corrupted from blackness or another cause, then it is necessary that you tie the vessels which are the suspender, fearing a flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with the suspender and extract the testicle; afterwards care for it as we have said.
But if the water is collected in two parts, then know that there are two branches; therefore cut off the other part according to what you did in the other equally.
And if it seems to you that the operation should be one, then do so; then wash away the blood. And insert into the incisions wool dipped in oil, or in rose oil; and place outside other wool which you have dipped in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles and observe the belly; and put double cloths on top of them, and they are poultices; and bind them on top with a bandage which has six according to this form.
Then take two cloths and take them and fill them with ground carminative according to this likeness; and let the ends be pressed from the elbow or damp wool; and join them together with a bandage on the upper arms: and let one of the ends receive above the anus up to the back, and let the second receive to the part of the breastbone; and let two ends above the two thighs below, and let the other two ends above the two groins, and let the whole bandage be joined on the upper arms.
And sometimes this wound is artificially treated with a cautery in the place of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a surgical cautery subtle and thin and cut the skin of the testicles with it; and it is ignited, as we have said: until when white pus appears containing water: then take a cautery, according to this shape.
Greek letters are assimilated.
Then pierce that pus with it and ignite it until all the moisture comes out.
Then extend the pus with hooks and flay it with a thin ignited cautery and cut it according to what is possible for you until you eradicate all of it.
And be careful that the fire does not touch it.
And know that this operation is safer and farther from bleeding and better than the operation with an incision.
But if it opposes you in the hour of incision, or in the hour of cautery a hot abscess, or a flow of blood, or anything else: then it is necessary that you cure what it opposes you with from it with the treatment which suits it, according to what has been said before.
But if he is weak and timid and does not await this operation because of its horror: then perform a perforation according to what I narrate that: and it is.
When it is confirmed to you with that which we said about the meanings, that the wound is watery: then it is necessary that you make the weak person sit on a raised seat.
Then pierce the abscess in the lower part of it with a wide speculum; and place the piercing according to the length of the body, and let the opening be wide until all the water runs out.
Then place a poultice on the place and bind it and let it so that the rest of the water runs out; then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is opposition to the exit of water at the hour in which you pierce it, and this is not so except because the white subcutaneous layer makes opposition at the opening of the wound and prevents the exit of water; then it is necessary that you introduce a pen into the wound, or an instrument with which water is extracted from dropsy patients, whose form precedes; or add a little in the operation of the wound.
