And know that water returns and gathers after six months, or around them. Therefore, when it gathers, then it is returned according to the same method, and the sick person will pass through his days.
Chapter 63.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy branches.
Know that in the section upon this branch, there is fear leading to destruction in many constitutions. Therefore it seems to me that its dismissal is saving from it.
And I remember the operation upon it and the nature of an abscess.
I say therefore that many abscesses occur in bodies, from which testicles are composed; and this happens from many causes, namely.
Either from acute or sharp superfluity which is poured out upon the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body and pain is not found in it; and the abscess is serious.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess whose color is dark brown, and there is no sensation to it.
And sometimes it happens from the nodulation of arteries and their inflation as has already preceded in its recollection; or from the inflation of veins.
That which is from them by inflation of arteries is recognized by the disposition of the abscess when you press it with your fingers. And it is not necessary for you to touch it completely.
That which is done by the inflation of veins, nothing is dispersed from it upon compression with the fingers.
And now it is possible that a section be made on this type and on the other flesh; and it is to cut the skin of the testicles. Then extend the testicle upward and extract it with white silk; and free the spermatic cord from the vessels and tie off the vessels; and cut the spermatic cord after you have freed it from every part of the parts of the testicle.
But if the testicle is consolidated with those flesh which are born, then it behooves you to draw out the testicle and cut into it.
And if there is adhesion between something from the spermatic cord or in that which is between the vessels: then it behooves you to free all that adhesion and cut into it with a round incision.
But if there is a birth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind: then it behooves you to cut into all of it and extract the testicle as we have said.
When therefore your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; and then care for it with the other care until it heals.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the Care of Branches which are with the Vine.
A hydrocele is a swelling enclosed by a certain involution, like a hernia, with softening of the testicles; and it makes difficult movement and exercise and walking upon the weak.
And there is fear in the treatment of this ailment because of that of which we have previously made mention.
But it is necessary for us to say that an operation should be done on it according to what the first ones did.
Therefore I say that it is necessary for you to make the weak one sit upon a raised seat. Then press the spermatic cord of the testicle downward. Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers, along with the veins that are near the penis; and let another attendant hold it and stretch it out with a vigorous extension.
Then make an incision with a broad, sharp scalpel in a transverse section directly on the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
Then remove the skin from all sides, as we have told you in the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Then fix a needle into them in which there is a double thread, and tie it in the first of the places where the veins occur; and tie it again at the last of them. Then cut them in a straight section in the middle according to the length of the body, and extract what is collected from damp, corrupted residues.
Then treat the wound with the care of the other wounds, in which you wish to generate pus, so that the vessels which nourish one of the testicles may fall away. For it is not bad through that.
But if it should happen to a vein with all the veins, then it is necessary that the testicles along with the veins be extracted; so that the testicle may not be deprived of nourishment because of the incision and waste away, or become charred, and there may not be pleasure from them.
Chapter LXV.
Concerning the care of branches of the intestine.
The cause of this branching is from a section which occurs on the intestines extended over the belly in the part of the groin out of the region of the belly.
Therefore the intestines descend from that rupture to one testicle. And this rupture is either from a section, or extension of it.
And these two kinds happen from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a shout, or the lifting of a heavy object, and similar things.
And the sign, when it is from an extension of the intestines. It happens gradually over a long time and does not happen suddenly: and it is an abscess equal towards the depth: because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it is when it is from the section of the sifach, because it causes to happen from its beginning intense pain suddenly: and it is a different abscess, apparent under the skin nearby: and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course to the exterior of the sifach.
And sometimes it exits with an intestine zircon: and this is called intestinal Ramex, and zircon: and sometimes it happens with windiness.
And sometimes feces runs into the intestine, and is retained there: and it is with this deadly danger to the sick person: because it causes severe pain to happen, and especially when it is pressed.
And in the treatment of these species of illness with iron there is fear: therefore it is necessary that caution be taken in this case.
And the method of operation is, that you instruct the sick person to reduce the intestine with his hand into the interior of its cavity, if it obeys return. Then he is laid back on his neck between your hands: and his legs are raised.
Then extend the skin which follows the skin above, and cut all the skin of the testicles lengthwise. Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to as you judge for opening the incision with them: and the incision should be according to the quantity, so that it is possible for an egg to come out from it.
Then scrape the siphak, and what are under the skin of the testicles, until when the white sifach is uncovered, hard from every side: then introduce your index finger into it which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white sifach, which is under the skin, and the second sifach, and break with it the adhesion which is in the back part of the testicle.
Then move with your right hand to the inner skin of the testicles; and with this extend a white bandage upwards with your left hand; and raise the testicle with the bandage to the part to be cut; and instruct the assistant to extend the testicle upwards; and you loosen the adhesion that is behind after the completion of the solution.
And search with your fingers so that nothing from the intestine remains uncoiled within the hard white bandage. But if you find anything from it, then press it down towards the lower belly.
Then take a needle with a thick thread made of licorice from ten threads; and insert it at the last bandage, that is, the tunic which is under the skin of the testicles following the incision; then cut off the ends that protrude from the thread until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over others with a shape like a cross; and tie them with the bandage which we mentioned, because it is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligament, until none of the vessels that nourish them can send anything to them: so that a hot abscess does not occur from there. And place another ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it by less than two fingers. And after these two ligatures, let out from the bandage which is under the skin of the testicles a quantity equal to the size of a finger; and cut the rest entirely according to its roundness, and remove the testicle from it.
Then cauterize in the lower part of the skin of the testicles with an incision from which blood and pus flow; as we narrated in what has preceded.
Then apply wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound; and administer the ligature which we have narrated. And sometimes the white bandage which we mentioned is cauterized after its incision fearing blood flow. This very often happens. And release the ligature, until it falls by itself.
But if its falling is delayed, then embrocate it with warm water and place upon it that which we have said, until it falls.
Then care for the wound with the remaining care of wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter Sixty-Six.
Concerning a windy branch.
In this branch, I have not seen anyone who dared to operate on it with iron.
And first indeed they said that it is done as we have narrated in the branch which is with the vine. Which is, that you should ligate the vessels after division above them with ease below: then cut in the middle and care for it finally with what opens it up, until the vessels fall: and care for the wound according to what we have said, until it is healed.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
Concerning a disruption which occurs in the groins.
When a rupture occurs in the groins, as we have said, and the place protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles: and if it does descend, it is little, and returns in all hours: but if time prolongs with that, a fissure is added in the groin, until the intestines descend into the groin.
And this happens from the extension of the groin which is in the groins, as we have said. Which is because the groin is extended: then it softens and the place becomes inflamed and protrudes.
And its care is with a cautery, as its previous narration preceded.
And sometimes it is cured with iron, according to this method: and it is, that the sick person should lie on their back between your hands. Then cut the inflamed and protruding place of the rupture by a width according to the measure of three fingers.
Then bind the ligature and, what are under the skin, until a white vein is revealed, which follows it underneath the skin.
Then take a needle and place it upon the prominent spot from the vein and press it deeply into the belly.
Then mark two prominent spots above the extremity of the needle from the vein and connect them with a suture, then extract the extremity of the needle.
And do not cut the vein at all, nor pull the testicle, or anything else, as I have made you know in the treatment of hemorrhoids.
Then treat it with wound care. When therefore you cut the threads, clean it and restore the wound until it consolidates.
For the vein contracts and no eminence of a rupture is added.
And cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to safety.
Chapter LXVIII.
On the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Very often the skin of the testicles is softened in certain men, on account of which the flesh between them is softened and its appearance is spoiled.
Therefore it is necessary for him who is asked in its treatment to make the sick man lie on his back and cut all the softened skin upon the natural skin.
Then bring together the two lips with a suture, and if you wish, sew the excess of softened skin first with three or four sutures and make the suture firm.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat it with the care of other wounds until it is healed and the threads fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
On Castration.
Know that castration is excommunicated in our law and because of that it would be permitted for me not to remember it in this book of mine, and there is no need to remember it except for two ways.
One of them is that it should be in the knowledge of a physician when questioned about it, and to know its cure for whomever it happens to.
And another way is that we often need the castration of certain animals for our own uses through it, such as rams, boars, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore I say that castration is done in two ways. Or by abrasion. Or by fissure and incision.
In that which is done by abrasion, the way of the operation is: that we make the animal sit in warm water until its testicles are softened and relaxed and hang down; then abrade them with your hand until they are dissolved and do not appear to the touch.
In castration by fissure and incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament and cut above the whole testicle with one incision until when two testicles proceed; then incise them after you have skinned both, and do not leave anything above them except a fine silk thread which is upon the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by abrasion; because abrasion perhaps leaves something from the testicles, wherefore the animal desires copulation.
Then care for the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
Concerning the care of a hermaphrodite in two ways.
A hermaphrodite is from males in two ways. One of them is because it appears in it that a space follows, or in the skin of the testicles, in that which is between the two testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva, in which there are hairs: and sometimes urine runs from that which is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one species, and it is the vulva of a woman above the pubic bone just like the testicles of a man, small indeed, prominent outwardly; one of which is like the penis of a man, and two like the two testicles.
And the treatment of the three species, two from men, and one species from women, is: because it is necessary that you cut away the added flesh until the impression of them escapes. Then treat them with the care of other wounds.
But in the second species of men, from which urine flows, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment at all.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On the incision of a tenting and prominent flesh in the vulva of women.
A tenting may perhaps be added upon the natural part until its appearance becomes unsightly and foul; and sometimes it magnifies in certain women so much that it expands like in men, and reaches even to copulation.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold the excess of the tenting with your hand or with a hook and make an incision; and do not stop in the incision, especially deep at the root; so that bleeding does not occur.
Then treat it with the care of other wounds until it heals.
But the flesh that arises is flesh that is born in the opening of the womb until it fills it; and perhaps it protrudes outward according to the likeness of a tail; and because of this some of the ancients call the ailment tailed. Therefore it is necessary that we incise it just as we incise a tenting and treat it until it heals.
On the treatment of alratica, which is an imperforate vulva of a woman.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
Alratica is when the vulva of a woman is not perforated, or when there is a small opening. It is either natural or accidental.
It is accidental from a preceding illness and indeed that happens either from added flesh born of the flesh, or a subtle or thick suture; and it happens either in the depth of the womb, or its sides, or its upper part, or its lower part; and it prevents coition and impregnation and childbirth, and perhaps prevents menstruation.
And knowledge of it is gained through the examination of a midwife if nearby opilation is apparent.
But if it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the opilation is from a subtle suture and is near the two labia, then hurry and break it apart.
And it is as if you should place poultices on the two labia.
Then put the index fingers of both hands together and let the woman be on her back with her legs spread apart. Then extend the two labia with force until the subtle suture is broken and the opilation is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil and put it on the spot; and let the woman come together every day so that the place does not consolidate with other juice.
But if the suture is thick and coarse then it is necessary to cut it with a broad spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
But if the opilation is from flesh born then suspend it with hooks and cut into it; and let there be with you medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and thus a mixture with albumen and egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal quickly, let her have it for several days; and administer a licorice from dry linen, then let the rest be cured until it is healed.
And sometimes other flesh will be born within the womb; it is therefore necessary that they be cut according to this method: but for a cancerous abscess, as an abscess which is cancerous and within the womb, do not oppose iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, fistulas, and red boils in the vulvas of women.
Hemorrhoids are indeed the opening of the veins, until much blood constantly flows from them.
Therefore, when hemorrhoids become inflamed, they turn into fistulas; and I have already said in the division of their kinds, and their signs; and I will say here which of them receive a cure, and which do not.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids which are in the opening of the uterus, and do not appear to the senses, then there is no cure for them with iron.
But those which are from them in the opening of the uterus upon which the sense falls, are those that are cured.
Therefore it is necessary that you introduce the woman into a cold house. Then extend the fistulas with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then powder over them at the flow of blood from one of the cutting powders without modification: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and thus, and similar to them.
Then press her feet against the wall for one hour. Afterwards, place her in cold water.
But if the flow of blood persists: then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, and pomegranate rinds, and galls, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it upon her back.
If, therefore, the blood is cut off with that, it is good; and if not, make the windlasses adhere to the back, and nipples, without scarification.
Then take wool and submerge it in the juice of tamarind, or the juice of arugula, or the juice of the branches of a plant called alaul, that is, aglant, Pontic wine, and make it adhere to the part.
Therefore, when the abscess is quiet, care for the place with ointments until it heals.
The bartholin's gland, however, is red and resembles a rosehip and has a rough appearance.
Therefore it is necessary that you cut out what appears from it, according to what I said about the thelil equally; and care for it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Fourth.
Concerning the perforation of a fistula which occurs in the womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stone abscesses, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is, herpes, aestiomemus, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and thelil, and hot abscesses.
And I have indeed already said all these diseases and their kinds, and their signs, and their cures in the division; it is necessary that I say in this treatise hot abscesses which occur in the womb, when they are from abscesses which collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is made with an iron instrument.
Therefore it is necessary for you to consider. Then if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is sharp with percussion, and burning, and fever; and you see it reddening; if numbness falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation; and care for it with that which helps towards maturation, until when the sharpness of the occurrences subsides.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit upon a seat which has two seats, and recline, or lean upon her back, and bring together her feet to the lower part of her belly; and let her hips be spread out, and put her arms under her knees, and tie each one with a suitable ligament to it.
Then let the midwife sit on her right side, and administer the instrument with which the opening of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you fix it with a Probe deep in the matrix of the woman: so that what enters from the instrument is not more than the depth of the matrix, and injures the infirm.
But if the instrument should be more than deep, then it is necessary that you place puluillos upon the covering of the deep matrix: so that entry of the whole instrument into the depth of the matrix is prohibited: and it is necessary that he put a verticel which runs in the instrument from the upper side; and that he hold the instrument, and that the minister turn the verticel until the deep matrix is opened.
Therefore when the Exitura appears and is touched by the hand and is soft and pliable: then it is necessary that you cut it at its head with a wide speculum.
Therefore when the blood is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place a licorice root submerged in rose oil: or green oil, in which there is some astringency; and place the licorice, and extract the incision in the depth of the matrix.
And place externally upon the matrix, and upon the comb clean wool submerged in water, in which apples have been boiled.
Then let it be treated after two days with an ointment suitable for it, until it heals.
And when the matrix and wound are washed, so that it is cleansed with water and honey; then with water in which lily root or long aristolochia has been boiled: then return to the ointment.
But if the wound should be hidden inside in the matrix, then it is necessary that you prohibit its cure with iron, but rather treat it with what we said in the division.
Chapter LXXV.
Doctrine of Obstetrics, how living embryos are treated according to an unnatural figure having emerged.
It is necessary for a midwife to know first the figure of natural childbirth.
Therefore, by these signs it is known that when you see a woman pressing down towards her lower parts, and she desires to smell the air, and relief comes over her concerning what is upon her from childbirth, and she hastens the exit of the embryo: then know that this birth is according to the natural figure, and its exit is upon its head, and secondarily with it: or suspended, or hanging from the navel.
Therefore when you see these signs, it is necessary that her belly be pressed so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends upon its head, the second one descends with it, and it is cleansed from those superfluities by a good cleansing.
But that which is besides this birth is unnatural, unpraiseworthy.
For sometimes the fetus exits upon its feet, and its body exits before its head, and its feet and its hands together, or one of its feet; or its head and hands exit; or its belly exits; and perhaps it turns upon its neck, and similar things from unpraiseworthy figures.
Therefore it is necessary for a midwife to be prudent, subtle in all these things, and to beware of error and deception; and I am showing every figure from them, and how the mind is in it, and let it be signified by that, and let it stand upon it.
When the fetus exits upon its head by its natural exit, and it becomes vehement upon the woman, and childbirth becomes difficult for her, and you see her strength already weakened: then make her sit upon a seat, or let women hold her; and vaporize the wool of her with decocted fenugreek, and with moist oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and find the second one with it, and cut it with her nail until what is in it runs out from moisture; and press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then she must be given an enema made from fenugreek mucilage and sesame oil.
Then order her to compress after the enema, and make her snort when she is compressed, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will immediately come out.
But if two hands of the fetus first emerge, it is necessary that you return them gradually with ease. But if it does not obey upon entering, then place the woman upon a seat and raise her feet upward; then strike the seat upon the ground, and let the woman be held so that she does not fall at the impact.
But if the hands do not come out and the fetus dies, then cut them, and extract the remaining fetus; and bind his hand with cloth, and extract it equally, for he will come out.
Delivery of an embryo over its feet. When the fetus comes out on its own feet, it is necessary to push them upward; then gradually turn the fetus; afterwards equalize it.
Then let him take one of its feet and extend it gradually.
When they come out as far as the hips, then order her to compress and make her snort when she is compressed, for it will come out if God wills.
And if it does not come out with what we have related, then return the fetus gradually until you place it according to its natural form. For then it will come out with an easy exit.
But if it is prevented with all that we have related, then take fenugreek mucilage and sesame oil, and dissolved gum; and strike the whole in a mortar with good percussion; then smear it upon the vulva of the woman, and below her belly. Then make her sit in warm water until the hypocondria follows.
Therefore when you see that her lower parts are already warmed, then give her a suppository of myrrh and insert it. When therefore she has held the suppository for one hour, then make her sit upon a seat. Then make her belch and compress her mouth and nose, and press the lower part of her belly with a gentle compression. For the fetus will come out immediately.
The delivery of the fetus upon the knees and hands. When it comes forth according to this form, then he should gradually insert his hands. Then the woman should be turned onto her neck, and her feet should dangle downwards; and she herself should lie upon a bed, and intertwine her hands. Then press upon her belly gradually. Then draw out what is under her feet from the cushions until she is prepared in the lower part. But if the fetus does not come forth, then take hold of her feet and move them with a forceful motion. Then press what is above the hypocondria gradually until the fetus ascends to the upper parts. Then let him insert his hands and equalize the fetus gradually; and instruct the woman to compress herself until the fetus comes forth.
The delivery of the fetus hanging laterally from the hands. He should apply himself to reducing his hand. But if he cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
But if she cannot walk, then make her lie upon a bed; and strike her legs with a forceful concussion.
Then administer the unguent which you have prepared from gums.
But if she receives her labor, then make her sit upon a seat. Then strive in the reduction of the hand, and equalize the swelling according to the natural shape; and make the woman snore, and order her to compress herself until it comes out.
The exit of the fetus over its neck, with hands spread out, and its face is towards the back of its mother. It is necessary that the midwife hold the hand of the fetus, then turn it gradually, and equalize it with ease.
When, therefore, it is equalized, then place upon its vulva what we have described made of oil and gums.
Then order her to move gradually. When therefore you have done this, then make her sit upon a seat, and order her to lean towards her right side. Then order her to compress herself; and make her snore when it is pressed.
The exit of the prepared fetus over the side. When it descends to the orifice of the matrix according to this manner with the second membrane, then split the second membrane with your nail until it is evacuated from moisture.
If, therefore, it hangs over the face of the fetus and its neck, then cut it from the umbilicus so that it is not retained, and dies.
Then press the fetus towards the inside until you place it as is necessary; and order the woman to compress herself several times, for it will indeed exit by an easy way.
The exit of twins, or many fetuses. Know that twins very often are born; and sometimes three are born, and four. However, that is extraordinary, and rarely happens.
Five indeed is against nature and they do not live entirely; and it contains them, although they are multiplied, with one second membrane; and they are separated through a siphon, distinguishing the tied part in the umbilicus of each of them.
And sometimes they exit as does a single fetus by natural birth, easily; and they exit by an inglorious outlet, as we have recounted.
And if some of them are difficult at birth, and they reach what we said about unnatural figures, then take care of them as we have recounted; let there be a subtle midwife, and let her do what she does with ease and slowness, and let her contrive according to every figure with that which is prepared for her by wit leading to safety.
A remembrance of that which is formed in the womb from embryos, and are aborted.
Sometimes one is formed in the womb, and two, and three, and four, and five, and six, and seven, and more than ten; and it has already been verified by me that a woman aborting emitted seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
Which was done by the measurement of the glorious sage.
Therefore all this ought to be in the knowledge of Obstetrics. For perhaps it may happen upon her like that: let her therefore oppose him with what is necessary
Chapter LXXVI.
Of the Extraction of a Dead Fetus.
When you care for a dead fetus as I said in the division from medication, and it does not exit, and you are forced to use surgery with your hand, then it is necessary that you consider.
If therefore the woman is of sound constitution, and she is not with any of the accidents from which her loss is feared: then it is necessary that the woman be laid on her back upon a bed, and let her head be inclined downwards, and let her legs be elevated; and let the attendants hold her from two sides, or let her be tied to the bed so that her body is not pulled out at the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with wet oils with the mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and flaxseed.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and gums; then let her introduce it into the womb easily. Then let her seek with it a place in which to fix hooks from the fetus, and suitable places for that.
Consider then if the fetus is prepared to descend upon its head; then fix hooks into its eyes, or into its neck, or into its mouth, or into its palate, or under its beard, or into its collarbone, or into places near the ribs, and under its hypochondria.
But if the descent is upon its feet, then into its breastbone, and its middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand; and place the curvature of it in that which is between the fingers of her left hand; and introduce it with her hand easily, and fix the hook in one of those places which we have said, until it reaches an empty thing.
Then let her fix another hook opposite it, or a third if necessary; so that there is equal contraction and it does not lean to one side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let there not be extension according to straightness only, but rather let it be moved to every part so that its exit is relieved; just as it is done when a tooth is eradicated.
And it is necessary in that which is between it, that the extension be softened. And if a part is retained from it, then the midwife must anoint some fingers with oil and introduce them from the side, so that she revolves what is retained with them.
If then a part of the fetus goes out, then let the hooks be changed to another place which is higher; do this until the whole fetus comes out.
If then a hand goes out before the others, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction; then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped over it. Then extract it until the whole thing comes out, cut it either at the junction of the sole or at the elbow; and thus you must do it on one hand and on both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or constriction occurs at its exit, or there is water collected in its head: then it is necessary that you insert a Spatumile with spines between your fingers and bore through the head with it and extract the water; or break it up with an instrument called Almisdac: whose form comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which follows.
Do the same if the fetus is of a large-headed nature, then you must cut through the algengematu: or extract it with a misdach, as we have said. Then draw out the bones with forceps.
But if the head comes out, and is constricted at the fork: then bore it, until the moisture which is in the chest is poured out.
But if it does not become entangled, nor yield to coming out: then cut the head into pieces as much as is possible for you.
But if there is an abscess below the belly, or there is dropsy in it: then you must pierce it until the moisture runs out of it.
But if the fetus is on its feet, then its attraction is easy and its direction to the mouth of the womb is slight.
But if it becomes constricted at the belly or chest: then you must pull it out with a cloth which you have wrapped around your hand: and bore the belly or chest until what is in them runs out.
But if the remaining limbs are taken away and the head returns: then let the midwife introduce her left hand. Therefore if the mouth of the womb is open, let her introduce her hand deep into the womb and seek the head with it and draw it up to the mouth of the womb with her fingers: then let her insert one hook or two hooks in it and draw it out with them.
If the mouth of the uterus is constricted due to a hot abscess which has occurred in it: then it is not necessary for him to labor over it, rather he ought then to administer an effusion of humid and fatty things, and apply poultices, and sit in waters which soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side, then it is possible that it may be equalized; then let what we said about the living fetus be administered.
If that is not possible, then it shall be cut into pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary that you not leave anything from the placenta inside at all.
Therefore when you have completed your operation then administer what we said about hot abscesses which occur in uteruses.
If a flow of blood should occur, then let her descend into astringent waters and administer the remaining care.
And I myself have indeed already seen a woman, in whose belly a fetus had died; then she became pregnant again with another, afterward that fetus also died. It therefore happened to her after a long time that an abscess formed in her navel, and it swelled up until it opened and began to discharge pus.
I was therefore called for her care and treated her for a long time; and the wound did not heal.
I therefore put on it some poultices of strong attraction, and a mouth came out of the place. Then several days passed for her, and another mouth came out. I therefore wondered from that point, since there was no mouth in her belly.
I therefore thought that they were from the bones of the dead fetus.
I therefore inquired into the wound and extracted many bones, with the woman being in better dispositions; and she has now lived thus for a long time, and sends out little pus from the place.
And I did not include this here as something foreign unless because there is knowledge in it and help for that which the craftsman physician administers with his hand from healing.
Chapter LXXVII.
On the Forms of Instruments Necessary in the Extraction of a Fetus
The form of a Whorl by which the uterus is opened.
Here below is the form of a Strictor, as it is found in the books, to which two whorls are equally fitted at the ends of two pieces of wood. However, these whorls ought to be more delicate than the rest of the Strictor and they should be made either from ebony or boxwood.
And the width of all the wood should be about two fingers, and the edge of each of the two is about one finger wide, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which have already been fixed to both, whose length is half a palm and a little more; and the width of each is about two fingers or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the uterus so that it opens with them when you turn the two whorls.
Another form similar to that one, more delicate and lighter.
It should be made of ebony or boxwood wood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at the end of it there should be two additions, as you see here above; and the length of each addition from them is about a palm; and the width of each is two fingers.
Therefore, when you wish to open the uterus with it, then have the woman sit on a bed with her feet dangling, opening what is between her thighs. Then insert these two joined additions into the uterus; and you hold the lower part of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with an instrument, as you do with forceps, equally according to the amount which you wish from the opening of the mouth of the matrix, until the midwife does what she wants.
The form of another Vertigo than the ancients said The Compelling Form by which the fetus is impelled.
The form of Almisdac, by which a large head is pressed, and it is assimilated to forceps, which have teeth at the extremity, as you see.
And sometimes it is made oblong, like forceps according to this form, as you see, which has teeth like the teeth of a saw, by which it is cut and pressed.
The form of Uncini having one spine.
The form of Uncini having two spines.
The form of Spatula having two extremities for cutting off the head.
The form of two Spatulas also spread apart.
All these instruments are exercised, and the more their kinds are multiplied and they are prepared by a craftsman, the quicker they are for their operation, and the higher they are esteemed among men, because of their quantity.
Therefore, do not despise any instrument from them, but have it prepared by you. For the necessity of them is inexcusable.
Chapter 78.
Concerning the Extraction of a Second Child.
When a second child is retained during labor, then it is necessary that you instruct the weak woman to retain her breath. Then have her groan when she is strained; and compress your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it exits through this, it is good.
If not: then take a pot and perforate a hole in the lid; and place herbs which open the mouth of the matrix therein, such as calamus, rue, anise, chamomile, dill, cassia lignea, and centaury; take all these herbs or some of them, and submerge them with water, and place them over fire.
Then place a reed tube over the hole of the crucible pot, and the other end into the mouth of the mold; and hold it until the vapor reaches the concavity of the mold.
Then make it snort, as we said. For then it will come out easily.
But if it remains after this treatment and does not come out: then order the midwife to submerge her hand in oil of sesame, or hollyhock mucilage. Then let her introduce it into the vulva and seek the secundina with it. When she has found it, then let her take hold of it and gradually extend it.
But if it is attached deep within the mold, then let her introduce her hand, as we have described, until she finds the secundina and gradually extracts it according to its straightness so that the mold does not fall due to strong attraction; indeed, it must be shifted with ease to the right and left sides: then add to the amount of attraction. For it then obeys and is preserved and comes loose from its adhesion.
But if the mouth of the mold is constricted, as we have already said: treat it with snorting and from the pot's medication with herbs.
But if it does not come out with all that we have said, then be careful not to tire yourself in the repetition of the extraction.
But it is necessary that what comes out from its mouth bind it to the woman's thigh. Then give her an enema with tetrapharmacon ointment. For it will rot it after a few days and dissolve and come out.
But when it rots, a bad smell rises from it to the stomach and head and harms a weak person. Therefore, it is necessary that suitable fumigations be administered for this.
Some ancients, however, have already tried fumigation with watercress and dried figs.
The form of the instrument with which a woman is fumigated during the retention of secundines and menses.
Let it be made of glass like an alembic, or of lead; and let the wide end be placed over the fire; and let the fumigation be placed on top of prunes; let her hold it until that fumigation is consumed; and repeat another.
Chapter LXXIX.
On the treatment of an unperforated anus.
Many boys are born with their anuses unperforated, which have already been sealed by a delicate suture. Therefore, the midwife must perforate it with her finger; and if not, she must perforate it with a sharp spatulum; and let her beware that the laceration does not touch it.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil on top of it. Then heal it with ointments until it is healed.
But if you are afraid that it will be sealed, then place a lead cannula in the opening for many days and remove it when the boy wants to defecate.
And it sometimes happens that the anus is also sealed from the consolidation of a wound or an abscess. Therefore, it must be cut and then treated with what we have said from its treatment and lead.
Chapter LXXX.
On the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower part.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part are nodulations and swellings that occur near the anus externally; or in the space of one part; and there is one fistula, or several.
When that nodulation becomes old, it opens, and watery moisture, white or thin pus, flows from it.
And sometimes these fistulas are penetrating. Therefore penetrating wounds are recognized by what comes out through the discharge, and flatulence, when the weak person uses the discharge; and perhaps a worm may come out of them.
And fistulas are from them when they are penetrating to the bladder, and as far as the urethra. And they are penetrating as far as the hip joint, and as far as the tailbone.
But by which a penetrating fistula to the anus is known, also from a non-penetrating one, is that you insert your index finger into the anus; and insert a slender probe into the fistula from metal, or iron: when there is no crookedness.
But if there is crookedness in it, then insert into it a slender probe from lead, or a horsehair, until you feel the probe, or hair on your finger.
But if you do not feel it thoroughly, nor does anything come out of the orifice from discharge, nor flatulence, nor a worm as I said; then know that it is non-penetrating: hurry therefore to treatment.
And when a fistula is penetrating to the bladder, or to the urethra; then its indication is urine exiting from it, and its prohibition from this so that the place may consolidate with medicines.
But if it is penetrating as far as the hip joint, or to the acetabulum; then its sign is that the probe reaches there, if there is no crookedness in it as far as the opening; and the discovery of pain in the surrounding area, and that pus always comes out from it; and that medication does not work on it, nor does it consolidate with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all: and the treatment of them is toil and vanity for him, from foolish physicians, upon whom it is performed.
But in that which is not penetrating, and not old, healing is hoped for with a cautery; according to what I tell you, and I have already proven it; and it is that the weak person lie between your hands on his back; and raise his legs up to above his hips, and let his hips be inclined toward his belly. Then insert a probe of lead, or air, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
But if he feels it weak around the anus, then it is necessary that you insert your index finger into the anus.
Therefore, if you feel the probe has already penetrated through itself, beyond which you feel between your finger and it, or flesh; then you will know for certain that it is penetrating; therefore do not labor in it, because it is not its cure, as we have said.
However, they said that the flesh is healed in some men, and from the medication from which help is expected, it is, to ignite a subtle cautery according to the width of the fistula, as preceded, and insert it ignited into the fistula until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat that twice, or three times, until you know that all those added filtered flesh, which are similar to a bird's quill, have already been burned.
Then treat it with linens wrapped in butter until those burned flesh come out; then treat it with ointments that generate flesh and consolidate.
Therefore if it heals, it is good.
And if not, then it never heals with any other treatment.
And if the probe comes out and does not reach your finger which is in the anus, and there is a thick membrane of flesh or sifac between it and the probe: and you see the fistula in that which follows the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will cut with the probe what is in the fistula until the cutting reaches where the extremity of the probe reaches, and the probe is freed and falls.
Then cleanse those filtered flesh which are assimilated to a bird's quill so that nothing remains from them.
If blood, however, overcomes you and dissolves the connection between you and your operation so that you cut those flesh parts: then it is better to do what is swifter and more helpful, to cauterize it with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Because cautery with fire brings together good qualities, burning of those added flesh parts, cessation of blood, and drying of humors.
Then care for it by wrapping it with linen soaked in butter or with crushed stalks until the place discharges pus and those burnt flesh parts go forth into the pus.
Then care for that wound with hardening ointments, producing hard flesh; and there are medicines in which are astringency and drying.
For when they are restored, then they are already healed, and you need not fear repetition.
But if the fistula has already reached as far as the depth of the anus and is elongated by excess body: then insert your finger into the anus and inquire with it.
If therefore you feel a test and between you and it there is a veil of linen or flesh and it is close to penetration: then there is no operation upon that except according to the way of trust and hope.
Which is because one of three ways is administered upon it, either to cauterize it itself as we said or to cut it until you reach its cavity so that it is possible for you to insert linen and healing from next to it. And do not proceed much with the cutting so that you do not fall into the muscle containing the anus and it happens upon a weak outcome of evacuation without will. Then care for it as we said.
Perhaps it will be healed or you lengthen the veil and clean it after you have dried it close to the anus as we said either with a probe or with another instrument of sharp extremity. Then clean those filtered flesh parts which are in the incision, as far as you can with careful examination.
Then try the restoration of the whole wound with the incision and the upper opening where it is consolidated; and a fistula will remain open on the inside part near the anus, and it will be easier for the sick person.
And sometimes a fistula is made in this way; and it is when you insert a probe into the fistula, which is on the side of the anus near the surface of the body with skin and the margin of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like an awl, that is, prepared leather, according to this form. And insert into it a thread wrapped from five threads or so. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its concavity.
If therefore it is penetrating into the inner margin of the anus nearby, then draw out the thread from that hole: so that you insert your finger into the anus and draw out the end of the thread, and join the two ends together: and tighten both, and leave it for one day or two days: and the more the thread falls into the flesh, tighten it well until those pieces of flesh between the two ends of the thread fall off; then care for the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating: then make it penetrate by any means that is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For it is not fitting for you to do that because of tearing, so that you do not fall into it.
Then do with it as we said until it is healed.
The form of a sharp-pointed speculum with which the fistula is divided, whose curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp so that it does not fall into what is not necessary as you see.
Chapter Eighty-One.
On the suture of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision; and care for fissures.
There are hemorrhoids according to two types. For they are either within the anus, like bladders of charcoal or prunes; or as if they themselves are grains of a pomegranate. And from them there are small ones, and large ones: and blood flows from them always, and is one, and are many. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
But those which are outside the anus are according to most few in moisture, from which runs watery fluid, or a little blood with constant flow; and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is within the anus, is that you order the sick person to strain; and it should be squeezed until the anus protrudes, and hemorrhoids appear to you. Therefore suspend them with hooks; or hold them with your fingernail. Then cut into them at their roots.
But if hooks do not hold them because of their moisture and softness; then take them with a rough cloth or a swab from double wool, and pull them out with your fingers. Then cut into them, and sprinkle over them after the incision certain hot powders so that they will serve as a cautery for them. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then care for them with the remaining treatment until they are healed.
But if the anus does not yield to protrusion, then give the sick person an enema in which there is a little mordant, so that it washes with what is in the anus, and yields to protrusion quickly when the sick person strains.
But if hemorrhoids are external from the anus, then their matter is light, easy; and it is as you take them with your fingernail or take them with a hook, and cut into them. Then care for them with what we have said until they are healed.
And whoever recoils from cutting with iron, then it is necessary that you administer a suture to them according to this method. Take an unraveled thread and insert it into the needle. Then draw the thread upwards through the needle and make it penetrate with the point at its root on one side; and wind the two ends of the thread around the lower part of the needle so that they are parallel to each other, and tighten the thread firmly with a strong knot. Then tie off the thread and remove the needle.
Do this with all the threads and leave one of them untied, so that excess blood may run from it.
Then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or carrot oil on top of the wound and put it over the area; and instruct the patient to rest. Then leave him until it falls off.
When therefore the threads fall off, care for them with ointments and the rest of the treatment until they are healed.
On the Treatment of Fissures. Very often they occur from drying of excrement and constriction of nature.
Therefore when they become chronic and medicine is not effective in them; then it is necessary that you scrape them with the point of a scalpel or with your fingernail until they become moist, and remove the top layer which prevents them from consolidating. Then care for them until they consolidate as is necessary.
But if they do not consolidate; then repeat them with a more vigorous scraping than the first until they bleed profusely and are flayed; then care for them until they are healed.
On the Treatment of Hemorrhoids, Both Inverted and Non-Inverted, and Threads, as Well as Ants.
Chapter Eighty-Two.
Know that a hemorrhoid is nothing but a round knot according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail; and it occurs throughout the entire body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes; and pain results from them upon walking.
Therefore it must be cut what is around the clavicle, and held with forceps or a hook, and eradicated from its root.
Then care for that; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
From the ash-tree however there is one small eminence, rough, filtered, round above the surface of the body. And it often happens in the hands of children.
And the treatment for it is, to extend the ash tree and cut it; or tie it with silk thread; or with a hair until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what was previously mentioned with fire or with a sharp medicine; and now inverted ash trees are eradicated, and non-inverted ones with the instrument which I will say.
An ant however is also a small eminence, filtered, thick, above the surface of the body, extending deep; and when it finds cold, it feels like an ant bite therein, and this happens throughout the whole body; and most of it occurs in the hands.
And its treatment is, to take a cannula from a goose feather, or a vulture quill strong; and place that cannula upon the ant, until the circle of the cannula contains its parts. Then revolve your hand with the cannula until you cut what is around the ant; and place it deep into the flesh and eradicate the ant from its root.
And if you wish, do this with an air or iron cannula, according to this form.
Let the upper part of the cannula be a loose winding, drawing upwards, so that the apprehension thereof and its revolution is relieved upon the finger.
And if you wish, cauterize the ant according to what was previously mentioned in the cauterization of the ash tree in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Concerning the forms of instruments which are used for fistulas in diseases of the anus, and rupture of the abdomen, and colic.
Sometimes an enema is made of silver, or of brass, or of cast bronze, or hammered bronze.
And sometimes it is made of these instruments small and large, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore, that which is administered in the healing of very young children should be small; and those whose anuses are weak, or painful, so also their enemas.
A large enema.
The tube of it, that is, the upper head in which the bladder is tied, should be wide according to this manner: and let there be a distinction so that the bladder is tied as you see, and its lower extremity which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt, that is, not open, smooth, very slender, drawing toward subtlety; and on one of its sides there shall be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And let the width of the hole be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little thicker; and let the bladder in which the medicines are placed be from the bladder of an animal; or from sheepskin parchment, which shall be according to the form of a small wineskin: the root of which shall be one palm.
And let it be pierced with many holes as it is revolved, and the space which is between every hole shall be the thickness of a finger. Then insert into those holes a strong thread from raw silk, which shall be from ten threads or about that, and combine it with a bladder like that of a wineskin. When therefore the bladder is placed in it, tighten at the head of the enema between two distinctions with a strong constriction. Then administer the enema with it.
And this is the form of the circle of bladders here depicted, and the holes.
The quality of giving an enema with it is to fill a syringe or bladder with medicine that has been warmed according to the amount the sick person can tolerate; and be careful that it is not very hot nor very cold.
Then the sick person is laid on their back and raises their legs upwards; and put something under them, that is, leather on which people lie, or a sheet.
Then smear the end of the enema with oil, or with egg white, or with fenugreek mucilage, or something similar. Then gently insert it into the anus. Then let the attendant hold the syringe with both hands at the same time and squeeze it vigorously until all that is in the enema is emptied into the intestine.
Then remove the enema and let the sick person raise their legs up to the wall.
If the medicine compels them to defecate, then let them hold it as long as they can. And if they sleep with it all night, it is best for healing.
But if you give an enema to someone with abdominal swelling and they rush to defecate, then repeat the enema until they are healed.
But if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the enema be very mild and gentle, like the enemas given to children.
And this is the form of a delicate enema for when the skin is tight.
Chapter 84.
I have already said in the division on the treatment of wounds what was fitting for the book, and I will say here the treatment of them with the hand and with medicines according to complement.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to what causes the wound and according to the place on which the wound falls.
Therefore, the things by which wounds are often made are like the throwing of a stone: or a cut with a sword, or knife; or a percussion with a spear, or wood, or arrow; and similar things from many objects.
But wounds which are made according to the locations of the body are, such as a wound which falls upon the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or thigh; and similar ones from those limbs.
And I am stating the cures of some wounds, so that I may put that reasoning and rule over the other wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds; because the recollection of complex ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say. When a simple wound occurs on the head, and there is no bone fracture; then consider if it is from the throw of a stone, or similar things, and has already broken only the skin: and the wound is large, and you fear for an unfavorable outcome of hot abscesses; then hurry and perform cephalic phlebotomy immediately; and do not delay it; and extract blood from him according to the amount of his strength: if there is nothing prohibiting phlebotomy, especially if the blood that comes out from the wound is little. And let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the sick person beware of overeating, and wine, and heavy foods.
But if something prohibits reduction, then let him reduce from food, and not approach meats, nor wines.
And place upon the wound, if a hot abscess happens to him, cotton infused in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
But if you are safe from a hot abscess, then place upon the wound, if it is fresh with its own blood and the air has not yet changed it, powder which I will state later a little: and if the air has already changed it, then place some ointments upon it until it heals.
But if the wound is large, or is from a cut by a sword, and similar instruments, and its edges do not come together by themselves, then bring them together with suture, as I shall explain concerning wounds of the belly.
But if the wound is already separated from the bone, and hangs down, and there is no retention of it except by a small suspensory bandage: then cut it. Then treat the wound with medicines and restore hard flesh in place of the skin, and instruct the patient to eat foods which are nourishing, such as meat and cooked wheat, until he is fat; and those similar.
But if you find putrefaction in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone: then cut out all the putrefaction and discard it; then treat that area.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flows and it is not stopped by medicines, then examine the artery above. If therefore you find it uncut, then cut it with a scalpel or tie it off. But if necessity compels when that which we have said does not work, then burn it until the blood is stopped.
But if these wounds are small and simple, then their treatment is easy and light so that you sprinkle this powder over them: because it hardens them before the air changes them.
Method of Making Flesh-Colored Powder Take one part frankincense and two parts dragon's blood and three parts unquenched lime; grind the whole thoroughly and sift it. Fill the wound with this powder and constrict it well with a bandage until it adheres firmly to it and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it over with bandages and leave it alone; do not unbind it until it remains so that it does not fester. For it is not eradicated by medicine until it is healed.
And sometimes lime alone does this when you do not have frankincense at hand, and dragon's blood also performs the same operation in many large wounds when it is fitting to place it upon them and bind, and they are recent wounds with their own blood which has not been altered by air.
But if it has already been altered by air, especially when it is in the summertime, then this powder is not intended for use; therefore treat it with what we said so that you place certain digestive ointments upon them; or place poultices of barley flour made with water and honey upon them until they discharge pus. Then treat them with the remaining care until they are healed.
But if it should happen that there is a fracture in the bone with the wound, and it is small, then extract it with forceps; and I will now speak of the treatment of skull fractures in what is to come.
Concerning wounds of the neck.
But if a wound should happen in the neck, then there is no difference in its treatment and that of a head wound when it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has already been cut, then if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to apply treatment that produces strong astringency, such as alum, and za'faran, and those similar to them. Because they are harmful to nerves and contract them.
And do not place anything cold at all upon the wound; because the substance of nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is the most exalted of the members; and let that with which it is treated be from mild medicines, such as lime slaked with fresh water repeatedly infused with oil, or with rose oil, and tutty when it is washed even with fresh water: and the remaining mineral stones, in this manner, and humid ointments.
But if the wound is large, then apply a suture; or bring together its lips with poultices. Then care for it until it heals.
But if the wound has depth, or a sinus occurs in its lower part, in which pus has already collected, then perforate it in a more depressed place within it.
And if an artery has already been cut in the wound and its flow of blood has followed; then separate or dry it and bind it; or cauterize it if necessity compels it.
But if the spine of the throat has already fallen, or all of it, and its ligaments are saved; then join the lips of the skin with a suture over the trachea, and do not touch the throat, rather equalize it, and reduce it to its natural form. Then bind it with a decent constriction, and let it go for several days. Then care for it with ointments in which there is astringency and desiccation until it heals.
But if the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then explore it; then if there are bone fragments in it, extract all of them, or as many as you can from them; and what is not prepared, leave it until the wound putrifies, and separates and its egress alleviates.
But if the wound is recent, then sprinkle powder which I have described upon it. But if bone appears to be seen in the wound, and a fistula is formed; then care for it with that of which mention comes in its chapter.
Concerning a Wound on the Shoulder Blades
If there is a thrust by a spear or knife; and you see its depth, then consider. Therefore if windiness comes forth from the wound when a weak person breathes from it, then you will know that the wound is mortal.
If it is not deep and fresh with its own blood: then do not put powder on it in the first hour, nor bind it up, so that the blood may not be retained in its depth and return to the heart and kill the sick person: but put an attractive ointment on it and dissolve it twice a day.
But if you do not have ointment present, then put an old rag in the wound opening so that it may draw out what flows from it of moistures; and make the sick person sleep upon the wound so that what collects in it may run.
But if three or more days have already passed since the wound and no spasm occurs to the sick person, nor evil trembling of the heart, nor constriction in breathing, and you see that his other dispositions are good: then know that the wound is healed. Therefore care for it with washes and the rest of its treatment until it heals.
But if its healing is excused and it is always opened up, then know that a fistula has already been made. Therefore care for it from its own chapter.
And if a wound is from the incision of a sword or knife and it is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then care for it with what preceded from suture if it is large; or with powder if it is small.
But if it has already made an impression on the bone and chips come from it, then probe the wound and hurry to extract those chips if they are separate. But if they are not separate, then leave them until the wound rots; for their extraction is relieved then.
But judgment in the treatment of other accidental wounds in other limbs is the judgment that we said.
Of wounds of the belly and the exits of intestines and their outlets.
Disruption which happens to the abdomen is great, and medium, and small.
From a great one therefore sometimes an intestine, or a number of intestines, comes out.
Therefore the introduction of them is made, and their suture more vehement and difficult.
And a small disruption also becomes difficult in another way. It follows therefore that the reduction of them is more difficult if you do not hurry in their introduction to their place immediately: because it is inflated, opened, and enlarged: therefore its entry becomes difficult.
Because of this, therefore a medium disruption is better: because with it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it becomes difficult in these two kinds.
Know that the suture of a vein is according to four methods.
Two sutures are common and apply to the suture of the abdomen, and to the suture of the remaining wounds of the body: and two other sutures properly pertain to the remaining wounds. However they are more proper with the suture of the abdomen: and I will tell you each one with explanation and declaration.
I say therefore that when a wound is small, and something comes out of it from the intestine, and it is difficult to reduce it: then it is because of one of two methods. Either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we said: Or because there is inflation in the intestine because of the cold of the air.
Therefore when it is so, it is necessary that you warm it, so that you immerse a sponge, or cloth dampened in lukewarm water only. Or you boil in water squints and cypress and saffron and embrace it with the intestine until inflation is resolved.
Sometimes wine in which there is astringency makes it: and it is better than water alone in the resolution of inflation.
When therefore you see the swelling already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which has been boiled marshmallow or mallow. For through that its entry is eased with a small thing.
But if its return is excused after this treatment, then make a small incision in the disruption with an instrument by which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when prepared than a section: and there is no compulsion to cut after necessity and defect of the instrument.
Let one part of it be twisted and pointed, and the other part not pointed, and let the fine end not be with the fineness of a spatula, but rather be a little blunter: and it is that which resembles a chisel, as you see.
When therefore the wound is enlarged and enters the intestine, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its own natural form in its proper place if possible above it; for it is better.
But when the disruption is wide in the lower part of the belly: then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on its back, and put their legs higher than their head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then put its head and chest higher than its lower parts.
And similarly if there is a disruption in one of the two parts of the belly, then put your attention and your consideration always on this, that the part in which the wound is be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary that you administer it in large wounds and in medium wounds.
But in small wounds, put it according to what is prepared for you.
Then make a pious minister be present between your hands who will hold the whole disruption with his hand: and join its lips. Then uncover from it for him who follows a suture something after something: and this is the manner of a common single suture.
And take a needle, or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Then let fall from the end of the disruption a quantity equal to the breadth of the ring finger, that is, a physician's breadth; and fix one needle beyond which you shall introduce a thread into both extremities of the skin, with both extremities of the sifac which is under the skin, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have now brought together two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the sifac: and there are four rows.
Then bind with a double thread around the needle several times on both sides at once, until the two lips of the wound are joined together in a decent manner.
Then let fall a quantity equal to the breadth of a finger, and fix another needle in them; then draw it tight with thread, just as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease to do that with as many needles as you need until you complete the constriction of the whole wound.
And let the needles be of medium size between thinness and thickness; because very thin needles pierce the flesh quickly, and thick ones have a difficult entry into the skin; therefore it is necessary that they are of medium thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be fixed in the two extremities of the skin very close together; lest the flesh be pierced quickly and the wound opened before its healing, and lest they also be lengthened with the suture, lest the wound be prevented from healing.
Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the sick man during his sleep; and put little wads of cloth on all sides to hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is already healed.
And this indeed, a species of suture with needles thus arranged, is more suitable for small wounds. Because one, or two, or about that number of needles suffice in the suturing of them.
The second suture is common, so that you bring together the extremities of four with a suture: two extremities of skin and two extremities of fascia in one stitch, with a needle which has thread wound equally fine and thick.
Then when you have pierced these four extremities with the needle, draw back the needle from the part where you began, so that the drawn thread may fall from the upper part of the wound: let the suture be like the suture of cloth whereby cloths are tied, and put between every suture a quantity equal to the thickness of the little finger.
But men know this suture.
And with this suture I healed a wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife and the disruption of the wound was greater than a palm; and about two palms had come out from the intestines from the middle intestine, and the disruption was in the middle of the belly.
I therefore returned it after his intestines had stood outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
The wound therefore became consolidated in the space of about fifteen days; and I cared for it and it was healed; and he lived afterward for many years, returning to his usual ways.
And the physicians had judged that it would not be healed.
And it was a marvel that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where ointment was not to be found, nor anything of medicine. I therefore put old linen upon the wound twice a day and constantly washed it with honey water until it was healed.
But I will tell two kinds of sutures proper according to the telling of speech.
And it is, that you begin with a sharp suture; and insert the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When therefore the needle has penetrated the skin and gone into the muscle following the length and breadth of the whole body: let the extremity of the fascia fall on the side in which you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate into the other extremity from the inner part to the outer part at the other extremity.
When therefore you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate thus in the same extremity from the outer part to the inner: and let go the sifac extremity which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate into the other extremity from the inner part to the outer; and make it penetrate with the penetration that you make it penetrate into the sifac extremity which is in its part until it penetrates into the whole.
Then begin also from the same side and with its extremity which is from the sifac on the other side, and draw out the needle from the skin that it penetrated.
Then return it into that skin and its sifac extremity which is on the other side with this mirach extremity; and draw it out from the skin which is from its part, and do this alternately one by one, and another; and do this alternately after another until you have extracted all of its extrinsic parts according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that its sutures be like those of skin layers; so that its sifac be alternately on one side with one extremity of skin; and that you let it go alternately on the other side, until you complete it.
The second kind of suture proper which Gale said.
This speech of his is narrated so that its suture be according to the likeness which some physicians make, so that they bring together every part to its corresponding fellow according to nature; and apply the sifac extremity to its other extremity, and the mirach extremity to its other extremity. And this is done according to what I tell you.
It is necessary that you fix the needle in the mirach extremity nearest to you from the outer part and make it penetrate to the inner in that alone; and let go the two sifac extremities. Then turn the needle and make it penetrate from the outer part to the inner in the two sifac extremities, namely on both sides.
Then turn both [instruments] over and make them penetrate from the inside to the outside, at two extremities of a pair of other forceps, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and it is a suture of which remembrance preceded.
This is therefore also the speech of Galen.
And some have said. The suture of the belly is corrected with two needles. Which is, to insert one thread into both of them; and begin with the insertion of the needle, from that which is at your side, and make it penetrate up to the other side, according to the suture of those who are leather equally.
And know that when the disruption is in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than that of the remaining parts of the belly.
The judgment indeed of the medication of a wound, is the judgment of other wounds.
Which is, because when you attain a fresh wound with its own blood, before the air alters it; and you reduce the intestine, and its own, and fit it; then place powder of incarnating on top.
But if the air has already altered the wound, then place on it certain ointments which are present to you, until they make pus; and the threads fall away, and the sutures consolidate, and heal. Then care for it as is the care of other wounds, until it is healed.
But if medicines are not present to you, then place on it immediately, from when it begins to emit pus, old lint; and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need another medication in many dispositions, when wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound may communicate to the principal members in injury, then it is necessary that you immerse soft wool in oil of tempered warmth, or in rose oil; and place it around the parts which are between the groins, and under the armpits.
If he feels pain, or pricks, or putrefaction in his bowels (for this often happens) then give him an enema with strong black wine, warm: especially if the putrefaction has already reached the intestine and is about to penetrate as far as its cavity.
And know that what is from the large intestine is of easier healing: and what is from them out of the subtle, more difficult to heal.
But the intestine which is called Jejunum does not admit healing from a wound that happens in it at all: and this is because of its multitude, which is from veins, and their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the nearness of its nature to nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the wound is a protuberance, and you overtake it fresh: reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce intestines equally.
But if a space has passed it, and it has already become green or black: then it is necessary that you constrict it with thread above the place which has been blackened from it, so that a flow of blood does not happen. For in a protuberance are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligament, and place two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it: so that relief comes upon you from its attraction, and extraction of it in case of a protuberance, and emission of pus from the wound.
A Reminder of a Wound That Happens in the Intestine.
When a rupture happens in the intestine, and it is small: then it is possible that it may be restored in some men: therefore because I have seen one who was wounded in his belly with a thrust of a lance, and the wound was from the right side of the stomach, and the wound became old: and a fistula was made, and there issued forth from it discharge, and flatulence.
Therefore I applied treatment until I was no longer anxious about its healing; nor did I cease to refine it, until it was healed and the site consolidated.
After I saw the site already consolidated, I feared for the patient, that some bad accident might befall him in his abdomen; and no such bad accident befell him from then on.
