And he remained in better dispositions, eating and drinking, and defecating, and entering the bath, and exercising himself in his diet.
And already some experimenters have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and is small, then it ought to be tied according to this method. And it is, that you take ants of large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place one ant from those which have an open mouth upon the two lips of the wound. When therefore its head is on them and it bites down with its mouth, cut off its head, for it will adhere and not be released.
Then place another ant near the first one, and do not cease doing this with an ant after an ant according to the total quantity of the wound.
Then reduce it, and its wound. For those heads will remain attached in the intestine until the intestine is nourished and healed, and no harm befall the patient deeply.
And already it is possible that the intestine may be tied even with a fine thread; which has been drawn out from the intestine of an animal, attached to it after it is inserted into a needle.
And it is, that you take the end of this thread from the intestine, and it should be scraped off very much. Then tie at its end a fine thread of flax wrapped around it. Then insert that thread into a needle and with it the intestine. Then put it back into its separation.
And this method of suture with ants and intestines is not except according to the way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound which is large, wide, especially if it is in one of the slender intestines: then there is no remedy except refinement, and its healing is not at all.
On the Care of Fistulas and Zuchemen.
Know that every wound or abscess when it grows old and becomes ancient, and becomes an ulcer and does not consolidate, and always discharges pus and is not cut off: then it is universally called a fistula, wherever it may be in the limb; and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula indeed is according to truth a filtered knot, hard, white: with which there is no pain and which has the concavity of a bird's feather: and because of that some call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And it is sometimes moist in certain hours, which discharges pus. And perhaps the moisture splits in certain hours. And sometimes this moisture is much, and sometimes little: and it is thick, and sometimes thin.
And I already stated previously in the division when I said wounds that every sore which does not heal and flesh is not born in it, it is not except because of one of nine new causes.
Of which one is, either because of the lack of blood in the body; or because of its malice; or because there is tough flesh between it and its upper lips, preventing the birth of flesh; or because there is much filth and squalor; or because the sore itself is putrid, and the material sent to it is of bad quality; or because the medicine is inconvenient in its healing; or because corruption falling in the region is a kind of pestilence; or through a property in the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta: in which digestion of illnesses is difficult and healing of abscesses by nature is delayed; or because there is one bone, or a number of bones in it.
And I have now shown all these causes and their signs, and their treatments with medicines in division; and it is necessary here that I make you know the treatment of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by the way of operation with the hand.
Therefore, know that Fistula occurs in all members of the body. For from them are Fistulas which reach the large veins and arteries, or nerves, or sinews: or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to one of the ribs: or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the joints composed in composition: such as the hand, and foot; and from them are those which go to the proximity of the principal member, and similar ones.
Which therefore of these Fistulas is according to this manner, is from illnesses difficult of healing, and does not receive healing, and occupation with it is toil and ignorance.
But if it is from Fistulas some of whose depth does not reach these places which I said: and you hope, and trust in its healing, and wish to arrive at the knowledge of it, then take a probe of bronze, or iron, if the Fistula extends according to straightness, and explore it with it.
But if there is crookedness in the Fistula, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead. For lead, with the gentleness of its body, is relieved, and curves around the crookedness.
But if the Fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to indicate upon it with a probe. Therefore wash out one of the openings from its openings.
For the moisture with which you wash it, goes towards the other openings, and runs out from them.
Then carefully inquire according to which manner it is possible for you: that you may know whether there is a bone, or nerve, and the Fistula's cavity is distant, or near: or whether the Fistula is one which has many openings, and stand over all of it, with your greatest strength: with questioning of yours, with which you question the sick person, and finding pain at compression with your hand upon the place, and similar things from signs and causes arising as an abscess.
Therefore, when you have stood upon all that true station, then go to the cure according to confidence: and it is, that you consider then, if there is a Fistula nearby apparent, or in a safe place far from the joint, or from a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places which I told you: then cut the Fistula according to what was stated from my narration, and remove what is in it from filtration, and corrupted flesh, which arises in its orifice from flesh, or thelul, and similar things: and cure it, until it is healed.
But if the Fistula is of distant depth, and is according to straightness: then it is necessary that you cut it deeply as much as is possible for you. Then clean it from all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply linen wrapped in sharp medicines: and insert it up to the cavity of the Fistula which you comprehended with iron: do this several times with it until the sharp medicine eats away all that remains in the cavity of the Fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
But if it does not heal through that, then cauterize it according to what we narrated.
But if the cause of the Fistula is a bone, and it is certified to you: then inquire about it, and cut it according to what I narrated to you: if nothing prevents you from a vein, or nerve, or principal member, as we said.
If therefore the bone is revealed to you, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it: then scrape it, until all its corruption is removed. Then cure it with what makes it flesh again, until it is healed.
But if it does not heal and emits pus as it was doing: then know that you have not reached the total eradication of corruption.
Therefore uncover it a second time and make a careful incision into it, and cleanse it with your greatest care.
Then restore it. If then it heals, it is good; and if not, then know that the corruption is deep within the body, which you have not reached. Therefore there is no skill for you in it except to leave it to nature.
But if the mouth of a young child is corrupted, and it is possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps that are suitable for it.
But if there are many bones, then carefully draw them all out, and do not leave any of them with your strength.
But if something opposes you from the humors and there is no fear or mistake in their incision; then cut into those humors and flesh, if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones in your time, then place over the wound something that will cause it to rot; and leave it until what is around those bones rots from flesh and humors.
And watch the wound so that it does not solidify. And constrict the section which you cut in your operation. Rather place over it, if you fear, a cloth immersed in sulfur ground with oil; or a cloth immersed in Egyptian ointment, or some green ointments.
For when those humors rot and separate from the bones, their removal is eased and their attraction.
But if there is one large bone, such as the bone of the leg or hip bone, and similar to them, and what is corrupted from it is a large part; then scrape it with the last incision until that blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
But if what is corrupted in it is a large part and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then you must saw it and cut it entirely up to the place where the corruption reaches; then care for it until it heals together.
And I tell you a story that happened to a man in his leg, so that you may take it as an example and help for your operation.
There was a young man about thirty years old, to whom came pain in his leg, from a cause which moved within him, that is, from his insides, until the materials reached his leg, and there formed a large abscess: and he had no external cause.
Time was extended with him in error of the physicians until the abscess was opened and many materials flowed forth with it. And his treatment was postponed until his leg became fistulous and many openings were made in it, which all emitted pus and the humors of the body.
A multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no one wise in the art of the hand among them, until I was called and I saw his leg and the materials which flowed from those openings with great force: even his whole body had already wasted away, and his color was citron.
Therefore I inserted a probe into one of the openings and it reached the bone.
Then I scrutinized all the openings, and I found that they reached one another from all parts of the leg.
I hastened and cut above one of those openings until I exposed a part of the bone; and I found it corrupted: which had already been corroded, blackened, putrefied, and perforated until it penetrated to the marrow.
Therefore I sawed away what was revealed to me, and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I judged that there was nothing in the bone except that corruption which I cut away and sawed. And now I had rooted it out.
Then I applied restoration to the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it did not consolidate.
Then I probed and found above the bone a second opening above the first discovery; and I found continuous corruption with the bone, and I sawed what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I tried its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore I detected above it again and did not cease to cut the bone in sections and try its restoration; and it was not restored until I cut about a palm from the bone and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines and it was quickly consolidated and healed; and this iteration was not necessary in its operation and section except because of the weakness of the infirm person's condition, and his lack of endurance, and my fear for him concerning death: because he suffered from evil syncopes due to excess evacuation every hour.
He was therefore healed with complete healing and firm flesh grew in the place of the bone; and a good arrangement was made in his body, and his strength returned; and he returned to his former condition, and did not cause him any hindrance in walking.
But if the prominent bone in the place of the body is already broken then it is necessary that you saw it according to this method: and it is as follows: take a bandage and bind it at the extremity of the prominent bone; and order someone to extend it upwards and put another bandage of thick wool upon the first bandage. Then bind it above the flesh which is under the bone and extend its extremity so that you draw the flesh downwards; and you expose the flesh above the place which you wish to saw, so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place wood or a board under the lower bone perpendicularly; because when you do this it is not prevented from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the saw be above the corrupted place for a short time so that there is no corruption in the cavity of the bone; and it does not appear to the sense in its appearance, and compel the saw to its own vice.
If the mouth is corrupted and not prominent, but its part continues with a part; and the corruption is in the middle of it, or in a part of it, then expose the flesh from all sides entirely. Then put wood underneath.
Afterwards saw it from the first part, where the corruption is, until the saw continues from the other part; and let the saw be according to the length from the corruption a little, as we said.
But if the corruption is at the junction, cut the corrupted junction itself, and saw the mouth where it continues from the other part.
But if the corruption is in the continuation of two junctions, then there is no skill in it except abrasion.
But if the corruption is in the comb of the hand, or in the comb of the foot; then the matter in it is very difficult. But it is necessary that you inquire how the corruption appears to you. And abrade it, and clean it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever skill you are able to rectify it: when it does not cause opposition to you by vein, or nerve.
And know that cutting tools and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and subtlety, and magnitude, and smallness, and hardness, and rarity. Therefore it is necessary that you prepare an instrument suitable for each kind of operation.
And know that these operations signify to you above the species of instrument by which you need, when you have a long study. And the knowledge of the ways of this art is a testimony to the ways of illnesses; for then you will already find by yourself what suits it from the instruments for every infirmity.
And I affirm to you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you place examples upon which you cut, and the trial by which you experiment upon others.
Shape of another saw.
Shape of a scraper for joints. Let the head of this scraper be according to the shape of the head of a star-shaped nail: and its points let be according to the shape of the points of an elischetfegi.
And it is not fitting unless the heads of the joints are rubbed with it when they are corrupted or the mouth is wide and large.
Shape of a larger scraper.
Shape of a wide scraper.
Shape of a scraper in which there is concavity.
Shape of another subtle scraper.
Shape of another scraper of curved extremity.
Shape of a small, subtle, thin scraper.
This resembles a spoke, and its extremity is like a saw: it is exhibited in many places for the reason of bones.
Shape of a scraper which suits the reason of that which is perforated from bones.
Its extremity is triangular, and its extremities are pointed.
It is made from India. And similarly it is fitting that all which we have said be made from scrapers, and incisors, and saws.
Concerning the incision of extremities, and the locking of bones.
Extremities putrefy, either from an external or internal cause.
Therefore, when you have treated that corruption with medical treatment, and the treatment does not help; and you see the corruption fall upon a limb, which is unaffected by anything: then it is necessary that you cut into that limb until you reach the corruption, so that the weak person may be saved from death, or from the greater evil which is the loss of the limb.
And the sign of this happening is that the limb turns black, until it seems as though fire has burned it, or it rots after the blackness: until that rot reaches what follows that limb and spreads throughout the whole body.
Therefore, hasten with its incision, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain venomous creatures: such as a sea scorpion, viper, lizard, and similar ones.
Therefore if the corruption or sting is at the end of a finger, then cut into the finger and do not allow space for the corruption to advance up to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut it from the root of the focus, and do not allow space for the corruption until it reaches the focus of the arm.
And if it invades that, then cut the arm at the elbow in its joint.
But if the corruption passes through and you see it around the elbow, then there is death for the weak person in that. And besides this from treatment, there is the quantity of strength.
And do likewise with the foot, when the corruption invades a toe, then cut it at one of its knots; and if it reaches the arch of the foot, then cut off the whole foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the knee joint.
But if the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no wisdom in it except dismissal and leaving the weak person to death.
The way to cut a limb, or sew it up, is to tie a ligature below the place you wish to cut, and another ligature above the place. Let one assistant extend the lower ligature downward and let another assistant extend the upper ligature upward. Then separate the flesh between the two ligatures with a broad spatula until all the flesh is exposed. Then cut or sew.
And it is necessary that you place a linen cloth over all the parts so that the saw does not touch the healthy area; and apply a warm poultice to the injured part.
But if internal bleeding occurs while you are working, then quickly cauterize the spot or place some powders that staunch bleeding over it. Then return to your treatment until it is complete.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage and care for it until it heals.
And I tell you that this same accident happened to a man on his foot.
For it happened that a blackness appeared on his foot, like a burn similar to fire: and that corruption first occurred on his toe until it began to spread throughout the whole foot.
The man therefore hurried to cut it away from himself after he saw the corruption spreading through his limb with its violence, as he found from the pain and burning: and he cut it at the joint and was healed.
And after a long time had passed, that same corruption happened to him on the index finger of his hand.
He therefore came to me and I tried to restore that excess with what I put on his hand from medicines after the cleansing of his body; and the excess was not repelled and began to spread into another finger until the corruption began to spread into his hand.
He therefore called me to cut off his hand; and I did not want to do it, hoping for a recovery from that excess on my part; and I also feared death for him at the cutting of his hand. For the man's strength was above the case.
And when he therefore despaired of me, he returned to his land; and there came to me news of him, that he hastened, and fell with his whole hand; and he was healed.
And I did not bring this account, except that it may be a help concerning that which falls upon the type of this disease; and that it may be a sign by which it is signified, and according to which it is acted.
Of the Treatment of Sinuses, and the Quality of Clysterization with Medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members, and the space of the abscess is prolonged, until it makes a collection of pus; then it breaks, or is perforated, and what is within it comes out as pus, and there remains an empty space as if it were a vessel; and the skin which is upon it, like a cloth which has already been thinned: and it does not proceed to its ultimate corruption, nor press into the mouth, nor into a nerve, nor into a ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus; and not a Fistula; nevertheless if the space of it is prolonged until it presses the corruption into any one of these members, then it is called a Fistula, or zuken.
And the treatment of it is, that you cut all that skin as it revolves; and especially when it has been thinned, and made like cloth; and trust because it does not adhere to the place, on account of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere, and the ultimate corruption does not follow from it; and if there is thickness in the skin from flesh, then treat it with a clyster; and it is, that you consider it as to the Sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus which is emitted from it has a foul odor; then clysterize it with Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, and oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, are gathered in a vessel: and they are decocted over fire until the medicine reddens; and it comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take from it what you need and dissolve it with water and honey, and inject it into the Sinus with an enema, and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the amount of two hours.
Then extract it with pressure; do this for as many days until the Sinus is cleansed and the stench is removed.
And sometimes it is enemaed with ash water, when this medicine is not present for you; and it is that you should take ash from vine wood or ash from holly wood; and pour water over it. Then strain it, and enema with it until you are confident that the Sinus has already been washed.
But if the sick person does not tolerate Egyptian medicine nor ash water, then enema them with water and honey to which some crushed ziniar has already been mixed. Or enema them with honey and wine mixed together, since it is the property of honey to cleanse and wash; and wine makes the Sinus adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you do that several times, and are confident that the Sinus corruption has already been removed; then enema them with that which causes flesh to grow in it.
And it is that you should take some diafynicon plaster and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or enema them with certain medicines and other ointments, the properties of which I have written in the treatise on ointments.
But if the opening of the Sinus is tight, into which the enema cannula does not fit; then widen it a little with iron; or place in it licorice wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in basil ointment until it widens.
And similarly if the opening is also wide; then bring together its lips with suture and let out from it the amount into which the enema can enter without addition.
And similarly if the opening from which pus flows is raised upwards: then cut it into a lower place within it so that the pus may flow downwards; for pus when constricted in the depth of a sinus prevents flesh from being generated there.
But if it is not possible for you to cut the sinus somewhat below it according as you wish: then endeavor to prepare the limb by preparation so that pus may flow from it with ease according as it is prepared for you. Endeavor that nothing of the pus be retained in it at all; and take consolidating medicines which are placed upon a sign, suitable to what you desire. And extend them upon linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that communication of the whole sinus may be made. Then cut with forceps a hole directly into the opening of the sinus, somewhat larger than the opening of the sinus. Then extend from soft ointments upon another cloth according to the quantity of the hole; and place it upon it at a distance from the opening of the sinus so that the flow of pus may be relieved therefrom; and do not remove the large cloth except after several days at once.
But that which is upon the opening of the sinus is that which you remove every hour so that what collects in it from pus may exit; and so that you may know whether that which flows from it is much or little; or digested or undigested.
And with this again consider the place of the sinus itself whether the master thereof feels pain in it, or there is no pain in it; and whether there is an abscess in it or not.
Therefore when your treatment is thus extended and you see the material which flows out upon the opening of the sinus; then know that the sinus already adheres or approaches adhesion; then therefore dissolve it in the morning or on the third day; and when you dissolve the wound, then change another cloth and renew the ointment.
But if after a long time a subtle virus comes out of the sinus, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, since there are people who delay the formation of flesh in their wounds, and some who act contrary to that.
But if after many days pus that is undigested comes out of the sinus, then you will know that the sinus does not adhere.
Therefore if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and its matter prolonged, then add to the drying medicines what physicians truly do, so that the medicines are humid in their substance and dry in their power: such as dyafinicon ointment, when an excess of zegi addition is added to it.
And from the best last treatment there is, take dyafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil, and pour old wine equal to it in substance over it.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And now honey acts similarly to this when you are not present with dyafinicon ointment, boil it until it thickens and use it. Or take myrrh and aloe and frankincense: grind the whole thing and sprinkle it on honey while it is over the fire. Then lay a cloth over it and bind it over the sinus; and smear it with honey which you have boiled until it has thickened and sprinkle spices over it, then place a cloth over it and bind it.
And sometimes long aristolochia is administered with roots of the celestial lily and lily; herb flour, and centaury. Use them simply or combined, grind them and sift them; then sprinkle them over the honey I have described and use it.
And if the matter of the sinus is prolonged and does not heal with this treatment, then know that there is corruption deep within it; or it is already making an impression on the bones, or in the remaining hard members, or ligaments: then cure it with a treatment for fistulas, according to what has been mentioned before.
On the treatment of whitlow and ingrown toenail, and cutting open a finger that has been added to it, and sectioning.
Whitlow is an abscess that arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and perhaps it arises in the other fingers.
Therefore when the matter of it is prolonged and its cure is neglected, and it becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, or it is corrupted and discharges pus until it corrodes the root of the nail, and perhaps it corrupts it entirely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone until it has a foul odor, and the extremity of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
Therefore when you treat it, as we said in the division, and your treatment does not succeed, then it is necessary that you cut with an iron all the excess that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cautery is very beneficial.
But if the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the empty additions of the nail already press the flesh inwards, and begin to prick it, and injure it: then it is necessary that you place a thin Radix under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and push it upwards; and cut the flesh with ease, and put on it what remains of the flesh from burning medicines, corrosive ones until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already pressed into the bone, then it is necessary that you cut open that bone and remove it; for it will never heal while a corrupted bone remains in it.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption advancing into the finger, then cut it at one node, according to what its previous remembrance has said. Then treat it until it is healed.
If however it should happen that a nail is rubbed or struck and there arises in it intense pain, then it is necessary first of all to let blood from the weakling. Then dry the nail with a sharp spatula and cut it transversely from above downwards.
And be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh which is under the nail for you will cause through that extreme pain to the weakling and it will be a cause of the addition of new flesh in the place.
Then treat the place with something that soothes pains.
But an added finger which is born in some men's hands, perhaps it is entirely fleshy and perhaps there are bones in some of them and perhaps there is a nail in it: and some of them are born at the root of the joint or its birth is in certain nodes of the finger. That therefore which is born in certain nodes of the finger does not move and that which is born at the joint of the finger perhaps moves.
Therefore the section of it which is fleshy is easy. That is, to cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
But the treatment of that whose birth is at the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore abstain from its incision.
But in that which is born in the finger at the last of the nodes then it is necessary first to cut its flesh with a round cut down to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for it. Then treat it until it heals.
The consolidation which happens to fingers to each other very often happens either from the birth of man that is it is born with him or from the consolidation of a wound or burning by fire and similar things.
It is therefore necessary to cut that consolidation until the fingers return according to their natural form. Then place between them linen cloths, or rags soaked in rose oil: so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate between them; or place between them a thin sheet of lead, until the place consolidates as it should.
And similarly if a consolidation should happen to certain fingers with the palm, then cut that consolidation according to how it is prepared and suits the shape of the limb.
Concerning the Incision of a Vice.
Varices are Veins enveloped, thick, full of superfluous melancholic humors which occur in many members of the body: and their most frequent occurrence is in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel, work, and carry burdens.
Therefore it is necessary that you administer a shaking of the body from black bile frequently with vigorous shaking.
Then phlebotomy for the patient, letting blood from the basilic vein.
The cure of them with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they be cut, and black blood be extracted. And the other method is that the Vein be raised and entirely extracted.
The cutting of it, however, is according to this method: First embrocate the leg with much hot water until the thick feculent blood is resolved. Then bind the infirm person's leg from the upper part of their hip to the lower part of their knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three, with a wide incision.
Then raise it up, that is, draw out the blood into your hand from the lower part of the leg upward; and from the upper part the phlebotomist must first phlebotomize the infirm person. Then cut with the instrument as far down as needed, until a quantity of blood comes out which you see is sufficient and which the strength of the infirm person can bear.
Then bind it and order him to abstain from foods that produce black bile; and repeat evacuation and bloodletting when the veins are full, and it harms the sick person.
The elevation of this is according to this method. Shave the leg of the sick person, if there are many hairs on it. Then introduce him into a bath; or embrocate him on his leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins swell out; or he is exercised with strenuous exercise, if a bath is not present for him, until the limb is heated.
Then dry the skin directly on the vein section lengthwise; or at its last part near the knee, or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks and expose the vein from all sides until it appears to the senses; and at its appearance, you see it reddish towards a dusky decline. When therefore it emerges from the skin, you see it white as if it were a cord.
Then introduce a Radix beneath it until it is raised and emerges from the skin; suspend it with a blind hook, gentle.
Then dry another section near that section by three fingers. Then expose the skin from above the vein until it appears. Then raise it with Radixes as you did and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then dry another section, or several sections, if necessary for that. Then raise and cut it with the last section near the heel.
Then extract it to the section which is above it.
And do that until you extract it from the higher section, or all the higher sections, until it emerges completely; then cut it.
But if it does not obey you in being extracted and raised; then introduce a needle with a strong double thread and tie it and extract it; or introduce a Radix beneath it and revolve your hand to all sides until it emerges. And beware, lest it fall. For if it falls, it will be difficult for you to raise it up greatly, and harm comes upon the weak one from it.
Therefore when you have extracted it entirely, then place wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil upon the place of the wound, and tend to it until it heals.
But if the Vine is contracted, having a twist with an involution to its parts, and it is not as we said according to straightness: then it is necessary that you cut upon it at every part of its twist, and its places of involution.
Then suspend it with hooks until you raise it entirely and beware at your section upon it lest you fall upon a vein, or wound it: for it will become difficult for you to raise or extract it. Therefore preserve your prudence.
The Form of the Falling Vine, How It Is Healed.
The Form of a Blind Vine.
It does not have a curvature like the other Hooks, nor is it of an acute point so that it would not wound a vein and it is of a thick, gentle flexuousness: for if it were slender, it would fall upon the vein by its slenderness: rather let it have excessive thickness as we said.
On the Extraction of a Leg Vein.
This Vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Alizegi and the land of the Arabs, and in dry, hot lands of little tenderness, that is, those in which neither herb nor tree is born.
And perhaps it is generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is indeed from the putrefaction that happens under the skin, just as it happens within bodies of serpents and ascaris worms, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
The mark of the beginning of this event of the Vein is a vehement inflammation which occurs in the leg. Then the place blisters. Then the Vein begins to come out from the place of that blistering, as if it were the root of a plant, or an animal.
Therefore when its extremity appears from it, it is necessary that you envelop upon it a piece of lead, whose weight is from one scruple to two and bind it: and let the lead hang suspended from the leg.
Whenever therefore anything issues from it to the outside, envelop that in the lead and bind it. And if it is prolonged much, then cut a part of it and envelop the remainder. And do not cut it entirely before it issues forth altogether. For if you cut it, it contracts and enters into the flesh: and it causes an abscess to occur, and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be gently handled and drawn out gradually until it issues forth from its last part, and nothing remains of it in the body: and from this indeed Vein there issues forth in some men what is five palms and ten in length. And now it has come to me that it issued forth from a man of twenty palms.
But if it happens to you in the hour of your medication: then insert a ray into its hole and pierce it with a long hole through the body until all that is in it of matter is evacuated: and administer putrefaction of the place with medicines for several days. Then care for it with the treatment of abscesses.
And this indeed Vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot, or in the foot itself: and there are many openings to it, and a branch issues forth from every opening.
Therefore care for it, as we said in the division, and with that which has been stated before.
On the Section of Worms Bred Under the Skin, and It Is Called the Disease Bouina.
This ailment is called in a certain land among us the bovine disease: because it very often happens to oxen, and it is a small worm, which is born between the skin and flesh.
And it wanders throughout the whole body, ascending and descending: and it is shown to the senses during its movement from limb to limb, until it bursts wherever it bursts in the skin, and comes out.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. Just as worms and snakes happen, that is, earthworms, and roundworms in the belly.
And it is feared because of its harmfulness. Because when it wanders in the body, and rises to the head, and reaches a place: then perhaps it opens up there, and comes out, and destroys the eye: and that happens very often.
Therefore when you wish to cure it, and extract it, then it is not done except at its movement, and appearance to the senses.
It is necessary therefore that you bind what is above it, and what is below it with a strong constriction ligature. Then cut above it, and extract it.
But if it burrows into the flesh, and you do not find it: then apply a cautery with fire upon the place, until it dies.
And the greater thing that is feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we said.
But if you see it has already reached the head near the eye, then bind a good constriction under it above the eyebrow. Then dry it over itself and pull it out.
And it is necessary that the sick person use purification of his body with medicines dissolving foul bad humors.
And let him beware of foods producing putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the ailment Nakir.
This ailment is called in our land Nakir, and it is a pain that occurs in certain members; then it is transformed from member to member.
And I myself have indeed already seen it, according as I relate to you. I was summoned to a sick woman in certain ulcers. And she exposed her arm and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the cord of the arm.
And after I remained for one hour, I saw one swelling advance with a hiss, just as a worm advances, ascending to her shoulder faster than it is possible; so that it be like quicksilver, when it runs from place to place.
Therefore the pain withdrew from that place and was fixed in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran through the rest of the body until it reached the other arm.
Then she narrated to me that it revolved throughout her whole body, according as I saw.
Therefore I marveled at the speed of its transformation from member to member. And I myself had not seen this ailment with my own eyes in this way before. Nevertheless I have seen many who find pain transformed from member to member; and I have not seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it to be anything other than because the woman was one of those with dry bodies and exposed veins; therefore this windiness transformed appears to the senses.
And it is necessary that they not appear according to this method in those who are delicate and with moist bodies, hidden veins.
Therefore when you wish its cure and the patient feels that pain: then if it is apparent to your eye as we said, then constrict above it and beneath it quickly: and cut above it until that wind comes out constricted: and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes: then treat it with shaking of the body and with that which expels winds and drives them out with foul-smelling pills, and pills of pellitory and the like.
On the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their kinds and according to the places in which they fall from the body.
Their diversity indeed is: because of them there are those which have large points and small points: and concave ones, and deaf ones. And of them there are those which have three angles. And of them there are those which have tongues. And of them there are those which have stars.
Those indeed which are according to the limbs in which they fall, are according to two ways. For either the limbs are from principal concave limbs such as the brain and heart and liver and lung and kidneys and intestines and bladder and those similar to them. Therefore when it falls into any of these limbs and the signs of death appear to you which I shall tell you afterwards: then it is necessary that you avoid the extraction of that arrow from them for death follows him in whom it is in many dispositions. And when these evil signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not concealed in the depth of the limb, then extract it and care for the wound.
For when an arrow falls into the brain and penetrates the mouth and exits through the siphac which is above the brain, the following occur: violent headache, dimness of vision, vertigo, redness in the eyes, swelling, redness of the tongue, spasm, confusion of mind, and vomiting of bile. And perhaps blood flows from the nostrils or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off and the voice is destroyed. And moisture white like pulp exits from the place of the wound. And there exits from it something similar to the wateriness of flesh.
Therefore, if these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment and extract the arrow if it does not exit.
The signs of an arrow when it falls into the heart are that it is near the left nipple and he feels as if it is fixed in a hard thing, not in a hollow thing. And perhaps there is motion of the arrow similar to the motion of the pulse and black blood runs from the wound. And following that is coldness of the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore that death descends without doubt.
And the sign of an arrow when it has wounded the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound and the vessels which follow the neck become inflamed and the color of the sick person changes. And he breathes out a great, deep breath and seeks the attraction of cold air.
But if the arrow should fall into the veil which is in the chest, then it is near the small ribs and there is great panting with violent pain accompanied by sighing and all the limbs of the shoulders are moved.
And if an arrow should fall into the stomach, then perhaps undigested food will come out from the wound.
And if an arrow should fall into the belly and lodge there: dung will come out from the wound, or phlegm, or intestine which has already been ruptured. Therefore there is no hope in its cure, nor in the extraction of the arrow.
And if an arrow should fall into the bladder and urine comes out: and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger upon the sick person: then you know that it is mortal.
But the remaining limbs, such as the face and neck, and throat, and shoulder blade, and armpit, and spine, and clavicle, and thigh, and leg, and those similar limbs are saved according to many arrangements: when the arrow does not find an artery or nerve: and the arrow is not poisonous.
And I tell you what I have seen about these arrows so that you may know through it concerning your treatment.
Which is, because to a certain man an arrow fell into the tear duct of his eye, at the root of the nose. Therefore I extracted it from him on the other side under the fat of his ear and he was healed and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which had fallen to him in the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid and the arrow was already hidden and I did not obtain from it except a small portion of its end with which it adhered to its wood and it was a large arrow from the arrows of Turkish bows, with mild iron, in which there are no ears.
Therefore the Jew was healed and no bad accident befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it is that which has ears. Therefore, I placed it upon her between two guides, and now it was submerged in her throat. So I dealt carefully with it until I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and we thought that he would die from it.
Therefore, after thirty days had passed him and nothing changed in his condition, I placed myself over the arrow, and I contrived upon it, and extracted it; and he was healed, and no bad thing happened to him.
And I saw a man to whom an arrow had fallen on his back, and the wound had become consolidated over it. Therefore, after seven years, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow had fallen in her belly, and the wound became consolidated over it, and the arrow remained inside her; and nothing changed in her condition, and the woman did not find harm from it in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow had fallen on his face, and the wound became consolidated, and it remained, not finding him much pain; and similar things to these, many.
And I extracted an arrow from a man of the King's princes, which had fallen to him in the middle of his nose, and it had already declined towards the right side a little, and the whole arrow was hidden.
Therefore, I was called to cure him after the fall of the arrow for up to three days. I found therefore the wound of the arrow very tight.
Therefore I sought it with a subtle touch and did not feel it; and he himself found a prickling and pain under his right ear. I therefore hoped that it was that prickling from the extremity of the arrow.
Therefore I plastered the place with a plaster in which there was attractive and digestive power, confident within myself that the place would be poulticed, and a sign of an arrow would appear to me, so that I might cut above it: and nothing happened in the place indicating that the arrow had reached the place.
Therefore I persevered with the plaster upon it for many days, and nothing happened, and meanwhile the wound became consolidated: and he remained weak from its extraction for a space of days until he felt the arrow one day within his nose. And he related that to me.
And he related that to me. Therefore I placed upon that wound a sharp, corrosive medicine for many days until it opened: and when I tried, I touched it and felt the slender extremity of the arrow adhering in the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine until the extremity of the arrow appeared visibly to us: and it passed for me with him a space of time of about four months.
Then after the wound was enlarged and it was possible for me to introduce forceps into it and move it and it did not obey the exit: then I did not cease to refine and contrive upon it with ways of ingenuity until I began upon it one day with forceps well made, according to as their form will come at the end of this chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And physicians indeed judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore I restored it, and the wound became consolidated and he was healed with perfect health: and nothing harmed him at all.
And I relate to you the quality of the extraction of certain arrows so that you might place that rule and meaning upon it of what I did not say.
For the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended with narration: nor does that book comprehend it, and a prudent craftsman compares with a little upon much: and with what is present, upon what is absent: and he finds a new operation and a new instrument among the descents of foreign things of this art when they happen.
Therefore, I say that arrows do not exit the limbs in which they are fixed except according to two modes. Or by attraction from the place from which it entered. Or by the contrary of the other side.
And that which exits where it entered is either an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place and is attracted and exits.
But if it does not yield to exit at the hour in which it falls, then it is necessary that you dismiss it for several days until the flesh around it putrefies and its attraction is relieved and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed in bone and does not yield to you for exit, then dismiss it also for several days and repeat it with attraction and motion every day until it exits.
But if it does not yield to you for exit after those days, then it is necessary that you perforate around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine awl until the arrow is enlarged. Then attract it and extract it.
But if the arrow is already concealed in a place of the body and is hidden from sensation, then inquire for it with touch. If, however, you feel it, then draw it out with certain instruments which are suitable for its extraction.
But if you are not able to get hold of it because of the constriction of the wound and because of the length of the arrow in depth; and there is neither a bone nor a nerve nor a vein there: then cut down upon it until the wound is enlarged and it is possible to extract the arrow.
But if it has ears by which to be held, then free the flesh fixed in them from every side with all the ingenuity you can do that, and ingenuity, if it is not possible to get past the flesh in the fracture of the ears and the twisting thereof until it comes out.
And when you administer the extraction of an arrow, in whatever place it is, then use the twisting of your hand with forceps to all sides until you make it come out; and subtle subtlety at the last so that the arrow is not broken and its attraction becomes difficult for you, and its extraction.
But if you are not able to get it out within an hour, then leave it for several days until those which are around it become putrid. Then repeat it for the tension will be relieved. But if the flow of blood causes you opposition then administer what we said from healing in its chapter; and guard your strength from cutting a vein or a nerve or a cord.
And administer ingenuity in every way possible to you for the avoidance of the arrow; and let it be with ease and quietude, or slowness, and firmness as I have told you.
And it is necessary, that you make when you draw out the arrow, so that it be weak according to the figure according to which it was at the case of the arrow itself. For it is more beneficial. But if that is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from the figures.
But the arrow which comes out from the opposite side, or already proceeds from it something to the exterior; or makes a hump at the extremity of the arrow by feeling from the upper part of the nearby skin and you see it prominent: cut then upon it, and let the incision be according to the amount that the forceps can grasp in it.
Then draw it out; for it itself becomes easy to remove.
But if it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand according to the roundness, until the arrow presses into the bone and enlarges itself; then draw it out.
And if not, leave it for several days; then repeat the process until it comes out.
But if the wood of the arrow is in it, then drive it in with that.
But if the wood has fallen out and you wish to administer a thrust, then insert a concave instrument into it, so that its concavity enters the tail of the arrow; then drive it with that.
But if the arrow is concave, then drive it with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow is relieved by that.
But if the arrow is poisoned, it is necessary that you scoop out all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so. Then treat it with what is suitable for that.
But if the arrow falls into the chest, or the belly, or the bladder, or the side: and it is nearby from what you touch with your hand, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and be cautious of incision of a vein, or nerve, and draw it out.
Then sew the wound if you see fit, and treat it until it heals.
And this is the form of forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the extremities be like a bird's beak, which are made like iron; when they grasp upon the arrow, they do not release it.
And many kinds are made from them, large, small, and medium, according to the quantity, magnitude of the arrow, its scantiness, and the width of the wound and its tightness.
The shape of the speculum is concave.
And the shape of the sound probe is blunt.
This is the extremity of the sound probe like a rod so that its entry is eased into the concave speculum and it is driven in with it.
Concerning the Phlebotomy of Veins.
The veins to be phlebotomized, the ones which customarily run in the body, are thirty.
Of these, there are sixteen in the head: two pulsatile veins which are behind the ears called the two Herbs; and two arteries which are apparent in the two temples; and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called Lookers; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein which is at the extremity of the nose. And two Guidegis which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
But the veins which are phlebotomized in the arms are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic and it is from the sylvan side and common people call it the head vein. And the Fusca. And it is the Middle vein and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic; and common people call it the body vein. And the Basilic, which is located on the domestic side and is also called the Titillaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the cord of the arm, and it is placed above the elbow; and it is that in which one performs bloodletting, and it is that which appears above the thumb with a manifest protrusion.
And the Little Savior; and it is a vein that is between the annular and the little finger; and it has two branches.
And in the leg and foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curvature of the thumb from the outer side. And the second is the Saphenous; and its location is at the heel from the inner side. And the Nessi vein; and its location is at the heel from the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
Concerning bloodletting from two veins behind the ears.
Bloodletting indeed from the two veins that are behind the ears is beneficial for old catarrhs, and hemiplegia, and epilepsy, and ancient evil ulcers of the head.
And the quality of bloodletting from them is according to what I relate. And it is that the head of the sick person is shaved; and their occiput is rubbed in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, good rubbing. Then let the sick person bind their neck with a bandage until two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in two depressions of the head. Therefore seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark there with a hot iron.
Then take a phlebotomy knife, and it is that which is called Inciding. Then insert it under the vein into the skin until the phlebotomist reaches the bone. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upward, and cut into the vein with the skin with an incidental incision. The length of the incision should be the amount of two joined fingers, or thereabouts; and let as much blood flow out as you wish.
Then bind it with lint, and leave it until it heals.
And now at the beginning of the book incision and cautery of these have preceded.
Indeed, bloodletting from the two arteries in the temples is beneficial for chronic headache, and for gout, and for habitual ophthalmia, and for the course of acute effusions flowing to the eyes.
And the quality of bloodletting from them is according as I tell you. Let the weak person bend their neck much until two veins appear clearly to the senses, and let their pulse be demonstrated under your finger. Then mark them with hot cautery.
Then raise the skin above the vein with your index finger and insert the lancet beneath it, raising the vein upwards. And cut into it as you did with the other two veins; and let the blood flow out according to your need.
Then release the pressure on the weak person's arm and place your finger upon the vein for an hour. Afterwards place a bandage and lint upon it and bind it tightly from above; and leave it until it heals.
And now the recollection of these, and their incision, and their extraction have preceded at the beginning of the book.
Indeed, bloodletting from the vein in the forehead is beneficial after bloodletting from the cephalic vein for old facial ailments; and for headache, and redness; and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its bloodletting is as I tell you. The weak person ties their neck with a tourniquet until a vein appears. Then take the instrument called a lancet.
Place the prominent point which is on the head of the lancet upon the vein itself and strike it with a pestle or some other thing in this manner; and let the blood flow according to the quantity that you wish. Then loosen the constriction of the weak person and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide bloodletting. However, it must not be of a pointed extremity, like the other bloodlettings, but rather of a wide extremity. And let the bloodletting be across it, because the mouth is nearby and perhaps the lancet will break when it is slender.
But the benefit of the two veins which are in the two lacrimal glands of the two eyes is for the eye from scab and redness; and swelling in the eye and illnesses of the face.
And the quality of their bloodletting is that the weak person ties their neck with a tourniquet. Then let them be bled; and you stand above their head.
And let the bloodletting be across according to a small length with a small lancet, slightly wide, for there is no flesh in that place. For if the lancet is of a slender extremity it may perhaps break.
Then let as much blood run out as is necessary for you. And place a compress over them and bind them one night and loosen them.
Phlebotomy of the veins of the nose is indicated for acute fever, and violent headache, and facial ailments such as hemiplegia, and redness which occurs on the face; especially when it is longstanding.
And the quality of this phlebotomy is such that the weak person should constrict their own neck. Then hold their nose with your left hand and take a thin, long phlebotomy instrument; and fix it in the middle of the nostril itself, between that which divides the nose according to its straightness. For the vein does not appear to the sense there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you hold your hand with the phlebotomy instrument for a little while; and let the blood run according to your need. Then bind it with one ligature at night. For it restores itself quickly.
Indeed the benefit of phlebotomy of the two temples is for constriction of breathing and the beginning of leprosy and melancholic ailments and impetigo and bad ulcers and corrosive ones, that is, summer boils.
And the quality of this phlebotomy is such that the weak person should bind under both temples on their neck with a bandage; and the practitioner should stand over the head of the weak person, and the weak person should be sitting on a seat.
Then the phlebotomy instrument cuts the vein lengthwise for a short time. Then extract a temperate amount of blood, or according to that which you see is necessary for it. Then do similarly with the other vein.
Afterwards loosen the bandage and constrict the two veins in the middle; so that the weak person does not become feverish. And let it be until morning. For the wound heals.
Concerning the Phlebotomy of Veins by Algeberich. Veins however are helpful for phlebotomy according to Algeberich: because they contribute to alcohol in the mouth, and corruption of the gum; and bad ulcers: and fissures of the lips, and bad ulcers, even those which are in the nose, and around it.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is that you make the weak person sit before you: and constrict his neck with a band. Then turn back his lip and look at the two veins: of which one is on the right of the lip, and the second on the left: and the demonstration of both is through the blackness of both. And this is true: because around them are small, black veins. Therefore incise both with an intersecting incision.
But if there is doubt upon you and you do not know which one it is: then attend to the incision of the larger one, and the more manifest one.
And do likewise with the two veins that are on the upper lip: and indeed most often this is done with it: and incise the two veins that are on the lower lip.
Concerning Phlebotomy of the Vein under the Tongue after Phlebotomy of the Cephalic Vein. Indeed the phlebotomy of the two veins that are under the tongue is helpful for: phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is for inflammations that are in the throat and pain of the uvula and pains of the mouth.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is that the weak person sits between your hands before the sun: and elevate his tongue, and look under his tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for a vein: and the color of both tends towards blackness.
Therefore phlebotomize both: and take care that you do not put your hand into them while phlebotomizing. For under it are arteries, and perhaps blood flow may occur from those arteries.
However, there are three veins in the elbow which are phlebotomized, and the practice of phlebotomy occurs in these in all people.
And phlebotomy of them is performed according to two methods. Either by puncturing with a broad myrtle phlebotomy knife; or an olive-wood one sharpened to a point; or by cutting with a surgical knife, and it is called Alnesil. And this is its form.
And among them are broad and fine ones according to the width of the veins and their firmness. The myrtle phlebotomy knife is broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave veins, full ones, those coming forth, apparent, large, and those which contain thick, cloudy blood.
And this phlebotomy knife is of lesser width and with a finer point.
And it is suitable for phlebotomy of fine veins which contain thin, yellow blood
The Basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, is favorable for phlebotomy. Because it draws blood from illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from that which follows the chest and abdomen; and it behooves the phlebotomist to take care when phlebotomizing it, and to be fearful of the artery beneath it, for an artery is situated underneath. Therefore if he errs and adds to the puncture during phlebotomy, he will strike that artery and blood flow will occur. Therefore it is necessary for the phlebotomist not to puncture the Basilic vein with a puncturing phlebotomy knife but rather to cut it with an Alnesil.
If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary that someone else expose it and change its position to another one, or seek another branch of it; or use a phlebotome in place of it on the arm. For it itself is manifest and cut it with the phlebotome Anesil as we have said.
But if you wish to perform phlebotomy on it itself then it is necessary before the constriction of the arm that you touch the place until you recognize the place of the pulse. Then mark the place with a cautery, afterwards constrict the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision with the phlebotome Anesil as we have said. And be careful that percussion does not fall through the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, as you are tightening the ligature, swelling in the place that you marked with a cautery, then that swelling is the swelling of an artery; therefore avoid it.
For if you see the blood rise at the phlebotomy like the urine of a child and it is thin, reddish blood; then know that it is from arterial blood. Then hasten, and place your finger over the long space, then remove your finger. If the bleeding is cut off (for it often is) then constrict the arm and release it; and let the weak person be warned against movement; and let there be fear over them.
And let them not move for several days until they are healed.
But if the bleeding is not cut off and overcomes you and there is no medicine present to you at that moment then cut into the artery if it appears to you. For its extremities are contracted and the blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of a pistachio nut and grind it; take one half and constrict it over the place of the vein with a decent constriction, with a ligature and poultices up to another day.
Then if blood is shed, it is good. And if not: then care for it with him whose memory preceded from the place of the powders cutting off the flow of blood: and the incision of his blood is not difficult in many positions, because of the small place of the wound, and the possibility of a ligature from the arm.
The phlebotomy of the Basilic vein is beneficial because it draws blood from both the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; therefore, because it is composed of a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Cephalica, as we have said.
And it behooves the phlebotomist to be above fear from its phlebotomy, for beneath it is a nerve. If therefore he adds in the puncture of the phlebotomy, and finds a nerve, numbness occurs in it, and healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it does not heal completely.
And this nerve often appears to the senses. But if it is hidden in some people and is subtle and does not show: then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy where the phlebotomist cuts a section with Alnesil, and avoids the nerves with your strength.
If the vein is between two nerves, then find the vein in length.
On the Cephalic Vein. The phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein is beneficial because it draws blood from the head and contributes to eye diseases.
And it is necessary in this vein especially if you wish, that the phlebotomist punctures it with an olive phlebotome or a myrtle phlebotome; because it is the safest of all veins, since there is neither an artery nor a nerve beneath it.
However, it behooves you at the time of phlebotomy to avoid with the phlebotome only the head of the lizard, and seek a gentler spot; and it does not harm it if it is not found with the first percussion, so that he repeats over it with the phlebotomy several times.
However, perhaps an abscess may form in some people when bloodletting is not performed in the first percussion, but it does not harm them to have such an abscess somewhere.
On the benefit of bloodletting and the cautions to be observed therein. The quality of bloodletting, however, and its accidents arising from it, in whose correction you ought to think.
First, therefore, it is of which you ought to know, that bloodletting is not administered except in the preservation of health and its continuance, and caution from the arrival of illnesses.
And that bloodletting should be done in one of three veins which are in the elbow, namely, the Cephalic, and the Fusca, and the Basilica. And that bloodletting should be done at the beginning of Spring on a fast, in Spring and Summer; and Autumn and Winter after a meal, when the signs of repletion appear.
And let bloodletting be done on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed since the day began.
Bloodletting which is administered in illnesses does not have a determined hour; but when it provokes suitability and necessity for that, at night or day, and in every hour, and in every time.
And children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled except from the fourth month up to the seventh. Nor should old men who have already passed sixty years be bled.
When, therefore, someone wishes to be bled for whatever reason: it is necessary that he cleanse his bowels before bloodletting with a gentle enema if there is much retained feces in them so that the veins do not draw away foul and harmful superfluities from the intestines to the principal members during bloodletting.
Nor should a patient be bled when he has surfeit, that is, sickening fullness; nor when drunk, nor recovering from drunkenness until that is removed from them. Nor a weak person with dropsy.
And let bloodletting be avoided again after a colic attack, and vomiting, and diarrhea, and multiplication of intercourse, and labor, and exertion, and wakefulness, and fasting, and everything that dissolves virtue from the bodily or animal thing.
Then consider in the subtlety of humors before that, if blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines: if possible to him do that.
Then let him enter a bath, if nothing prohibits him from it: or let him exercise with some exercise, so that the blood becomes thinner.
And place his bloodletting in the chest, around the third hour of the day, as we said: and try, that his chest be evacuated that day from all animal evils accidents: such as distress, and anger, and fear: and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and pain, and excessive intercourse, and those like them.
And let the things customary for his dwelling be present with whose use men ran custom from fragrance, and scent, and rejoicing and those like them by as much quantity as possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion which is higher than the cushion of the person undergoing bloodletting; afterwards let him extract his arm and rub it with his hand twice or thrice. Then let him tie a ligature with a bandage, and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be temperate, for when the constriction is unequal, or by excess in the constriction then it hinders the course of blood: or in slackness, then it also hinders the course of blood.
Then after the constriction it is necessary that the phlebotomist rubs his hands together at the same time until the veins swell and declare themselves to the sense.
Then let the phlebotomist cleanse the place to be lanced with a little oil, properly an old one.
Then he should place the index finger of his left hand upon the very vein under the place where he desires to perform the phlebotomy, slightly: so that the vein does not flee, and the percussion may be made. For since some veins are like a fleeing chord at phlebotomy and some are full of windiness; and when you place the phlebotome upon them, they contract under the phlebotome, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and the phlebotome does not open the vein; and if it opens it, it is strict: therefore on account of this, it is necessary that the phlebotomist be firm and diligent in all these matters.
Then he should lay down the phlebotome. If therefore the vein is opened from one place, then it is good. And if not, then repeat that at another place a little below or above it with haste: if the place is not apostatized.
But if it is apostatized or becomes weak and swollen, then dismiss him for a day or two and do not bind the ligature. For it might perhaps draw a hot abscess. Nor let him enter a bath.
Then let him repeat the phlebotomy if he desires.
But if the opening is small, or the blood runs subtly; and you fear that the quantity of blood will not go out which you wish: then repeat the phlebotome in the same hole according to straightness, and add a little in the operation.
And do that with haste before the place is apostatized; for an apostatized place is in many men at a small operation.
But if you see that it is already apostatized, then do not repeat upon it at all, for nothing excuses this to you. And put something from the dregs of oil upon it; because it relieves the course of blood. And it is better in this place than oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly apply the feces of oil in all your phlebotomies, which phlebotomists do well at the stopping of the course of blood; and they do this with tyriaca, alfarach, and sagzenea, when one of them is placed upon the place.
For blood is thinned and resolved when it is thick.
And if it should happen that a large abscess occurs at the place of phlebotomy, for this often happens, especially to one who is not a phlebotomist except for that vein, or the opening of the vein is small, then hurry and put a sponge infused in water and salt, heated a little, upon the place; and bind it for an hour, for it will be dissolved.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has come out from the vein itself, with its complement, or from another vein.
And if something of blackness, or greenness should remain in the place after a few days, it does no harm; and if you wish, put something of aloe, or myrrh dissolved upon it: or something from the juice of mint, that is, calamint or something like it.
And indeed abscesses and prominences often occur at phlebotomy in the Basilica.
Therefore put your hand upon it; then if you find softness under your pressure upon it, then that prominence is bad. Therefore beware, that you put something of what we said upon it. For perhaps arterial blood will flow from it.
But make a plaster for it with that in which there is astringency, so that the place hardens. Then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you draw from every man blood according to the quantity of his strength. And what appears from the color conquering over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it go out until it reddens; and similarly if you see it thick, then let it go until it becomes thin; and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is taken away.
And it is necessary for him who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting with one cup, that the phlebotomy of the vein be enlarged, and the phlebotomist be wide; and in him who is weak, let it be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary that he extract it in cups, and that the hole be narrow; and better in phlebotomy of the vein is what is administered, that there be a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of phlebotomy is safe from the flow of blood, and from the cutting of a nerve; and it is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy by width and length.
And for whom syncope is accustomed to happen at bloodletting, it is necessary that he eat before phlebotomy a small loaf soaked in pomegranate juice water; or secaniabin if it is heated; and you should draw from him three or four cups of blood.
And if he is of a cold complexion, then he should take before phlebotomy bread soaked in syrup of mue, or in syrup of honey spiced with spices, or in fragrant aromatic syrup.
If syncope should happen at bloodletting and the cause of it is excess blood loss then it is necessary that he drink meat broth and a subtle fragrant syrup; and use aromatics with agarwood and line his chest with them; and use the other things which we have mentioned in the division in the chapter on syncope from evacuation.
He who wishes to spare his arm and to let out his blood a second time; it is necessary for him whose bloodletting is for great evacuation, and whose strength is weak, that he should let out blood gradually by the amount of his strength on continuous days.
He who wishes to experiment on his own arm and to let out his blood a second time, and whose body is strong, then he should do this after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting.
But he who wishes to draw blood from his body to the opposite side to which it leans, ought to have it done to him on the second or third day.
But in him whose body has much blood already heated and sharpened, and it causes a fever to happen: it is necessary that the blood should go out from it at once, and a large quantity be drawn from it, and the opening be enlarged until fainting occurs: after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting and so that you put your hand upon his pulse along the course of the blood, so that death does not happen in place of fainting. Very often this happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs.
And it is not necessary when you wish to open the arm and let out blood a second time, since the opening of the vein has already closed and the blood's exit becomes difficult, to press upon it with violence and quickly straighten it. For that is very bad indeed, rather let it alone until a second bloodletting is performed.
Or remove with sharpness of the phlebotomist's tool that which has been clotted from the blood in the opening of the vein and put something dissolved salt upon it. And put something from Tyrian agaric or wormwood on it and apply a soft compress until the blood flows out.
But if the vein has already become inflamed, then leave it alone and do not press upon it until the inflammation subsides.
But if necessity compels to let out blood a second time and there is no excuse, then either the phlebotomist should perform it above that place or on another arm in another vein.
The vein-cutting place for the arm is on the side of the elbow, and on the wrist, if they are not found hidden, because it is composed of them.
And the quality of its vein-cutting is such that the sick person should insert his hand into warm water until the arm reddens and the vein appears manifestly.
Then it should be bound upon it with a ligament with a little medium constriction. Then the vein-cutting should be done transversely little by little, not laterally nor according to length, and let the opening be wide, and let your vein-cutting be such that you do it above the junction of the hand a little.
And if the flow of blood is prohibited, then immerse the hand again in warm water and let the blood run into the water until you achieve your purpose.
But if you are in the summer days, then excuse yourself from immersing the hand in warm water again. And indeed the course of blood is put mostly into warm water in the time of winter.
And the vein-cutting of this vein is safer than all veins because there is no pulsating vein nor nerve under it.
But vein-cutting of the Saluatella vein on the right hand brings relief to illnesses of the liver.
And the quality of its vein-cutting is such that you should bind the inner part of the hand with a ligament, or your own hand, after you have immersed it in warm water until the vein swells and is shown distinctly to the senses. Then cut the vein transversely. And if you happen to cut it entirely, it will not harm her in any way.
And preserve, that you do not finish your hand with the vein-cutting. For under it are the nerves of the fingers exposed from flesh.
Then immerse the hand again into warm water and let the blood run into it. For if you do not repeatedly immerse it in warm water, the blood clots in the vein's opening and prevents its course.
Therefore, when you have extracted the necessary amount from the blood, then place oil and salt upon the vein so that it does not quickly solidify.
And similarly, it is necessary to do this with every narrow branch.
The performance of its phlebotomy from the left hand is: because it brings relief to spleen diseases: and similarly do this in its phlebotomy just as you did in the second equally.
But the benefit of phlebotomy from the saphenous vein is for diseases that are in the lower part of the body, such as diseases of the womb, and retention of menses, and infirmities of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar diseases.
