And the quality of its phlebotomy is that the sick person should place their foot in warm water and rub it until the veins protrude; and bind above the joint of the foot with a tourniquet. And indeed the location of the veins is near the heel, apparent towards the thumb; and there branch out from it many branches on the surface of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in a larger branch of it, or at the heel in its cluster; because this is better and safer.
But if you phlebotomize it on the surface of the foot, then be careful of the nerves which are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And place your incision for phlebotomy across it, as if you wished to cut it; and let the phlebotomist be Nesil.
But if the flow of blood is impeded, then immerse their foot again in warm water and let the blood run into it until it is evacuated.
But if the phlebotomist misses the vein at first, then repeat the phlebotomy a little higher up, for this location is safe and you need not fear any harm from it when you are careful of the nerves, as we have said.
And likewise with the saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The location of the sciatic veins, as we said, is at the heel from the outer side.
And the benefit of its phlebotomy is for pain in the buttock when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is for the weak one to enter a bath and hurry; and to bind his leg from the side of the buttock up above the heel with four fingers using a thin, long strip: for it does not appear except because of that.
Therefore when it appears, then make a phlebotomy on it according to whatever skill is possible for you, or according to transversal cutting; and it is better either that you strike it with an incision or divide it by sectioning. For its location is safer, and it itself is very hidden in most people.
For its location is safer, and it itself is very hidden in most people. And if you do not find it and it does not appear to the senses at all, then make a phlebotomy on one of its branches, and that is the one which appears on the back of the foot and towards the auricle; and be careful of the nerves, and let a quantity of blood run out as much as you wish.
Then loosen the constriction and put skin over the place of the phlebotomy and bind the place. For it heals quickly.
On Cupping and the Quality of its Use
Cups are made from horns, and from metal, and from glass.
And cupping indeed is done according to two methods. With scarification and the extraction of blood: and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification is according to two methods. For either it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And there are fourteen places in the body where cupping is administered with scarification and bloodletting. Of these, one is Ventosatio Alnocrati, and it is the back of the head; and Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck; and the cuppings of Alhagdahim, and they are two plates of the neck from both sides together; and the cupping of the mind, and it is under the jaw in the lower part of the mouth; and the cupping of two spatulas; and the cupping of Alachosos on top of the end of the tailbone; and the cupping of two focilium, and they are the middle of the two arms; and the cupping of two legs; and the cupping of two calves.
And cupping indeed does not extract blood from you through the fine veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore its virtue does not fall away from them, as is the case with it from phlebotomy. Nor should cupping be administered either with fire or without fire in any diseases that arise from repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
And if necessity compels cupping from illness or for custom, we administer it at any hour in the beginning of the month, at its end, and in the middle of it, and at any time it may be.
For it is because some people when blood multiplies within them until they need its extraction through cupping, find heaviness and a headache in their head.
And there are some of them who find repletion and redness in their face, head, and neck. And there are some of them who find itching on their face and forehead, darkness, and dullness in their eyes.
And there are some of them who scratch the place of their cupping. And there are some of them whose laughter increases. And there are some who find the taste of blood in their mouth, or their tongue swells and sheds blood. And among them is one whose sleep is multiplied. And among them is he who sees in his dream blood and redness, and those who are dead, and wounds, and things which are similar to these.
Therefore when we see something of these things, and especially if it is in the third part of the month, we order then that venesection be done, after two hours or three have passed from the day.
The advantage of venesection of the neck is that it brings gravity to the head, and what is poured out goes to the eyes. But truly it is necessary that this be done after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this indeed is a venesection in place of cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted that one with a cold complexion, or who has catarrh, use it. For it brings him great harm.
And because of this, it is not necessary that old men use it, and those in whose head are cold illnesses.
But whoever habitually does this upon himself, forgetfulness arises in him.
And because of this, it is necessary that you order the phlebotomist to lower his hand a little with the venesection towards the lower parts, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
Venesection or cupping is in place of brown phlebotomy and basilica phlebotomy, and because of this it brings relief to asthma, and constriction of shortness of breath, and fissure of the instrument of breathing, and cough, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the cupping be applied for a short time, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Venesection on the temples brings relief to pains happening in the head, and ophthalmia, and hemicrania, and perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth, and it is in place of basilica phlebotomy.
And it is necessary that you instruct the one who draws blood not to press with his hand during scarification so that he does not strike an artery and cause bleeding.
Bloodletting under the chin gathers humors in the mouth and for the inflammation of the gums and similar illnesses which are in the mouth; and it stands in place of phlebotomy in other parts which are on the two lips.
Bloodletting with spoons gathers for the phlegmatic humor which comes from reflux and heat.
Bloodletting with two cupping glasses gathers the benefit of phlebotomy from the veins the Basilica, the Fusca and the Cephalic; because it draws blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh and they draw from other larger veins until the drawing reaches those three veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the one who draws blood not to proceed much in scarification because the place is stripped of flesh and under it are nerves and arteries.
A cup which is cupped on a swollen part gathers for hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the one who draws blood that the cup be large and that it be of metal because the place needs strong suction and perhaps slender cups will break and it should be scarified with a large scarification.
Cupping glasses on the legs diminishes noticeably; because they draw blood from the whole body and they gather for old pains in the kidneys, the womb and the bladder; they provoke menses, and they gather phlegm and mucus; and they stand in place of phlebotomy of two saphenous veins and the femoral vein.
However they weaken the body greatly and cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit of cupping on the femoral vein is close to the benefit of cupping on two legs.
The quality of the position of the cupping glasses is to place them first empty and suck with temperate suction; and do not prolong the application of the cupping glasses but put them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors may come to the place by equal advent; and do not cease to repeat and continue this until you see the place already reddening and swelling, and the redness of blood appears; then scarify and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider in the arrangement of bodies.
For he who is of men of tender flesh, rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once, no more: so that the place is not ulcerated.
And instruct the one who applies cupping glasses to enlarge the scarification and deepen it a little; and to temper the suction in ease, speed, and gentle motion.
But if there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary that you scarify him twice. For in the first indeed a way is opened for the refinement of the blood and its aqueousness; and in the second indeed a thorough examination for the exquisite extraction of thick blood.
But if the blood is very feculent, then let there be a third scarification to follow the ultimate.
And lastly, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or a little, one scarification suffices us. But if we wish for much bloodletting, let us scarify with many scarifications.
And if we have measured that the blood is thick: then it is necessary that it be scarified with a deep scarification; and the equal end in scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is it that ought to be administered from oils at the application of cupping glasses and what should the one applying cupping glasses beware of?
Now in him indeed whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, it is necessary that the places for cupping be anointed with opening, softening, resolving oils: if it is in summer time, such as oil of alkana or oil of violets, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of pumpkin seeds.
And if it is in the time of winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or syrup: and those like them.
But if the excess is thick, cold: then let the oil be bitter almond, or balm of Gilead oil, or white oil, or anise oil: and those like them.
But if the pores are to be opened on delicate flesh, then it is necessary that it be forbidden from oil, and it is necessary for them to wash the places of the openings after opening with rose water, or with cold water, or with solar water, or with cucumber water, or with purslane water: and those like them.
But he whose blood is of much humidity, let him wash the openings with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac: and those like them.
But he whose excesses are thick, let him wash his openings with old wine, or with water of samshoot, or a decoction of anise, or chamomile: and those like them.
And it is necessary that cupping be done in a bath, or in the vestige of a bath, indeed it is necessary that it be administered after exiting from the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not necessary for anyone to sleep after cupping.
What is it by which cupping, or phlebotomy should be regulated before cupping?
And after it is necessary for you to consider first: then if the cupping or phlebotomy is choleric, and acuity and inflammation overcome his blood: then it is necessary that he take refrigerants, such as pomegranate rinds: and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and chicory, and jujubes: and those like them.
And make his foods chicken, and eggs, and foods seasoned with sourness: and those like them.
And for him whose constitution is cold, it is necessary that he take in drink syrup of honey, or of almond, or of asafetida made from seeds; and let him take spiced wine which is medium, that is between old and new; and let it be instructed that he use scarcity of food. And let his food be chickens, and sparrows; and finches, that is, small birds; and squabs of pigeons he should eat.
And it is necessary that drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting be more than food; and it is necessary that there be given in drink to some men in the hours tyriaca alfaroch, or a medicine from musk, that is, musk, or alesselithe before cupping; and before bloodletting, or after: so that the principal limbs may be strengthened, and the blood thinned. And it is not necessary that they take it in heated drink.
Concerning Cupping Without Scarification. But cuppings which are without scarification are those which are placed upon the liver, and spleen, and gouty foot, and upon the belly, navel, and places of the kidneys, and the narrow part of the thigh; because these members do not suffer scarification upon themselves.
And there is not sought through them except either attraction of blood from a member to a member; just as we place the cupping glass upon the nipples in the sickness of a nosebleed. And we use them so that we may loosen from a member invisible coldness and wind in a member; just as we place the cupping glass upon the belly and navel. For it itself rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes the pain therefore because it dissolves that windiness.
It is also placed upon the kidneys when obstruction or a stone occurs in them. For by its power of attraction it perhaps opens the obstruction, or extracts the stone from its place.
And he does the same when it is placed upon the liver, and the spleen in windy weather to ward off wind in both.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with incision only. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in pleural diseases; and that is, that the cupping glass be filled and large, with warm water alone, or water in which herbs suitable for the purpose have been boiled.
The form of a cupping glass administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be two open fingers, according to what we have described; and let its depth be half a palm; and let there be a small hole on its side in a place which is approximately its middle, according to the size that a needle can enter; make it from brass or copper, with thick edges, smooth, equal, polished; so that it does not injure the limb upon which it is placed. And let there be a transverse rod of metal, or iron, in its middle, where a candle with fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, greater than that which we have described, and a little smaller than that, according to the diseases and age of him who uses it.
For cupping glasses for children and weaklings are different than cupping glasses for men and those with large bodies.
The manner of placing this cupping glass with fire upon a limb is to light a wick with fire from linen properly, or a small candle from wax; and place it upon the middle of the transverse rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass; so that the ignited fire be approximately beneath the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the body of the sick person is not burned.
Then place it upon the limb; and let a finger be upon the hole which I mentioned, until whenever we hold the cupping glass: when we need to, let us remove the finger, and let vapor escape through that hole, and the cupping glass will immediately be released.
Then apply fire to the ligature according to its manner, and repeat it if it is insufficient.
But the cupping glass which is applied in pleurisy with water has neither a transverse stick nor a hole, and it is not applied unless it is filled with water and placed only upon the limb.
And this is its form.
And the larger this cupping glass is, so that it can hold a great amount of water, the better it is.
On the suspension of bloodletting instruments.
Bloodletting instruments are not administered in many places except those where the positioning of cupping glasses is not possible due to their small size, such as the lip, and gum, and those similar to them. Or on a limb denuded of flesh, such as a finger, and nose, and those similar to them.
And the quality of using them is: that you should take it from bloodletting instruments which are in fresh waters, clean from putrefactions.
Then let them be dismissed day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body should first be evacuated with bloodletting and cupping. Then wash the limb of the sick person until it reddens. Then place them upon it.
Therefore when they are filled and fall, and suction of the place is possible with a cupping glass, then it is best for healing; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, rub and squeeze it.
If the flow of blood continues after the fall of the bloodletting instruments, and it is oozing; then infuse a linen cloth in cold water and place it on top until the oozing stops.
If the blood multiplies, then sprinkle powdered saffron or galls and those similar to them from astringents upon it until the blood stops.
Or peeled beans are placed on the spot and left until they adhere to the place for the blood is cut off.
And if repetition of the cupping is necessary, when it is possible, change them.
But if leeches are forbidden from suspension, then wash the place with fresh blood. Or stick a needle into the places until something comes out of the blood. Then place them, for they themselves when they feel something from the blood, adhere immediately.
When therefore you wish them to fall off, sprinkle over them something from ground aloe, or salt, or ashes, and they will fall off immediately.
This Chapter is also very often necessary in the Art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know you sons, that foolish doctors and the common people boast about this Chapter who have never looked at an ancient book nor read a letter from it.
And because of this reason, this method is private from the sciences in our land. And I never encountered anything entirely good in it and I have not attained from it what I attained except for the length of reading ancient books and study upon understanding them until I extracted knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and study throughout the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this Chapter all that contains my knowledge and on which my experiment passed after I shortened it for you and freed it from the occupation of elongation and shortened it without shortening and showed it without declination: and I formed in it many forms from the forms of instruments which are administered in it, with addition by declination, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is fitting that we say in the prologue of this chapter, summaries and chapters, to whose understanding the craftsman is compelled at first to attain: namely so that you and those who are ready may know or learn this noble art and teach it to others.
Therefore I say that when a fracture or dislocation or torsion or fall happens to someone, then it is necessary for you to hurry first to phlebotomize him and loosen him, or both together: if nothing forbids it such as weakness of strength, or he who suffers from one of these things is a child or decrepit old man, or the time is one of intense heat or intense cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables and poultry and kid meats; and forbid wine, fatty meats, and all foods that fill the veins with blood. Until when you are safe from hot abscesses and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb; then let the invalid return to his first usual diet with which he was accustomed to run.
Therefore when a broken bone begins to be restored, it is necessary for the invalid to eat nourishing foods of much nourishment, coarse and heavy, in which there is viscosity: such as foods made from boiled wheat with water, rice, heads and feet and bellies of cows, eggs, and fresh fish; and thick wine, and things like them. For by this regimen coagulation of the fracture is done faster and better.
And know that broken bones when they are in veins which are in the first old age and decrepit, it is not possible for them to be continued and consolidated according to their nature always, because of the desiccation of their limbs; and those are consolidated and continued which are from them at the end of tenderness, such as the bones of small children.
Nevertheless, nature attaches to a fractured bone from every side something like glue, in which there is thickness, so that it adheres and binds itself until they are joined together and are united until it comes to an end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its own operations; and for this reason it is necessary that you place nourishment for the weak foods, in which there are heaviness, and viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that fractures are of different kinds, according to the diversity of members; for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the breastbone is different from a fracture of the spine bone; and similarly all the other members are different from each other. And the kinds of bone fractures are also diversified within themselves.
For a fracture of it is arched, besides which stars occur in it. And sometimes a fracture of it occurs along the length of the bone; and there are fractures of it that are sagittal, that is, stars, and additions proceeding, as are in a reed pen, when a knife is fitted for writing. And not proceeding; and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head; and there is a small fissure fracture.
And every kind has its own ingenuity or instrument for its restoration, according to what will come to remembrance of it, distinguished in its places.
And from what a bone fracture is known is its crookedness and prominence; and the appearance of it to the sense, and the sound of it when you press it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor a sound at your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, nor does the weak one find great pain; then there is no fracture there, rather it is possible that there is crookedness, or a small fissure: therefore, it is not necessary that you move it with your hand and compression altogether, rather place upon it from medicines, of which remembrance comes, after that moment which suits the place; then let it be bound with a fine bandage.
And know that when a place arches and is split into two, except for what may happen to it in terms of satagiae, nevertheless every part is removed from its companion: then it is necessary for you to begin at your hour towards its rectification by evening it out before a hot apostema occurs to it.
But if a hot apostema should occur to it, then let it alone for several days until the apostema becomes quiet. Then even it out according to any way possible from gentleness and ingenuity.
And know that its restoration and evening out is easier than bone, in which satagiae occur.
Then bind it up according to what will come to your recollection of it.
But if there are satagiae in bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from its two parts, if it is a hand or foot; or with your hand, if the limb is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb upon an equal place, according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the broken limb are extended: then try to make those additions return with all your ingenuity and according to any way you can in their places; and be careful according to your strength, lest pain or injury should occur upon the weak one through your operation: and try, according to your strength, to apply one of the two bones with its companion according to the better forms.
And it is necessary at that hour for you to try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see anything different there, correct it and even it out according to the amount of your strength.
And be careful of vehement extension and strong compression, as many stupid people do. Very often they cause a hot apostema or contraction in the limb to occur through their operation: just as we have already seen that very often from their operations.
Then apply after equalization and a decent operation and constriction of that member and rest and quietness: and warn the sick person about it. And make him beware, lest he move it in the hour of his watchings and his sleep at its turning and its agitation and at its excretion and all its motions with its last strength. And that he beware lest its position be a position with which pain follows. Which is because when he feels pain or injury in the arrangement of the position of the member then it is necessary that he change it to another arrangement from which he does not feel pain.
And beware with him that its position be equal and straight so that crookedness may not happen in the member when it is restored.
And the quality of the constriction of the fractured member is according to what I have said.
Know that fractured members are diversified in their smallness and their largeness and their shape.
Which therefore of them are small such as the arm and the finger and the knee joint and similar to these then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, subtle cloth. But which of them are large such as the hip and the leg then it is necessary that there be a broad, hard wrapping upon them.
Because a broad bandage connects a large member and tightens it from every part with equal constriction so that a blemish does not enter it.
Then begin after your evacuation from equalization to put a suitable plaster upon it on soft lint over the place of its fracture. Then begin to wrap the bandage upon the place of its fracture with three or four wrappings according to what the member deserves and constrict your hand a little with the bandage.
Then go with it to a part above the place of the fracture and constrict less than was your constriction with which you constricted the fractured place. Then lengthen it with inversion from the place of fracture a little, and soften the constriction gradually, until you take a large thing from a healthy place.
Then take another strip, and again wrap it over the fractured place with inversions. Afterwards go with inversion to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in the constriction of inversion, and its softening, according to what we said in the first upper inversion.
Then put between the inversions from soft tow, or cloth, with which the crookedness of the fracture may be equalized, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then wrap another strip over it itself. Afterwards equal over these inversions stars well-made immediately, if there is not inflation in the place, nor hot abscess.
But if there is inflation or abscess in it: then put over it that which sits upon the abscess and removes the swelling; and let it be so for several days. Then bind stars over it at that time.
And let the stars be from halves of broad cane, planed, wisely shaped; or let them be from wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or palm, that is, branches of palm, or calamus; and let the width of every star be according to this form, and this same figure.
However it is necessary that the star which is put over the very ligature be somewhat thicker and broader than the other stars; and the length of the star shall be according to what is permitted by the limb in size and smallness.
Then bind over the stars with another strip, according to the same first binding. Then bind over it with threads well-made, according to what we said concerning the binding; and it is that your binding be over the fractured place more; and the further it is lengthened from the fracture, let the binding be less.
And it is necessary that they are threads medium in thickness and fineness, and are from soft flax.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the practice of foolish people putting their threads from twisted flax cords, it is a great error: because constriction falls with them beyond quality.
And slender threads again are unsuitable: because you do not achieve through constriction with them what you wish.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger between star and star.
If the weak person is injured through the extremities of the stars after constriction in healthy places: then put under them from soft tow, or carded wool, until nothing is injured from it.
When however there is a fracture with a wound, and disruption occurs in the skin, I will come with remembrance of it in the second chapter singularity.
And know that not every fractured limb ought to be constricted with stars from the first day. For which is because when the limb is large, it is not necessary that you place stars upon it except after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the event of hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, with which the ancients restored, which are placed upon fracture and dislocation.
Method of a common shortened plaster, restoring fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women, and boys: because it is from it on account of which neither heat nor cold overcomes.
And it is, that you take from the powder of a mill, and it is the marrow of flour which hangs in the walls of the mill at the motion of the millstone: and you make it just as it is, except that you sift it with bran: and put its confection neither thick nor thin. Then use it.
Method of another Plaster, restoring fracture, and dislocation, and torsion. Take almonds, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugwort; and juice of all, each ten drachms, myrrh and aloes each five drachms and alethil twenty drachms, let all be ground and sifted, and mix with alethil water. Or with egg white if the complexion of the patient is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, it restores broken bones quickly, and it benefits many people.
A different method for a plaster, which also benefits fractures and dislocations. Take mugwort, mess, and white alteae, all ten drachms each, myrrh and aloes both five drachms each, acacia six drachms, armenian bole twenty drachms, grind all together thoroughly, sift them, and make them into a plaster with water or egg white.  And apply it.
A method for a plaster for joints and separated bones from their places; and it eases pain that occurs to them, and bone division, and fracture. Take boiled wool and submerge it in vinegar and boiled oil, and place it on the area.
And in this plaster there is no power of restoration, but it is good for easing a hot abscess and expels pain properly.
A method for a plaster which restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the deaf fig and leaves of wild poppy; grind all together and make a plaster from both.
A shortened method for a plaster administered for the restoration of a bone fracture; and when you want the resolution of remaining abscesses. Take root of altaea, chamomile, violet flowers, and herb flour, all equal parts; grind all together and make them into a plaster with wine if the limb is not heated.
But if it is heated then make it with humid coriander water or with water and use it.
A stronger method for a plaster to resolve this [first] one is when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of a limb. Take equal parts of the root of althea, flaxseed, balaustium, melilot, samsuck, violets flowers, and chamomile. Grind all together, and make them into a paste with willow water, or sweet water, or wine. All of this according to the heat of the limb and the rest of its warmth.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration except a plaster made from wax and oil; nothing else; and he narrated that it should be a medium between thickness and thinness.
But Galen thought that things should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration which have a drying effect of heat, such as myrrh, aloes and frankincense, and similar ones from those we have mentioned.
Method of a plaster that brings together injury and pain. Take equal parts of mugwort, chickpea flour, human hair clippings or bird feathers, althea and salt; grind and sift them, and make a plaster from it.
The quantity of this plaster, by which the bandage should remain, then be loosened, is such that you consider whether it does not happen to the weak pain, nor itching, nor the fractured bone moves from its place; then do not loosen it for many days. But if intense itching occurs to him in that place, or disturbing pain, or swelling; then hurry and loosen it in an hour, and remove the plaster from him; and take a soft cloth, or a soft, moist marine sponge. Submerge it in warm water and wash the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain settles.
But if intense itching occurs to him in that place, or disturbing pain, or swelling; then hurry and loosen it in an hour, and remove the plaster from him; and take a soft cloth, or a soft, moist marine sponge. Submerge it in warm water and wash the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain settles. Then let the limb rest for an hour. Then place upon it wool cut up, dipped in vinegar and oil, or rose oil; and bind it on for one night, until you are safe from hot abscesses, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is removed.
Then repeat it to a subtle stricture, and a small plaster; and do not constrict it with your first constriction, and subtle with it, until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all accidents are now removed altogether, and it no longer needs a plaster and constriction, then repeat it as you did at first equally.
And if nothing of those things which we have said should happen to the limb, then do not untie it except after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when that whole thing is dismissed for twenty days, according to what appears to you from the loosening of the limb, as we said, until when the fracture adheres, and the coagulation of flesh approximates upon it: add also then in constriction more than your entire first constriction; and add also in thickness of food for the weak, according to its previous remembrance.
But if you see the place of the fracture already drying out, and wasting more than it should: then know that nourishment is prohibited from reaching it. Therefore embrocate it with warm water at every time that you untie it, and let it be so in all three days, and relieve the constriction a little.
Through this operation therefore nourishment runs to the limb, and heals quickly.
But that which foolish restorers do from a fracture of a limb with another vice, when it is not restored at first according to what is necessary, and is restored according to tortuosity: this is an error from their operation, and great fear; and if it were right, the ancients would have said it in their books, and they would have operated with it.
And I have not seen any trace of them there at all, and it is better that it not be done.
On the making of an accidental wound on the head.
The kinds of fracture are diverse, and its shapes are many; and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture which occurs from a blow with a sword; and the blow removes the entire mouth until it reaches the syaphne, which is under the bone, just as an axe does to wood; and on account of this, this kind of fracture is called Axial.
Or a sword strikes a certain bone, and strikes its face, and the incision does not penetrate to its very end; and this kind of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Elevation absolute.
And a wound may be of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is comminution, or attrition; and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone, or things similar to these.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which already approaches the panniculus, which is under the bone; or it is in the face of the bone; and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone to the subtlety of a hair; and it is a small fissure, and on account of this, this kind of fracture is called Capillary.
And from them is a fracture which occurs from a fall or a blow with a stone, and things similar to these, which cause a sheet of bone to enter inwards; and a cavity is formed in its place; just as it happens to bellows of air, when they are struck by a blow, and a part of them enters inwards.
And this very often occurs in the heads of moist bones, such as are the heads of children.
And all these kinds of fractures are separated dislocations, and I will come with a recollection of the whole in its place.
And we recognize all these kinds of fractures by examination upon them, and inquiry into them with probes, and removal of corrupted flesh which are upon them.
The capillary kind, however, is recognized by this, that it is detected upon the bone, and it is removed and smoothed upon it with a cautery; for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, consider first the accidents of the patient; then if you see from the accidents that it signifies with manifest meaning upon fear, such as vomiting cholera, extension, and loss of intellect, and abscission of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and egress of eyes, and redness of them, and similar things to these accidents in many constitutions, without a doubt it does not heal; and if you see the accidents are not terrifying to you, and you hope for salvation; then begin its treatment.
Which is, if a wounded person comes to you first when wounded; and it is that in the winter days, then it is necessary for you to study in the removal of bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it is in the summer days, then it is necessary for you to hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone from the fatty tissue is corrupted, and those accidents occur which we have recounted.
Which if the fracture of the bone has already reached the fatty tissue touching the brain, and it is with small pieces, and attraction; then it is necessary for you to cut into the comminuted and crushed part according to what I narrate to you.
And it is for you to shave the head of the wounded person; and expose it upon the bone according to whatever way is possible for you; and according to the shape of the wound and what is light upon the sick person.
If you encounter a flow of blood or a hot abscess at the exposure of bone, then oppose it with what is necessary. And it is, that you fill the place with cloths dipped in wine and rose oil; and make the wound rest, until the abscess subsides, and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin in the care of the bone, and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a sharp-edged Incisor; and this is its form.
Then apply another Incisor after the Incisor, slightly wider than it; and this is its form.
Then apply yet another Incisor, even wider than the second.
And the second method is that you have a number of diverse Incisors; some of which are wider than others, and some of them shorter than others; and that they be in ultimate sharpness at their extremities, and be made from tempered iron or steel; and use facility in percussion upon the Incisor, so that it does not strike the head, and injure it.
But if the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary that you pierce around its circumference, before you apply the Incisors, with Awls which are called non-penetrating Awls; and they are not so named unless because they do not pass through the end of the bone into the flesh beyond it; therefore, because the Awl has a rounded extremity above that which is below its pointed head, similar to a rim, and a small circle prevents submerging and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it behooves you to take a great number of these Awls, each of which suits the quantity of thickness of the bone, until there is present to you an Awl for every skull; whose pointed extremity in length and shortness is according to the quantity of thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of augers, or trepans.
The quality of perforating in a circle around the broken bone is: to place the auger upon the bone and revolve it with your fingers until you know the bone has been penetrated. Then transfer the auger to another place and put between each hole a quantity of thickness equal to the radius, or thereabouts.
Then cut with the incisors what is between each hole from the bone and make it thinner than you can on top of it from the point of lightness, as we said, until you remove the bone either with your hand or with some other instrument that you have prepared for it such as forceps and fine pincers.
And it is necessary that you take all care that the auger or incisor does not touch anything of the spinal cord.
Therefore, when you have gone around the bone perforating and it has been separated from the spinal cord, if it was continuous with it, and you have removed it, it is necessary that you scrape and equalize its extremity which remains in the bone with another similar instrument like an incisor. However, it is necessary that it be thinner and more subtle than other incisors.
And if anything remains of small bones and splinters, then take them with ease with what you have from your instruments. Then take care of the wound after that with linens and ointments as we said.
And another method of operation is easy and far removed from fear which Galen spoke of and praised with great praise and this is his speech. It is necessary for you first to begin to expose the part of bone from the place where the fracture is more violent and manifest until when you have exposed that part, and placed under it the extremity of this lenticular incisor, which has this form.
And the lentil-shaped part, being smooth, does not strike anything; and its sharp part on both sides thereof extending in length, as you see: so that the lentil-shaped part is attached to the bone, its face striking acutely upon the bone.
Then strike upon the Incisorium on one side with a small mallet until you have cut through the whole bone easily, as it turns; and you should be safe from the pouch, so that no accident may happen therein which you fear at all.
And if the physician were not more foolish than men, and more ignorant thereof, nor were he sleeping, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the pouch in some places, he would cut it off therefrom with the extremity of the lentil-shaped part of the instrument itself, and free it therefrom easily. For it is freed therefrom without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate as far as the pouch, and that which is cut off from the bone is its surface, and roughness and subtle burrs remain therein: then it is necessary that you should plane away that roughness, and eradicate those burrs with fine rasps out of which you have prepared a number of diverse sizes, so that it may be possible for you to administer from them in every place the more suitable ones thereof, and straighter ones of them, according to that to which your operation leads you, and the fractured bone itself, and its shape. And it is necessary that you should administer in your reasoning, by which you first plane the bone, the wider ones of those rasps. Then use after that a finer one.
And it is necessary that you should administer in your reasoning, by which you first plane the bone, the wider ones of those rasps. Then use after that a finer one. And do not cease doing that continuously until you arrive at administering the thinnest and most delicate of them all.
But in every one of the remaining small fissures, capillary ones, and subtle fractures, it is necessary that you should administer care according to that which leads to its rectification.
And it is a thing which is not hidden from anyone who has any study in this art, and it stood upon what we have written and shown in large fractures.
But if the exposed subcutaneous tissue is retained when you remove the bone, then it is necessary that you take linen cloth, according to the amount of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth; and do this as easily as you can over that, so that it does not press upon the bone.
Then apply a broad bandage over it; and do not tighten it except by the amount that holds the wound only; and leave it with this care for one day or two days until you are secure from hot abscess. Then untie it and administer afterward medicines with which there is superfluous dryness: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and aristolochia, and those like them; and it is that you make a powder from these medicines, which is sprinkled over the wound as they are dry.
And generally administer to it all medicine of a property which is to cleanse and not bite. And beware according to your ability that the wound in your care is clear; and there is neither dirt of ointment nor filthiness in it.
And beware according to your ability that the wound in your care is clear; and there is neither dirt of ointment nor filthiness in it. Nor allow pus to gather in it at all; for when pus gathers over the cerebral tissue, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and there occurs a great evil upon the sick afterward; and this already happens in the skull when bone is removed from it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness appears on its surface.
Therefore consider then if accidents happen to the sick which we have said, know that they are undoubtedly pernicious.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine which is placed upon it, and it is in the power of that medicine to make it black: then it is necessary that you take one part from honey, three parts from rose oil, and lines with them a cloth. Then place it upon the wound; afterwards care for it with the kinds of treatments which are suitable until it is healed.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except for its two upper parts together, or one of them; because it consists of two bones: since the lower part of it is cartilaginous, it is not broken, and nothing happens to it except attrition, twisting, and bruising.
If one of its two parts is broken, then it is necessary that you insert a small finger into the nostril and equalize that fracture with your index finger and thumb from outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape; and let this be done with skill and ease. And take care that during your operation there does not occur excessive pain upon the weak part.
But if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger does not reach it: then it is necessary that you equalize it with the end of a radius, in which there is a small thickness.
But if the fracture is in both parts, then do as described above; and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day near rest and a warm compress.
Then insert into the extremity of the nose a linen wick if the fracture is in one part; or insert two wicks if the fracture is in both parts: and let the thickness of the wick be such a quantity that it fills the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said to infuse the wicks in butter and change them every day. It does not seem to me, rather it is necessary, that licorice be poured into the albuginea, having been made with flour of mill. Then let the licorice be left until the mouth is strengthened and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is introduced into the Nose in place of licorice, after it is covered with a soft cloth; and it happens that it holds the fracture of the Nose more strongly, and so that the weak one is not prevented from inhaling.
And this thing is not necessary. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, do licorice.
But if it should happen that the Nose breaks again while you are working, apply a plaster to the nose with carrot, or with quince infused in rose oil, or a little from dyakelon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that plasters be made with sesame flour and frankincense powder mixed with albuginea. Then put a soft stopper over it, and do not bind the Nose at all with anything.
But if the bones of the Nose are broken with a small fracture, or are crushed, then it is necessary that you split them open and remove them with instruments that suit them. Then suture the incision and treat it with what heals and consolidates with suitable plasters or ointments.
But if a wound should happen between the Nose, then it is necessary that you treat it with licorice; and administer a tube of lead until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jawbone, when it is broken.
When the lower jawbone is broken and its fracture is not with a wound: then look to see if its fracture is only outward, and it is not broken into two, and curves inward; then the recognition of it is easy.
Therefore it is necessary, if the fracture of it is on the right side, that you insert the index finger of your left hand into the mouth of the infirm person: similarly if the fracture is in the left jawbone, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and press with it the gibbosity of the fracture inwards with ease to the exterior with your other hand from the inner part of the bone, and equalize it with it.
But if the fracture of the jawbone has already been separated into two, then it is necessary that you apply extension from both parts, according to straightness: until its equalization is possible.
But if it happens that there is concussion or disruption in the teeth: then bind those of which you hope that they will remain with gold, silver, or silk thread. Then place a cerecloth over the fractured jawbone. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and put a large, well-made star over the cloth: or a piece of tanned sole leather equal in length to the jawbone.
Then tie it on top, according to how its bandage is prepared for you, and it suits its application until it is not destroyed: and instruct the infirm person that he keep quiet and still: and have his food be gentle sips.
But if you judge that something is already altered in some way: then hurry and loosen it on the third day. Then correct what has been altered from it, and plaster it with powder of millstone grit, with lime or with sesame flour, after you have removed the cerecloth: and put soft wool over the plaster.
While that plaster remains attached to it, and the bone arrangement is not altered, leave it alone, do not loosen it until it heals: or bind the fracture. Very often this fracture is bound for three weeks.
But if a hot abscess should happen in the meantime, then administer what we have often said in its calming, until that abscess is removed.
And if there is a fracture with a wound, consider it. For since a fragment is now separated from the bone, or by a saw; then carefully attend to this, so that you remove those fragments of the saw with what suits you in their removal from instruments.
But if the opening of the wound is narrow, then enlarge it with a phlebotomy according to the amount of your need.
Then when you have removed those saw fragments, and nothing remains of them, suture the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place a plaster suitable for it on top of it, and let it grow together until it heals.
Concerning the Restoration of the Collarbone When It Is Broken.
The Collarbone is indeed very often broken from the front part near the shoulder, and its fracture occurs according to one of three ways.
For it is either broken and separated into two, besides which saw fragments may occur; and this is easier to restore.
Or so that saw fragments occur in the bone; and this is more difficult to restore.
Or so that there is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation on it when the fracture is without a wound is that you have two assistants present, so that one of them holds the support following the broken Collarbone, and the other extends the neck towards the side.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers until its form is made as it ought to be; and there should be neither eminence nor concavity in it.
If it needs more extension, then you must place a sphere of cloth or wool under the collarbone of the weak patient; and its size should be according to the amount of your need; extend and elevate the Collarbone, and compress the sphere with your hand until you equalize the fracture as needed.
If you cannot draw the extremity of the Furcula outward because it has reached a depth, then it is necessary that the infirm person be laid on his back and a medium-sized cushion be placed under his shoulder; and let the attendant press his shoulder downward until the os of the Furcula which is deep is raised upward; and then straighten the fracture and equalize it with your fingers.
But if you perceive a splinter from the Furcula, and it moves, then you must cut above it and extract the splinter easily.
But if it is retained in the bone, then apply ingenuity in incising it with one of the forceps which you have prepared for it, after you have placed under the Furcula an instrument that preserves the sifach; and this instrument is made of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon which has no concavity.
And let its width be according to that for which you need it from the size of the bone and its parity. Its length, however, also as much as is possible for you in the operation.
And let it have two extremities, as you see: and one extremity be wide and the other narrow.
But if the disruption of the wound which you opened when you extracted the bone splinter is wide, and you are cautious of hot abscesses; then join the two lips of the wound with sutures.
And if the disruption is small or you fear hot abscesses; then fill the wound with cloths and lint according to the quantity of the wound's fissure.
But if a hot abscess should occur, then infuse a cloth in rose oil, and vinegar, and wine, and place it upon it.
But when the stricture of the bone is without a wound and without incision it is, as you place upon the bone a plaster made from miller's flour with egg white. And place a soft pledget over it. Then place a sphere under its nipple, if it needs that. Then take a himematem, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long: and let its width be a palm, or thereabouts. Then place a doubled pledget over the pledget and plaster. Then take a splint from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the splint in the place of the alhimemati which falls over the fracture and place. Then place the alhimemati over the fracture as it revolves, and wrap it over its neck, and under its sound nipple, and under its unsound nipple: and draw it over all parts, and how you see that the strapping holds the fracture with decent strapping: and this is, that which is not hidden from you, and the whole, so that the splint does not move off the fractured bone. And for this it is necessary, that you look at the sick person every day. Whenever therefore the ligature is softened, and you see the splint already moving: then straighten that, and tighten the ligature.
Then place the sick person's sleep on its back, and put under its nipple at its sleep a small cushion at night, so that it may be raised with it as assistance from its side. The fracture of the clavicle is therefore raised by raising the shoulder.
Or bind its arm to its neck: and do not loosen the ligature unless it should happen in the place an accident from itching, or from an abscess, up to twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see its face towards it. And bind it tight and let it go until it is restored and coagulates.
And indeed a fracture of the clavicle is bound and strengthened for the most part in twenty-eight days, and sometimes it happens in some people in a lesser time.
On the restoration of a scapula fracture.
The scapula is rarely broken, and in its broad part; and its margins are not broken except from it.
Therefore, when a place is broken from it and broken in its middle, and it is not recognized except by touch; then, according to what the fracture figure is, try its preparation. And reduce it to its natural shape in every way possible for you.
Then place powder of mill with egg white on the site, and a soft pledget, and put a poultice from doubled cloth over it. Then place a broad splint from a thin board according to the quantity of the whole scapula, or a little wider.
But if there is a concavity under the splint in any part of the scapula, then equalize it with a soft pledget until the splint descends according to equality.
Then bind over it with a long bandage, with decent constriction and firmness of the splint so that it is not removed from its place.
And consider the ligature every day. Whenever therefore the bandage softens, bind it, and equalize the splint if it is removed from its place; and let the weak person lie on his healthy side.
And indeed the scapula is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then untie the bandage and you will be safe. For it is from bones whose union is not feared, nor their loosening.
If, however, a fragment proceeds from the bone and pricks under the skin: then split above it and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the clavicle from the calming of a hot abscess, if anything should happen from it.
On the restoration of a breastbone fracture.
The breastbone is sometimes fractured in its middle, and this rarely happens. Its extremities indeed are most often broken and crushed from accidents.
When its middle is fractured, which inclines downwards, and there occurs to it intense pain, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing: and perhaps he vomits blood, and a concavity is shown in the broken bone, and is not hidden from the sense; The restoration of it is that the sick person be laid on his back; and put pulvinar between his spatulas.
Then press his shoulders and join the ribs from both sides or parts with hands; and make it equal with subtlety, according to every way that it is possible and suits, until the figure of the bone returns, according to what is necessary.
Then put plaster and a wad over it, and put a splint from thin wood from willow, or elder, and such like in lightness on top of it, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then make subtlety in binding it over the broken bone so that it does not move; and go with a bandage according to its roundness to the back many times, and constrict it with decent tightness.
Then consider the bandage every hour, and whenever it softens, tighten it; and if its need provokes to loosen it at itching or corruption which occurs in the place or pain or abscess: then hurry and loosen it, and remove the plaster, and rectify what happens with a method of its treatment.
Then apply a plaster if you see that it is fitting for it and add a bandage to it until it heals.
On the Restoration of Ribs, When They Are Broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in thick places which follow the back and from their extremities on the front side. And attrition does not happen to them unless it is because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the senses upon inquiry with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way that you equalize the fracture with your fingers as much as possible until the shape is equalized, according to what is necessary. Then apply plasters and bind the broken bone with splints if it needs it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is sloping inward: then there occurs severe pain and a stabbing sensation, like the stabbing sensation that occurs to one who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the membrane, and he also experiences difficulty breathing and coughing, and vomiting blood frequently; and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have already devised many remedies for it, and among them are those who say that you should give the patient foods which produce inflammation and windiness so that the abdomen becomes inflamed and extended, and the fracture is pushed outward.
We, however, forbid it lest a hot abscess be quickly made if it does not already exist. But if it has already occurred, then it adds to it.
And some of them said: place something windy on top of the spot, and then suck it out with force, and this is similar reasoning. However, it is feared that the windiness will attract superfluities to the spot because of the weakened condition.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the place be covered with wool which has already been soaked in warm oil: and poultices should be placed between the ribs until it is filled so that there will be an equal bandage when you wrap it according to its roundness.
Then let the weak patient be treated for pleurisy with diet and medicine.
If a strong matter constricts the patient, which he cannot bear, and pricks the mouth with piercing membrane, and we fear for the patient: then it is necessary that we cleave over the place and expose the broken rib. Then let us place upon it an instrument which retains the silk, a remembrance of which has preceded; and let us cut open the mouth with ease, and cut it itself.
Then let us join together the two lips of the wound, if it is large with suture; and let us treat it with plasters until it heals.
But if a hot abscess should happen at the same time, then hurry and pour little poultices in rose oil and put them on the spot; and let the patient be treated with that which suits an abscess.
Meanwhile also let him lie supine upon the side upon which he sleeps until it heals.
On the restoration of a fracture of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Indeed a fracture sometimes happens to the bones of the neck, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly to the vertebrae of the back also. Therefore when it happens to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not: then consider. For if you see his hands already softened and stunned and dead; and he cannot move them nor extend them nor constrict them; and when you constrict them with your fingers, and prick them with a sharp point, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that it will not heal, and is mortal in most dispositions.
But if he moves them and feels constriction in them with fingers and pricking: then know that the vertebrae of the neck are safe, and that the patient will be healed with treatment.
If it should happen with a spinal injury like that, and you wish to know whether it will be healed or not: then consider its feet; then if you see that they have become softened, and there occurs in them what occurs in the hands; then when he lies on his back, flatulence and defecation exit without volition; and when he is turned over onto his stomach, defecation exits without volition; and when he is turned over onto his back again, and he wishes to urinate but cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore do not labor in its treatment.
But if none of that happens to him, the matter is lighter.
And the treatment of what happens from that is, to try sedative fomentations of hot poultice: so that you place on the fractured spine rose oil alone: or with yolks well cooked.
Place on it that several times a day until when the hot poultice is sedated: then place on the place one of the comforting and drying plasters, and bind it over it with a bandage: and order the sick person to use quietude and tranquility. And let him not sleep on the side on which he finds pain until he is healed.
But if it should happen at the abrasion of bone that is shattered, or something which already becomes separated from it: then it is necessary that you cut upon the skin and remove that. Then join together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with suture. Then care for it with some flesh-producing substance, from ointments, until it is healed.
But if the last bone of the coccyx is fractured and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary that you insert the thumb of your left hand into the anus and equalize the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible, and by a second equalization.
Then place it on top and remove it, and care for the wound with the care that preceded it until it heals.
On the Restoration of a Wound.
Chapter Eleven.
Rarely are the bones of a wound broken. But if they are broken, then their fracture is not anything but a comminution at their ends; and they split lengthwise and incline towards the inside; and there occurs a feeble pain in the place, and stinging; and the leg which is broken becomes numb.
And its restoration indeed is that you extend your hand over it until you stand over the fracture as its shape is.
But if the fracture is only at its ends, then equalize that fracture according to what is prepared for you from equality until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or has already inclined inwards, then make the weak person lie on their stomach until preparation for the restoration of that fracture is ready for you. Therefore, when you equalize it, place a plaster over it; then place a splint made of wood or leather over it; and bind it tight with a binding which you do not fear will cause the fracture to change position or the splint to be removed.
And equalize the concavity of those with something which fills it until the binding takes it according to equality; and order the weak person to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess should occur to it; then stop from extending it and its restoration until the hot abscess subsides; and place what subsided over it according to what preceded, then return to its restoration; and bind it as is necessary.
But if a bone becomes loose or breaks apart, remove it.
On the Restoration of a Fracture of the Assistant Bone
That is an assistant, which is between the elbow and the head of the spatula.
Therefore, if it is broken, its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form, which follows.
And two ligatures are tied at the two ends.
Then it is suspended from an elevated place, and the patient sits upon a seat. Then he should project his broken arm onto the wood until it reaches its middle, in which the curve of the wood is attached.
Then suspend something heavy from above it so that it extends the whole thing downwards. Then the physician straightens the fracture with his hands at the same time until the fracture returns as it should be.
And the other method is that the patient lies on his back and his hand is suspended from his neck with a ligature. Then instruct two assistants, that one of them should hold that which is above the fracture with his hands and that the other should hold below it; and let each one extend towards their own side.
And if you wish for a stronger extension, then bind below the fracture with a ligature and above it with another ligature; and let each of the two assistants extend them towards their own two sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the shoulder, it is necessary that one ligature be placed below the head and another below the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, it is necessary that a ligature be placed above that place and above the elbow itself.
Then straighten the fracture with ease without exertion until when the fracture is equalized as it should be and a good dressing is applied; then bind it if a hot swelling does not occur.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs, then loosen the bandage until the seventh day. And place upon it wool dipped in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess subsides; then bind it. And the manner of binding it is to place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place an application from new cloth over the plaster. Then let him bind his arm upon the support and place his open hand upon his shoulder.
And place a cloth and bind it upon the support so that the arm remains in the place of the stars; if nothing prevents you from doing so, nor anything changes over the broken bone.
But if you fear that something might change over the broken bone, then use a splint; and it is to place upon the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place the width of a finger between every splint; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
Then bind upon the splints the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the longer the fracture extends, the looser the bandage should be.
Therefore, if you see the position of the splints and bandage, as we have said, in the hour that you restore the limb immediately do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then loosen the bandage and splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the bandage every three days so that itching or swelling does not occur in the place, nor is food prevented from reaching the limb because of the abundance of the bandage. Therefore correct it all according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that nothing will happen from it; then do not loosen the bandage except after three days or more.
Let the sick man lie on his back and let his hands be upon his stomach; and put under him a bolster filled with wool evenly.
And consider every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened, or that the ligament is not softened. Therefore rectify it all with your strength.
And put food for the sick man according to the order which we have prescribed, so that it be subtle food at first until he begins to bind his mouth; then it is necessary that you thicken his food.
For by custom of the helper and leg it is to be bound for forty days; then therefore it is necessary that it be loosened, and a bath be given, and be treated with ointments which rectify it.
But if the fracture is foul and crushed; then do not loosen from it the bandage and splints for up to fifty days, and even up to two months.
Concerning the Restoration of a Fracture of the Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two humeri.
Of which one is small and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is placed under the small one from the lower part.
Therefore perhaps only the larger humerus is broken, or the smaller one; and perhaps they are broken together.
When therefore the smaller humerus above is broken, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower humerus is broken, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse is it when the two bones are broken together.
If therefore the bone which is broken is the smaller humerus above, then it behooves the doctor at its restoration to put a small extension with ease until it equals itself.
If, however, the large bone is fractured: then it is necessary that he should apply a stronger splint.
And it is necessary that he should apply the shape of the hand at its restoration, and its extended extension upon a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers towards the superiors: and let the lower joint be lower than all the fingers, or the rest: and a weak sitting square upon itself, and let the cushion be before it, or in its direction, at a height, so that the weak one does not incur labor.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from the lower part, or with his hand, or with a ligament. And let another attendant extend similarly from above.
Then let the physician equalize the bone, until he reduces it according to the best shape that he can.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are splinters, then endeavor to reduce all fragments into their place by your strength.
But if a separated splinter appears in it and pricks the skin, and you have no hope of its restoration: then cut upon it, and remove it according to the way that we said in those things which are preceding.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter for that, therefore take its treatment from there.
But if a hot abscess should happen at the beginning of your restoration, then make a cloth with cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium between thickness and subtlety: and bind the cloth upon it with a fine bandage, until the abscess subsides: then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared from flour powder with egg white. Then place stars, and let the star which is placed upon the fracture itself be a little wider and stronger.
And know that the number of stars for the arm is six in most arrangements: whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones at the same time.
Then place your bandage over the place of the fracture stronger and more tightly, and somewhat higher with the bandage above the fracture, or below it; or place a softer bandage on the same side, according to the previous recollection mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths which are wrapped over the fracture be soft, damp cloths; and not hard, rough ones. And let the thread with which it is tied be of flax properly, medium between fine and coarse, as we have described.
And consider the limb and the bandage after a few days. And if anything should happen of which correction is necessary, such as itching which occurs in the limb: then it is necessary that you bathe the limb with warm water until the itching subsides, and you release the limb unconstrained until it subsides, then repeat the bandage.
And if the bandage has already become softened, and the bone already moved, and similar things to these: then correct all that by your own skill, and consider again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, because of excess bandage, then it is necessary that you soften it a little, and release it for several days until nourishment flows to it, then tighten it.
But if none of the things which we have said happen to the weak one, then it is not necessary that it be loosened except after twenty days, or thereabouts.
Then let the weak one's hand be suspended to his neck. And let his neck be equal, and let it be guarded by his own strength from agitated movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days; and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All of that is according to the dispositions of complexions, and dispositions of strength.
On the Restoration of Fractures of the Hand
Know that fractures of the ribs of the hand and the knuckles of the fingers rarely occur. And it does not happen to them unless there is much rubbing.
Therefore, when a fracture or rubbing of the sole occurs, it is necessary for the weakling to sit down and for there to be a seat in front of him according to equality. Then place his hand on it extended.
Then let the attendant extend the broken bones, and let the physician equalize them until they are joined together in a good conjunction.
Then it is necessary to place a plaster and wad if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a splint on top according to the quantity of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is near the belly of the sole, then make a sphere-like thing from cloth and order the weakling to press upon it with his fractured sole; then let it be tied with a long cloth.
And let the splint be made from leather, in which there is softness so that the leather may be applied to the body of the sole; and bind it as needed.
But if the fracture is on the outside, it is necessary to place a splint on top and another splint from beneath in the sole; and let the hand be open and straight. Then put a bandage, as the hand is turned over; and extend between the fingers with a ligament.
But if it happens that a fracture occurs to any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, equalize it as needed. Then bind it with the sole; and if you wish, place a small straight splint for it so that the fracture may be raised and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle finger, index finger, or ring finger and little finger, then equalize it and bind it with the healthy finger that follows it; or let them all be bound according to continuity, and this is better. Or place a small plaster on it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the placement of the hot poultice: and oppose it with what is necessary when something occurs from it, according to as its account is repeated.
On the Restoration of a Hip Fracture.
The hip bone is often fractured, and it is revealed to the senses because it turns to the inner and rear part.
And its restoration is done in such a way that a ligature is tightened above the fracture, and another ligature below the fracture. And the patient should lie face down.
Then let the minister extend all the ligatures to their proper side according to equality: and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let the ligature be on the side of the pectineal muscle so that the extension falls upward: and another ligature below the fracture.
And thus similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then let the ligature be near the knee so that the extension is downward.
Then let the physician equalize the bone with both of his hands until he reduces it according to the likeness of the natural shape: and let the bone be joined with a good conjunction.
Then it is necessary that you place a plaster and bandage, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it: then let it alone for several days until the hot abscess subsides, then return to your treatment.
The bandage of it should be thus. It is necessary that you wrap a hard linen cloth, that is, a bandage, over the fracture; and double it twice, or three times: and let there be a surplus from it. Then wrap the leg until the heel reaches the root of the ankle.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee to the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then roll over the leg and thigh what is left over from the splint.
Then place on the thigh in the place of the fracture itself splints, and place one splint from them over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the vacancies between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the tightness is equal.
Then begin with strong binding from the middle above the place of the fracture with three or four windings. And the more you extend with the bandage from the place of the fracture, let your binding be less and softer, and gentler.
Then attend to the two ends of the thread which you inserted between the thigh and the leg. Therefore tie with both three splints which are above. Then go with the two ends of the thread to the lower parts, until you reach the heel of the foot. And with both tighten also the ends of the splints from the other side, so that the bandage is not removed from its place.
