Then let the binding remain on it as long as it stays, so that neither corrosion nor abscess nor swelling nor anything like them happen to the limb. But if something of that kind should happen. Therefore hurry with its resolution and correct everything that happens from it completely, according to what we have said many times.
But if there is a fragment of bone in the bone that pierces: then it is necessary, if it is possible for you, to equalize that. And if not, bind over it and remove it; and care for it with a memory of what preceded, until it heals.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is held together, in addition to which stars are added to it, as we said in the auxiliary and arm.
However, in its restoration thus fractured, infirm lameness does not occur. And if it is restored alone, except that another leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that he who owns it always limps.
And know that the hip is held together for fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is diminished: all of that is according to the diversity of complexions, and other dispositions.
On the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but attrition often happens to it.
But if a fracture does occur to it, then it is nothing but either a fissure or a hole in its parts. And it is that with a wound, and without a wound. And the whole thing stands upon it with sensation.
And its restoration is such that what is disrupted from its parts is equalized with the fingers until it comes together and is joined according to what is possible from equality and ease and fabrication.
Then place a plaster from sesame flour, or millstone, with egg white: and place a round star on top of it if that is needed: and bind a suitable ligature on top of it for that.
Then consider all the dispositions which we have narrated in the other fractures: such as a hot poultice, and things similar to it: so that you oppose every accident with what suits it until it is healed.
On the restoration of a fracture of the leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick and is called by the name Leg: and the other slender and is called Fibula.
And it happens with both kinds of fractures that it happens to two bones of the arm; and for this reason, its restoration is done just like the restoration of an arm equally, and the operation is one.
If two bones are broken at the same time, the leg is turned in all directions.
And if a slender bone is broken, the leg is turned to the front part.
And if a thicker bone is broken, you will find it below; and it is among those that are not hidden from you.
Then apply extension, and equalization, and ligation of the stars according to that equally.
However, it is necessary, if the fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having many splinters: that there be less extension, and gentler alleviation by you.
And in the leg indeed there is something more from the operation than in the arm; and it is that when you equalize the stars, and complete all your operation, then take two bandages from pine wood, which is administered in the making of sun dials that are put under boards; and they are made from palm spatulas; and choose from them those which have a small thickness, and are not from the smallest ones: and let the length of them be according to the length of the leg from the knee downward.
Then wrap upon each cloth with two wrappings according to its length. And put one part of the leg from one side, and the other from the other side; and let them be from the knee downward to the foot.
Then tie the two bandages in three places from the two ends and the middle. For when constriction is forbidden, the leg inclines to the right and left, and stands in a good position.
And now indeed let a crib be administered from wood according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it, so that it preserves it from motion.
And indeed it is very necessary that you do this in a fracture when there is a wound with it properly.
Then consider the leg every day and strive with the greatest care to keep it free from abscess, or swelling, or other things. If, therefore, any of those things should happen, then oppose them with what is necessary until it is healed, if God wills.
Of fractures of the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the heel bone does not often occur.
Fractures sometimes happen to the bones of the foot and rarely to the toes; they do not occur except by attrition in many positions.
And if a fracture should happen to the bones of the foot and you see those bones rise up over one another, then let the patient place his foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand and place your foot on that which is raised from those bones; then press or tread it down and equalize it until it returns to its place.
Then apply a plaster and put a splint over it; and place under the sole of the foot a small board with two flat heads.
Then bind it with a proper bandage after you have wrapped it in cloths and other things as needed.
When three or four days have passed, then untie the bandage. For you will find the bones equalized, whether they were broken or dislocated. The operation on them is therefore as you see.
But if any of the toes are broken, then restore them and equalize them according to what I have told you about the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place with your finger a splint along the length of the toe and let it be a little wider; then place under the foot this board which I have told you about, and bind it with a proper bandage.
If, however, what is broken is two, three, or more bones, then place upon each finger a splint of its size, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the board under the foot; and let it stand on one head of the board outside the belly of the foot, so that it may hold with good retention.
And it behooves you not to forget to consider all that I have said to you in the remaining fractures from the accidents we mentioned; and oppose every accident with what is necessary.
Concerning the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the bone of the breastbone, and the man's penis bone.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, then make her sit squarely. Then incline her somewhat toward her own back, and let her be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with cotton until it is full; and let it be made in the vulva like a sphere from this.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. For the cotton will come out until it is made before the vulva's gate like a sphere, and the broken bone returns.
Then place a cushion upon her back. When, therefore, she wishes to urinate, let her remove the cotton with ease, then urinate; and put it back according to the manner in which you first inserted it; then let her return to her first cushion.
Let her do this for seven days, or thereabouts, for the bone itself is restored.
And if you wish, take a bladder of a sheep, and bind a reed tube over its opening; and insert the whole bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder is inflated within the vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then it should be filled with cotton and left for several days, as we have said, until it heals.
And when the bone of the breastbone of a man or woman is broken, then apply in its restoration and its equalizing that which we have said about the bone of the shoulder blade.
And the rectum should not be concealed in these nearby fractures, which rarely occur, especially for someone who has some study or practice.
And understand this book of mine well, for the fracture itself indicates to you in many positions the straight path in its restoration and its union; therefore understand.
But when the rod of a man is broken, take a goose's neck; and insert the rod into it. Then place an envelopment of cloth over it, and let it be wrapped up, and leave it for three days, or thereabouts, and it will heal, if God wills.
On the restoration of a fracture of bones when there is a wound.
It is fitting for one to whom a fracture occurs with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone or thigh bone or similar to them, that you hasten and let blood from him immediately; if conditions for bloodletting are attested to you, as we have previously stated.
But if the wound is flowing blood, then it is necessary that you hasten to cut away from it so that you sprinkle powdered charcoal over it; if you do not have anything else present.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day and do not delay it unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then defer its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause bad things to happen to him.
If, however, the fractured jawbone is prominent above the skin exposed, then it is necessary that you try to reduce it and equalize it with your hands with ease and little extension.
If, however, its extension is not prepared, and its equalization by your hands: then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument made of iron, whose length is the quantity of seven fingers, or eight: and its width is according to the quantity of the wound. And for this reason it is necessary for the physician to take three or four from them according to the quantity needed for that cure in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round: and let there be a small thickness in it so that it is not doubled upon compression on it in the hour of the operation: and let it have an acute extremity, which has a margin at its extremities. And let it be above its thickness from its middle up to its lower parts.
And this is its shape.
It is necessary in this operation that the acute curved extremity of it be placed on the extremity of the prominent bone and press it with it together until the jaw returns and is equalized in some way.
Therefore try to equalize the extremities of the fracture to each other.
And if the extremities of it are fractured finely and do not receive good acceptance by the instrument, then it is necessary that you cut the extremity of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
But if you are not able to reduce them altogether with what we have described, then cut it with what suits it from the Incisions which we have mentioned or saw it with a saw in whatever way is possible for you.
Then scrape away that which remains in the bone from roughness and fine cortexes.
When therefore the jaw is reduced and finds violent vehement pain after its reduction, then you know that the jaw has not returned to its natural place.
Therefore do that. For you bestow a great benefit on the sick man when you complete the restoration of the bone which you make.
Then immerse the cloth in black wine vinegar. And especially if it is summer. And do not place a bandage on the wound which contains wax, nor anything in which there is oil: so that you do not cause putrefaction and decay to occur in it.
Then administer splints in the hour of your completion of the restoration of the bone. And let the wound be left open; so that you may make an incision with forceps into the folds according to the size of the wound.
And be careful with all caution not to constrict the wound with a fracture. For very often foolish doctors have done this, and they have caused death, or decay, or gangrene, that is, a spreading sore, to occur upon their patients. And let your binding be soft, gentle, different from the binding of other fractures.
But if the wound is bad, or it is a large wound, and you fear some evil accidents which we have described upon it, and you find pain in the place hanging: then it is not necessary that you should put splints upon it. And make for them folds out of hard cloths in place of the splints, and bind it with them.
But when it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound already beginning to generate pus in it: then remove from on top of it the cloth which you placed upon it with wine. Then administer bandages and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds: such as the tetrapharmacum ointment and those similar to it.
And it behooves you to loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every day in the morning and evening until it consolidates and heals.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that the pus flows from it to the lower parts with ease.
If many days pass and the wound does not heal and the pus is not separated from it, then know that there are small bone fragments there. Therefore you must inquire into the wound with your hand. But however many of those bone fragments are separate, remove them and extract them yourself. And those which are not separate and prick the limb and cause pain to occur, try to cut into them and remove them in any way possible for you to do so.
But if it should happen that the wound becomes infected, or gangrenous, that is, estiomenus, or another species of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary that you oppose to each of these what suits it from its treatment, of which a preceding account was given in its chapter.
And from this point on which my speech must stand and to which you should direct your mind when a bone is broken and protrudes above the limb, such as the thighbone and the fibula and those similar to them from large limbs, it is, that you do not presume to pull it back, for very often death results from this, but rather let it be until it putrefies.
For perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then therefore treat the wound if it seems right to you, and if not, leave it alone.
Concerning the treatment of nodules in the scar after a fracture.
Very often this nodulation occurs in the scar after healing of a fracture. Especially that which is near the joints and disfigures the shape of the limb from it. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Therefore consider then if the nodulation is recent, administer to it medicines which have astringency, such as aloes and frankincense and myrrh and sarcocolla and acacia and those similar to them.
So that you may take some of these, or all of them, and mix them with vinegar, or with egg white, or with vinegar; and lay them upon the swelling on a wad, and bind them tightly over it.
And leave the binding, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another, until the swelling is removed.
Or bind a sheet of lead over it firmly. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned to stone, and is tight, and urgent necessity calls for its removal: then make an incision over it from its upper part, and cut away the excess that protrudes: or shave it with certain razors, until it is removed; and tend to it until it heals.
Concerning the healing when it is restored, and the limb remains beneath it subtle, not according to its nature.
When a fracture of a bone is healed, and the limb remains after it subtle, weak; then it does not happen except for many causes. One of which is the multiplicity of loosening of the ligament and its binding not as it should be: or because of excess tightness of the ligaments' bindings, until nourishment is prevented from running to the limb; or because of too much application of embrocations at an unfitting time; or because of scarcity of blood in the body of the weak person, and its debility.
And its cure is to feed the weak person, and make his body tender, until blood is multiplied in it. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then lay pitch over the limb: so that the pitch may draw much nourishment to it. And diligently apply embrocation of it with warm water, until nourishment flows and it returns to its natural form.
On the treatment of broken bones when they are restored crooked.
When it happens that crookedness and eminence of the broken bone, or a knot, occur in a limb already restored after its healing, and the form of the limb is spoiled thereby, yet the limb is not prevented from its natural operation, then you ought not to listen to him who thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And there have already been many foolish physicians and setters who have done this in our land.
And this operation is truly very blameworthy, leading to death, or great fear, so that it may be mortal.
But if the limping and knotting are recent, then it is necessary that it be bathed with warm water, in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as leaves of the mallow, and its root, and melilot, and those like them. And let a plaster be made for it with a softening plaster, such as well-made diachylon.
Or let the mucilage of mallow root be taken and shaken with hen fat, or sesame oil, and plastered with it.
Or let plump figs be taken and rubbed with dove's dung, and similar things which are called from medicines that lessen consolidation.
And sometimes the knot is loosened with persistent, ancient, and gentle friction, which is done with wet hands. And let motion be given to the limb to every part in all hours.
But if the crookedness has already preceded and become strong, and has been converted into stone, and necessity has arisen for its treatment with iron, then it is necessary that it be cut above it, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and what overflows from the knot or bone be cut off with fine incisors; and ease be given to it with care and diligence.
Concerning Speech in Dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any joint from its place. Therefore it is prohibited from its motion and the figure of the limb is deformed. And it causes severe pains and violent injuries to befall the weak one.
Therefore, when dislocation happens to someone, it is necessary to hasten its restoration and not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and perhaps the reduction of the dislocation with it.
Therefore, it should not be moved nor extended during the hour of its inflammation. For it often causes spasms and painful injuries to occur.
But when that happens, you must hurry to the phlebotomy of the weak one. Then let it be dismissed until the inflammation subsides.
Then let the limb be embrocated with warm water and oil. Then let it be reduced with ease.
And let all the limb be treated with it so that the recollection of which will come to its place.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters of dislocation, even according to what preceded in the fracture, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws rarely dislocate unless rarely.
And the dislocation of both occurs according to one of two ways. For either they are moved from their places by a small removal and softened a little. Or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation until they soften to the chest part, until the saliva of the weak one flows and he cannot hold it nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is enveloped with speech.
But when its dislocation is small, then it returns in many positions by itself with little effort.
But if the dislocation is complete, entire: then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly and not delayed at all: and it is for the attendant to hold the head of the weak one; and for the physician to insert the thumb of his own hand into the root of the mandible of the dislocation between his mouth, if the dislocation is from one side. Or he inserts his two thumbs together, if the dislocation is from both sides. And let the remaining fingers of his hands be outside, with which he equalizes.
And instruct the weak one to cleanse his mandibles and open them for eating on every side. And the physician equalizes the mandible and pushes the mandible until it returns to its place.
And if its reduction is difficult, especially if both mandibles are together; then administer steaming with warm water and oil until an easy reduction of both is made: and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When therefore they return and are equalized and the mouth of the weak one is closed and they do not soften; then place poultices of cloths on them with cerate which is made from wax and rose oil.
Then it is bound easily with soft bandages.
And let the sleep of the weak one be on his back; and let his head stand between two pillows so that he does not move it to the right and left; nor does he try or is forced to chew anything: but rather let his food be a gentle sipping until when the pains are removed and the mandible is knotted; then let him eat what he wants and let it be administered easily.
Let him not labor upon the operation of his mouth at eating and drinking and kissing until the mandible is united and healed.
But if the reduction of the two mandibles becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some hour and do not return to their places, then fevers and continuous sweats often happen; and perhaps the belly of the sick person is loosened, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore when you see that, know that it is destruction and he to whom it happens often dies in ten days.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and extremity of the shoulder.
The clavicle indeed does not dislocate from the inner side on account of its continuity with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates to the outer side, and that is declared by sensation.
And its restoration is, for the sick person to lie on his back and extend his arms. Then constrict the place with your palm in a strong constriction, for it will return by itself.
Then put a plaster upon it and poultices, and bind it.
The extremities indeed which follow the elbow and are continuous with it do not dislocate except rarely. But if it dislocates on some day, then it is necessary that it be reduced and equalized according to what we have said and what is prepared for you.
Then put a plaster upon it and poultices and a binding. And advise the sick person to adhere to tranquility and quiet until he heals.
And with this same treatment the extremity of the shoulder is reduced when it also separates from its place.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the shoulder.
Know that the humerus does not dislocate except according to three ways.
One of them is to dislocate it towards the side of the fibula below. The second is to dislocate it towards the chest. And perhaps it may be dislocated to the upper part of the humerus, and that happens rarely.
And it does not dislocate backwards because of the spatula. And it does not dislocate forwards because of the nerves.
Indeed, it most often dislocates and comes out below towards the fibula, especially in those whose flesh is scant. Because it goes out quickly in them and goes back in quickly.
But in those whose flesh is plentiful, it is the opposite of that, namely because it goes out with difficulty and goes back in with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people that a blow or fall occurs and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot inflammation, and it is thought because of that that it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, you ought to test it until you stand on the certainty of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And dislocation is recognized when it is below towards the fibula, and you distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a sound humerus. For you find a manifest difference between them, and you find in the head of the humerus a concavity, and under the fibula at the touch the head of the humerus as if it were an egg itself: and the weak person cannot bring the hand to his ear, nor move it with all motions.
And similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards: then you find a manifest touch and it is not concealed.
And the reduction of this dislocation is indeed easy when it is recent or when the person is a weak child.
And its reduction is that the attendant raises his hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under his clavicle, and elevate the fracture with strength upwards to its place; and let the assistant raise his hand and extend it upwards. Then let him put it down below, for he returns quickly.
But if he does not return through what we have said, and there have been many days from the beginning since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary that the sick man bathe in warm water, and an embrocation be administered which softens and soothes, such as if the roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot are decocted in water and administered.
Then let the sick man be laid on his back; and let a sphere and wool be placed under his clavicle, which is equal between softness and firmness.
Then let the physician place his heel on the sphere, and elevate the head of the humerus with strength, and draw the hand of the sick man downwards.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this method: and it is that you make a man who is taller than the sick man be present, and let him stand on the side, and insert his shoulder under the clavicle of the sick man; and elevate the clavicle upwards until the sick man is suspended in the air. And let another assistant draw the hand of the sick man downwards towards his belly. But if the sick man is light: then it is necessary that he be suspended with something else so that he becomes heavy; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and it is that a long piece of wood be fixed into the ground, whose head is round in shape: such as the handle of a mortar: which is neither thick nor thin. Then let it be placed under the sick man after soft cloths are placed on top of the head of the wood; and let the sick man stand according to the length of the wood.
Then let his hand be extended downwards on one side, and let his body be extended on the other side with force; for the junction returns to its place with speed.
But if its reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this care: and it is, that you take wood whose length is the measure of two arms, and its width the measure of four fingers, and its thickness the measure of two fingers: which has a round head, so that its entry is eased into the depth of the bone, according to this form.
Then soft cloths are tied over the round head so that the wood does not injure the weak flesh.
Then let it be placed under the weak bone so that the hand or arm is extended upon the wood up to the lower parts; and let the wood be tied over the support, the arm, and the end of the hand.
Then let the arm be placed upon the step of a ladder for its width, and let the hand be extended downwards, and let the remaining body be left hanging from the other side. For the junction enters immediately.
Therefore when its entry is completed according to whichever way is possible: then it is necessary that you put under the bone a sphere of equal size from wool. Then place a plaster prepared from miller's powder with frankincense and albumin over the whole shoulder, just as it rolls down from above.
Then bind under the bone with a decent bandage; and let the bandage be rolled over the plaster from above; and attach his hand to his neck, and let it go, and do not move his hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that little food be given to the weak one until the limb is strengthened; for it is quicker for its healing.
Then let a wider bandage be put on after seven days, or five, and he should be raised with motion. If therefore it is hardened and is not softened, then it is already healed.
But if the junction dislocates many times, because of the moisture that happens to it or because of another cause: then it is necessary that a cautery with three holes be administered in it, according to what has been previously stated in the chapter on cautery.
But if all this is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the junction is not confirmed, and the plaster and constriction are returned upon it several times, and it is not made firm, and it falls, and it cannot be raised to the upper parts: then know that its nerves which are at the head of the humerus have already been cut or stretched or softened: and know that the junction will never be fixed in its place.
But reduction of the dislocation which occurs towards the chest and nipple, or to the back, is made with a thrust and extension with the hands: until it returns, and the remaining constriction and care are administered in it until it is healed.
But if it should happen after healing that there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its motion, then let the weak person bathe many times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its former nature.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty also: and indeed it dislocates to all sides and especially to the front or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: because it falls under sight and under touch, according to whichever shape it dislocates. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the sound one, it is declared to you manifestly. For the joint becomes concave and the arm cannot bend nor touch its own shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hurry and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess occurs to him.
For if a hot abscess occurs to him, its reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it does not heal at all, especially when it is towards the back. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocations, and of more violent pain to them; and very often death comes down with it.
And the restoration of it when it is from those which are possible to reduce, is that his hand be extended with two hands: and his arm be spread out, and the hands of the physician be above the elbow and below it, and he presses the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or truly the dislocation is to the front: then it returns thus, that he doubles his hand with his own hand, until he strikes with the base of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
But if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer strong, vehement extension; and it is that two attendants extend the arm: and two other attendants also hold it fast so that it is not moved during the extension.
Then let the arm be revolved to every part after it is wrapped over his hand a doubled long cloth, or a wide bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil: so that it be one of those things which aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then he presses the joint with a vehement thrust, until it returns.
And after its reduction it is necessary that a plaster be placed upon it, in which there is astringency, and drying with white of egg; and let it be bound with a decent bandage. And let the arm be suspended from the neck of the sick person: and dismiss him for several days.
Then let it be loosened. If therefore the junction is secured in its place, then loosen the ligature from it and let it go.
And if you see the junction not well constricted, then repeat the plaster and the ligature and let it go for several days until it is constricted. Then loosen it.
But if it happens to have heaviness after the reduction of the junction and the disposition is in motion: then administer moisture in a bath and delicate friction and compression until it becomes straight or place on the junction a plaster, that is, fattened lamb's caul. Then let it be ligated and leave it upon it day and night. Then remove it and introduce it into a bath. When therefore it sweats, then rub the junction with temperate friction. Then repeat upon it lamb's caul juice once, and a second time, and a third time with the entry into the bath until it is softened.
And if you wish, place upon it cow dung, moist, heated with butter and bind it on top of it.
Do that several times. For it is softened and returns to its own disposition.
On the treatment of dislocation by Albucasis.
Albucasis often dislocates the hand that is, the junction which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. Contrary to the rest of the junctions. However it is necessary that there be swiftness in the reduction of its dislocation within the hour in which it dislocated before an abscess occurs: or heat should occur in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is that you place Albucasis' hand infirm on a table and let the assistant extend his hand and let the doctor put his palm on top of the eminence of the junction and push it until it returns.
However, it is necessary that it be considered whether the palm has already receded to the belly of the hand; then let the infirm place the back of his hand on the table at extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be inwardly on the table, that is, let the inner part of his hand be placed on the table so that the hand of the doctor may fall upon the prominence of the joint.
Therefore if it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, then bind it with a plaster soothing the swelling and send him away, and do not touch it again.
For if he does not tolerate it, and you are unable to effect a reduction of it after days have passed, nevertheless the joint remains according to its crookedness; and nothing harms the infirm except that his hand is softened and he cannot grasp anything; then know that the nerves are cut or rubbed.
Therefore there is no remedy in this except to cauterize it near its joint. And perhaps that does not effect anything.
When therefore Almahassem does not return, then put upon it a plaster which we have described. Then let it be bound and sent away for five days; afterwards let it be loosened and sent away, and let the hand be exercised.
But if its motion is deprived and something weighty happens to it; then soften it with warm water and frequent friction until it is softened.
On the Treatment of Dislocations of the Fingers.
When a finger is dislocated from it, to any part, or from it, to the back of the palm or its belly; then extend the finger and press the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it toward the part to which it is dislocated and send it away for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until its straightness is equal in its own day.
Therefore, when it is night, bind it according to the narration itself; and do not cease to loosen it during the day; and exercise it with motion; and bind it at night.
Do that for several days until it is tightened.
And do the same with it if it dislocates towards the belly of the hand; and bind it to the part itself, and perform your first operation on it until it is healed.
And do the same with it when it dislocates to the other parts.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Dorsal Vertebra.
When it happens to someone that the dorsal vertebra or the vertebrae of the neck are completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure in it, because death hastens the infirm person.
And the sign of it is that the excretion of the infirm person comes forth without volition, and he cannot hold it; and very often some of his limbs are softened from it, or both legs of his, or both arms of his, or one of them; or one vertebra is removed from its place.
Very often it is removed, because very often the removal of them happens in four parts.
That which is therefore removed to the rear is called a Kyphosis.
The treatment of it, however, is that you consider then if the kyphosis happens from childhood; then there is no cure in it, nor any healing at all from it.
But as for that which happens from a fall, or a blow, or similar things like them, the ancients have already said modes of treatment in lengthy speech. Of which most does not return with profit.
I, however, have already shortened it from that which excuses a little from a great deal of it because of my intention's proximity to it; and by my exposition in which I exposed it and formed its instrument in a different way from the one in which they showed it and exposed it.
Therefore I say that in a hunchback which occurs from the front part, there is neither skill nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which happens on both sides also.
And it is not cured from them, except that which occurs in the back properly, with that which I relate: and it is, that the weak one be extended onto his face above a level platform near the wall; and let straw matting be spread underneath him so that his chest is not injured.
Then let a straight piece of wood be placed fixed into a hole in the ground, at the extremity of the platform, around his head; and another piece of wood around his feet at the other extremity of the platform; and let an assistant hold the wood, and let it not be fixed into a hole. And let another assistant hold the other one.
Then let a soft, strong bandage be wrapped over the head of the weak one, and under his armpits; and extend the end of the bandage to the wood which is by his head, and bind it there.
Then let another bandage be tightened above his hips, and above his knees, and at his heels. Then bring together all the bandages and bind them to the other wood which is by his feet.
Then let each assistant extend the bandage to the wood, and let the two pieces of wood not be removed from their places in which they are fixed; however, let them not be fastened, as we have said.
And the doctor shall place his hands upon the spine with strength until it returns; or he shall place them upon a board. Then he attaches his feet to the board until it returns.
But if it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is approximately three cubits. And dig in the wall of which we said that it is near the weak one, a place into which the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board on the Spondilem. And let the physician put his feet on the other extremity. And let him tighten it well, until the Spondilem is constricted and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a lathe which is enclosed with your hand; and it is, that you fix at the ground by the head of the sick man on the last step two pieces of wood, whose length is one arm's length: and by his head on the last step two pieces of wood.
And let the length which is between all wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made in every place, in which the lathe runs; and let all the wood be fastened firmly in the ground so that it does not move at all.
And insert a round piece of wood; and it is a lathe in which the ligature revolves in two holes of two pieces of wood.
And at its extremity there is a hole, in which wood is fastened, whose length is one palm with what revolves; and similarly in the two other pieces of wood.
Then tighten the ligatures which you have tied in the chest of the sick man with the lathe which is by his head; and the ligatures which you have tied in his legs with the lathe which is by his feet.
Then let a servant stand by each lathe who twists his hand with licorice with which the lathe revolves; and the physician adjusts the Spondilem according to what we have said.
This is the form of the lathe, which follows.
Then after the Spondilem returns and the place is equal, it is necessary that you put a drying plaster with egg white. Then a cotton swab. Afterwards place on the plaster a splint from a board, whose width is three fingers or thereabouts. And its length is such a quantity that it takes hold of the place of the Gibbosity and above the part of the Spondilem which is sound.
Then it is tied with the ligature which we have said. And let the weak one use delicate nourishment until he is healed.
But if some eminence should remain in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that medicines be administered which soften and soothe, with the application of a plaster as we have described for a long time. And now a plate of lead is applied to that area.
And sometimes an eminence occurs in the last vertebra of the back: and it is thought to be a dislocation, and that is the bone which already protrudes. Therefore it is not necessary to presume this with this medicine: for it may perchance cause death.
On the treatment of a dislocated jaw.
Know that dislocations of the jaw and shoulder do not occur unless it is a dislocation only: nor does what happens to other joints from slight removal and concavity happen to them.
And indeed the joint of the jaw dislocates according to four parts. Which is because it dislocates forward and backward.
And indeed it most often dislocates inward: and rarely dislocates forward and backward.
And the treatment of its dislocation inward is: because when you join the leg of a healthy person with the unhealthy one it is longer: and the knee of the unhealthy one is more prominent than that of the healthy one, and he cannot bend his foot at the groin: and there is a place following the groin inflamed with a warm poultice, because the head of the femur has already arrived there.
And the sign of him to whom a dislocation outward occurs is that these are accidents contrary to those accidents.
And the sign of him to whom a dislocation forward occurs is because he extends the healthy leg according to complement: however he cannot bend it without pain which is in the knee: and if he attempts to walk, he cannot go beyond that forward: and his urine is retained, and his groin becomes inflamed, and when walking there is pressure on his heel.
The sign of one to whom dislocation has occurred backwards is that his knee does not extend, nor can he bend it forward before bending his groin; and his leg is also shorter than the other; and his groin is softened, and the head of his femur is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of this kind of dislocation is, however, that you consider then if the dislocation is old, which has already grown old with him having it. Then if you administer its reduction and it remains according to its own disposition, then there is no cure at all in it. Therefore, it must not be entirely presumed upon him.
But in him whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four species of dislocation, then hasten to this so that you wish for the juncture, and extend it to the insides and outsides, and move it right and left: perhaps it will return, and not need any other cure.
But if it does not return in this way, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong attendant, and he extends his leg from the lower part: either with his hands or with a ligament which is tied above his leg above the knee: and another attendant extends his hand from above so that he inserts his hands under his malleoli. Then let him tie with a soft ligament above the root of the femur, and hold the end of the other ligament.
And let its extension be either from the front part of the groin to the fork part, or from the back to the dorsal part.
And let the extension of them all be together, until the weak man is raised with his body from the earth, and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common kind of the four kinds.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we said it is good; and if not then it is necessary for every kind that which I shall say about its own proper cure.
In reducing it when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary for the weak side to be laid upon its own sound side. Then let him place a bandage over the root of the thigh, on that which is between the head of the thigh and the place which is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the collar bone.
Then let another attendant take hold of his strong arms and reduce the thick place of the weak thigh, and extend it outward with a vehement extension. Now it will return to its place.
And this kind is easier than the other kinds of treatments by which this limb is reduced.
But if it is excused over you and does not obey you to enter with this kind of treatment altogether, then it is necessary for the two feet of the weak one to be bound together with a strong, soft bandage, over the two heels and over the two knees: and let the length of each be of the quantity of four fingers from its companion, and let the weak leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then let the weak one be suspended over the head of a beam which is in the house, and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arm spans.
Then order a strong young man to brace the head of the thigh and let another young man be suspended with the weak one: and let the other young man urge it forward bracing the thigh with strength, for the joint will return to its place with speed.
But in reducing it when the dislocation is outward, it is necessary for the weak one to lie upon a bench, according as we have narrated in treating a hunchback. And let a bandage be tied over its leg, properly the weak one, and over its chest.
Then let two pieces of wood be placed, one at its feet and the other at its head. Then a piece of wood is placed in the middle of the footstool, fixed well; upon which wet cloths are wrapped so as not to injure the sick man; and let the wood be between his thighs so that he is not drawn downwards during extension.
Then let every attendant extend to his own part, and the doctor equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it yields to reversion, it will be good.
And if not, place the board upon it and press it equally as we said in the humpbacked condition. However, it is necessary that the sick man lies on his sound side.
And when the dislocation is forward, then it is necessary that the leg of the sick man be extended quickly; and this according to the same account is upon the footstool: and let the doctor place the palm of his right hand upon the groin of the sick man. Then he shall constrict him with his other hand, and with that constriction place him extended downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is backward: then it is not necessary that the sick man be extended downwards, and he shall be raised up above the ground; rather he ought to be placed upon something hard as it ought to be done also when his elbow is dislocated outwards according to what we said about his reclining upon two elbows; and it is upon his face, and the ligaments are extended according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board also upon the place to which the joint goes forth, when reduction is completed as it ought to be.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you; and it is that you extend the two legs of the sick man; and when you see them equal, and the sick man draws up his leg, and extends it without excuse: then you shall know that the limb has now returned as it ought to be; then join the two thighs together, and apply a plaster, and bind with an imemati, that is, a bandage, so that the elbow does not move towards any part; and let the sick man remain quiet for three days or four.
Then loosen the ligature and bandage, and compare the leg with the other. Then if you see them equal in condition, then you will know that the dislocation is now firm; therefore dismiss the weak one to walking.
And if you see anything of softening: then return, and bandage that, and bind it according to your first binding; and dismiss it also for three days.
Then loosen it and make it delay in walking on both for several days, until it is well strengthened.
Concerning the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated according to three modes. It is dislocated outward, inward, and downward, that is, backward.
And it is not dislocated forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the weak one to apply his leg to his hip. But if it does not adhere with the hip, then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of its dislocation is that you make the weak one sit straight so that now he extends his leg if he can upon it and adheres your back to his face; and place his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere with your palms, and place them straight between your fingers above his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with force. And another attendant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of restoration is that the leg adheres with the hip easily, not laboriously.
Then bandage it and make the leg adhere with the hip. Then bind it together with a splint for three days or four. Then loosen them.
And let him not walk for several days until he is healed.
But if he refuses reduction to you, then administer strong extension with ligaments, of which mention was made previously in the treatment of a sprain, until it returns.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the heel.
The heel is sometimes removed with slight displacement and sometimes dislocated according to its complement. And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen, protruding to the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its removal is easy reduction, and it is that it be extended with ease with the hands, and equalized until it returns.
But in the treatment of it when it is dislocated according to its complement, it is necessary that you make the sick person sit straight, and let a strong attendant hold him firmly from behind on the middle part of his back.
Then take hold with your right hand of his foot from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot near the ankle.
Then draw the foot toward you with your right hand. Then push with your left hand twice.
Then extend with your left and push the chest of the foot with your right toward the leg without effort.
Do that twice as we have described.
Then push the chest of the foot toward the leg in the third time, and you draw the ankle.
If therefore it returns in one or two times according to this method, and you see the foot equalized, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation upon him for it will return.
But if reduction is prevented from you with what we have described, then make the sick person lie on his back on the ground, and fix a wedge firmly in the earth so that it falls between his thighs; and you now have wrapped a cloth over him so as not to injure the sick person.
Then let the assistant take hold of his thigh; afterwards, let another assistant extend the foot, either with his hands or with a bandage that he ties around the neck of the foot. Then let every assistant extend himself against the extension of his companion; and let a bolster stand between the two thighs of the sick man, holding him so that his body is not drawn downwards during the extension.
Then let the physician equalize the dislocation with his hands, and let another assistant hold the sound leg downwards. For the dislocation returns with speed.
Therefore when the dislocation returns and its restoration is made clear to you: then apply a plaster and wad, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware or are afraid of the nerves which are above the heel behind, so that the bandage is not tight upon them and injures them.
Then dismiss him for two days or three. And if the bandage becomes loosened, tighten it; then untie it on the third or fourth day; and forbid the sick man from walking for forty days. But if he should attempt to walk before that time, there is no safety lest the dislocation occur upon him again and be corrupted, and it will not receive a cure afterwards.
But if a hot abscess should happen to him: then it is necessary that you administer to him in its sedation what our previous narration has stated elsewhere for treatment, and an application, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes, and their cure.
It is necessary that what is dislocated from them be equalized with a little extension without labor; and indeed this is not difficult, but rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in some of the knots on the back of the foot: then it is necessary that the sick man makes his foot sit upon a place equal to the earth, or upon a board; and he stands as though walking.
Then you stand, and put your foot on that which protrudes from those joints. Then press it with your foot with strength, until it returns, and you see it now level, and no prominence appears in its place.
Then place under the belly of his foot a plank which takes the whole foot, which has two heads. Then bind it with a decent binding for three days. Then unbind it, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is bound and secured from displacement.
Concerning the kinds of dislocations with wound or fracture.
When something of that kind happens, and you attempt its cure and restoration, very often death follows; and therefore it is not necessary that one presume upon the cure of such a thing unless he is prudent in the art from long practice, merciful, pious, slow, neither bold nor presumptuous; and that he use in the beginning medicines which allay only hot abscesses: and let him send the sick man to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for salvation from destruction with the mildness of the illness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning, before a hot abscess occurs.
And if therefore the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen which allays the abscess; and care for the wound with that which suits it from drying ointments.
But if the dislocation is with a fracture and separate chips occur in the bone, then attempt their removal and do in it what we said in simple illnesses from that which preceded in their places.
And be careful according to your strength, and guard yourself from entering the way of fear; according to my previous preaching to you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and more saving for your body among them in your time.
